The Mirror Argument Against Mu'tazila on Vision
Ghazali presents a mirror analogy to refute the Mu'tazila's condition of face-to-face for vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses a mirror analogy to expose a flaw in the Mu'tazila's condition for vision, and why their own logical division leads to a contradiction when applied to real experience.

Core Idea
If logical reasoning denies an actual phenomenon, the premise is false.

In this passage, Ghazali presents a clever logical objection (ilzam) against the Mu'tazila who insist that vision requires face-to-face confrontation and direction. By imagining a person unfamiliar with mirrors, Ghazali shows that the Mu'tazila's own exhaustive division of possibilities would deny the possibility of seeing oneself in a mirror—yet it actually occurs. This reveals the narrowness of their reasoning and opens the door to affirming that Allah can be seen without direction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation to grasp the argument's flow.
Pay attention to the three divisions the hypothetical person considers.
2
Identify the three divisions
The passage enumerates three possibilities for seeing oneself in a mirror. List them and note why each is rejected by the Mu'tazilite reasoning.
3
Understand the Mu'tazilite position
The Mu'tazila accept the division as exhaustive and thus conclude that seeing oneself in a mirror is impossible. Recognize that this is consistent with their general rule about vision.
They require muqabala (confrontation) and jiha (direction) for any vision.
4
Spot the falsification
Ghazali points out that the falsity of the division lies only in the assumption that seeing oneself requires facing oneself. The other divisions are correct. This shows the Mu'tazila's error.
The actual occurrence of mirror vision proves the assumption wrong.
5
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this argument undermines the Mu'tazila's denial of seeing Allah on the Day of Judgment, since they argue vision requires direction, but mirror vision occurs without facing oneself.
This is a key point in the debate on ru'ya (vision of Allah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا ينبغي أن يُستحقرَ هذا الإلزام؛ فإنه لا مخرج عنه للمعتزلة، ونحن نعلم بالضرورة: أن الإنسان لو لم يبصر نفسَه قط، ولا عرف المرآة، وقيل له: إنه يمكن أن تبصر نفسك في مرآة .. لحكم بأنه محال، وقال: لا يخلو إما أن أرى نفسي وأنا في المرآة.. وهو محال، أو أرى مثل صورتي في جرم المرأة، أو في جرم وراء المرآة .. وهو محال؛ إذ للمرآة في نفسها صورة، وللأجسام المحيطة بها صورة، ولا تجتمع صورتان في جسم واحد؛ إذ محال أن يكون في جسم واحد صورة إنسان وحديد وحائط، وإن رأيتُ نفسي حيث أنا . . فهو محال؛ إذ لستُ في مقابلة نفسي، فكيف أرى نفسي ولا بدَّ من المقابلة بين الرائي والمرئي؟! وهذا التقسيم صحيح عند المعتزلي، ومعلوم أنَّه باطل، وبطلانه عندنا لقوله: (إني لست في مقابلة نفسي .. فلا أراها)، وإلا .. فسائر أقسام كلامه صحيح؛ وبهذا يستبين ضيقُ حوصلة هؤلاء عن التصديق بما لم يلفوه، ولم تأنس به حواشهم.
It should not be taken lightly this objection; for the Mu'tazila have no way out of it. We know necessarily that if a man had never seen himself, nor known a mirror, and it was said to him: 'You can see yourself in a mirror,' he would judge it impossible and say: 'It is not free from the divisions: either I see myself while I am in the mirror – that is impossible, or I see a likeness of my image in the substance of the mirror, or in a substance behind the mirror – that is impossible; for the mirror in itself has an image, and the surrounding bodies have images, and two images cannot coexist in one body; it is impossible for a single body to have the image of a human, iron, and a wall. And if I see myself where I am, that is impossible, for I am not facing myself; how can I see myself when facing between seer and seen is necessary?' This division is valid according to the Mu'tazilite, and it is known that it is false. Its falsity according to us is because of his saying: 'I am not facing myself, so I do not see it.' Otherwise, the rest of his divisions are correct. And by this the narrowness of the comprehension of these people regarding believing what they have not experienced and what their sides have not become familiar with becomes clear.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage presents a logical argument (ilzam) against the Mu'tazila's claim that vision requires face-to-face confrontation (muqabala) and direction (jiha). Ghazali imagines a person who has never seen himself or a mirror. If told he could see himself in a mirror, the person would falsely reason that it is impossible because he cannot face himself. The Mu'tazila would accept this reasoning as logically exhaustive, but Ghazali points out that in reality such vision does occur, falsifying their premise. The actual falsity lies only in the assumption that self-vision requires facing oneself; the rest of the divisions are correct. This exposes the Mu'tazila's inability to accept realities beyond their limited experience.

Key Terms
إلزام ilzām
مقابلة muqābala
جهة jiha
مرآة mir’āt

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Vision necessarily requires physical confrontation (muqabala) and direction (jiha).
Correction: The mirror argument shows that vision can occur without the seer facing the seen object directly, as in seeing oneself in a mirror. This opens the possibility of seeing Allah without direction.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why does the mirror argument refute the Mu'tazila?
A) Because the mirror creates an image that is seen without direction.
B) Because the Mu'tazili's own division of possibilities leads to a contradiction when applied to real experience.
C) Because the Mu'tazila admit that vision requires a physical eye.
D) Because the person in the argument knows nothing about mirrors.

Key Takeaway
The mirror argument demonstrates that the Mu'tazila's condition of confrontation for vision is not universally valid, as exemplified by seeing oneself in a mirror, thereby supporting the Ash'ari position that Allah can be seen without direction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Second Method: Redefining Ru'ya
Ghazali explains the second method: analyze the meaning of ru'ya and remove impossible attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's second method for defending the possibility of seeing God: analyzing the common meaning of vision, subtracting impossible attributes for God, and determining whether a real or metaphorical sense remains.

Core Idea
Vision of God is not physical; Ghazali extracts a real meaning by removing impossibility.

In this passage, Ghazali presents a sophisticated linguistic and theological method to resolve the dispute about seeing God. Instead of simply asserting the possibility, he engages with the opponent's misunderstanding and offers a way to purify the concept. Let's read his approach carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the overall flow of Ghazali's argument.
Notice the phrase 'وهيهات' which expresses Ghazali's strong rejection of the opponent's assumption.
2
Identify the opponent's error
Ghazali says the opponent denied ru'ya because they misunderstood it as physical perception like seeing bodies and colors. He clarifies that this is not what Ahl al-Sunna mean.
The opponent's error is assuming ru'ya must be physical.
3
Understand Ghazali's procedure
Ghazali proposes to obtain the meaning of ru'ya in an agreed-upon context (human vision), then remove any aspects that are impossible for God. If a core meaning remains, affirm it as real; if not, use the term metaphorically with revelation's permission.
This is a method of 'purification' or 'subtraction'.
4
Consider the two outcomes
Either a real meaning remains (then God is seen in reality) or only metaphorical use is possible (then we still use the term by permission of revelation). In both cases, the meaning is as reason indicates.
Ghazali does not force a real definition; he lets the analysis decide.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكشف المسلك الثاني: وهو البالغ، أن نقول: إنما أنكر الخصم بما ورد به الشرع الرؤية لأنَّه لم يفهم ما نريده بالرؤية، ولم يحصل معناها على التحقيق، وظنَّ أَنَّا نريد بها حالة تساوي الحالة التي يدركها الرائي عند النظر إلى الأجسام والألوان! وهيهات؛ فنحن نعترفُ باستحالة ذلك في حق الله تعالى، ولكن ينبغي أن نحصل معنى هذا اللفظ في الموضع المتَّفق عليه ونسبكه، ثم نحذف منه ما يستحيلُ في حق الله تعالى، فإن بقي من معانيه معنى لم يستحل في حق الله تعالى، وأمكن أن نسمي ذلك المعنى رؤية حقيقة .. أثبتناه في حق الله تعالى، وقضينا بأنه مرئي حقيقةً، وإن لم يمكن إطلاق اسم الرؤية عليه إلا بالمجاز .. أطلقنا اللفظ بإذن الشرع، واعتقدنا المعنى كما دلّ عليه العقل.
The second method, which is the penetrating one: to say: The opponent denied the vision (ru'ya) that came in the revealed law only because he did not understand what we mean by ru'ya, and did not grasp its meaning with certainty, and thought that we mean by it a state equal to the state that the seer perceives when looking at bodies and colors! Far from it! We admit the impossibility of that with respect to God Most High, but we should obtain the meaning of this word in the agreed-upon context and then remove from it what is impossible with respect to God Most High. If a meaning remains that is not impossible with respect to God Most High, and it is possible to call that meaning vision (ru'ya) in reality, we affirm it with respect to God Most High and judge that He is seen in reality. And if it is not possible to apply the term vision except metaphorically, we apply the word by permission of the revealed law and believe the meaning as reason indicates.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage introduces the second method (al-kashf al-maslak al-thani) for defending the possibility of seeing God. Ghazali diagnoses the source of the opponent's denial: they misunderstand what 'ru'ya' (vision) means. They assume that when Ahl al-Sunna say 'we will see God', they mean a physical, bodily vision like seeing physical objects. But Ghazali clarifies: we admit that such a vision is impossible for God. Instead, we analyze the word 'ru'ya' in a context where its meaning is agreed upon (presumably human vision) and then systematically remove any aspects that are impossible for God (such as need for an eye, direction, distance). If a core meaning remains that is not impossible for God, we call that 'real vision' (ru'ya haqiqa) and affirm that God is seen in that sense. If the term can only be applied metaphorically, we still use it with the permission of revelation and believe the meaning as reason dictates.

Key Terms
رؤية ru’ya
حقيقة Haqiqa
مجاز majāz
استحالة istiḥāla

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ahl al-Sunna claim God is seen in a physical, bodily manner like material objects.
Correction: Ghazali explicitly admits that physical vision is impossible for God. The 'vision' they affirm is a purified concept, stripped of material limitations.

Checkpoint
What is the 'penetrating method' that Ghazali proposes for dealing with ru'ya?
A) To accept the literal wording of revelation without any analysis.
B) To analyze the agreed-upon meaning of ru'ya, remove impossible aspects for God, and see if a real meaning remains.
C) To reject the term ru'ya altogether because it implies physicality.
D) To reinterpret ru'ya as metaphorical from the start without analysis.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's method shows that affirming vision of God does not require physicality; we can extract a meaningful core from the concept of vision that is not impossible for God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Ghazali's Analysis of Ru'ya: Locus and Relation Not Essential
Ghazali argues that the eye and specific objects are not essential to the meaning of vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Ghazali distinguishes the essential meaning of vision (the state of perception) from its physical locus and specific objects, and that neither the eye nor the particular visible quality is essential to the term ru'ya.

Core Idea
Seeing does not require an eye – the essence of vision is a state of direct apprehension.

In this passage, Ghazali performs a careful conceptual analysis of the word ru'ya (vision). He breaks it down into three components: its true meaning, its locus (the eye), and its relation (the specific object seen). Through a series of thought experiments, he argues that neither the eye nor the specific object are essential pillars of the concept. This prepares the ground for the theological claim that God can be seen without a physical eye or a finite object.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let Ghazali's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Ghazali uses a very structured, analytical style.
2
Identify the three components
Ghazali breaks down ru'ya into: the true meaning (haqiqa), the locus (mahall, the eye), and the relation (muta'allaq, the object seen). Find these in the text.
3
Understand the argument about the locus
Ghazali argues that the eye is not essential. He imagines perceiving the same state through the heart or forehead – we would still call it 'seeing'. The eye is just an instrument.
This is a thought experiment to abstract the essence.
4
Understand the argument about the relation
Ghazali argues that the specific object (e.g., blackness, color, motion) is not essential either. If vision were tied to one quality, it couldn't apply to others. Vision is a state independent of its object.
This shows that ru'ya does not depend on a particular kind of visible thing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتحصيله: أن الرؤية تدلُّ على معنى له محل، وهو العين، وله متعلق، وهو اللون والقدر والجسم وسائر المرئيات. فلننظر: إلى حقيقة معناه، وإلى محله، وإلى متعلقه، ولنتأمل: أن الركن من جملتها في إطلاق هذا الاسم ما هو؟ فنقول: أما المحل .. فليس بركن في صحة هذه التسمية؛ فإنَّ الحالة التي ندركها بالعين من المرئي لو أدركناها بالقلب أو الجبهة مثلاً .. لكنا تقول: قد رأينا الشيء وأبصرناه، وصدَقَ كلامنا؛ فإنَّ العينَ محل وآلة لا تراد لعينها، بل لتحلَّ فيه هذه الحالةُ، فحيث حلت الحالة .. تمت تحقيقة، وصح الاسم. ولنا أن نقول: علمنا بقلبنا أو بدماغنا إن أدركنا الشيء بالقلب أو لدماغ . . فكذلك إن أبصرناه بالقلب أو بالجبهة أو بالعين. وأما المتعلق بعينه فليس ركناً في إطلاق هذا الاسم وثبوت هذه تحقيقة؛ فإن الرؤية لو كانت رؤيةً لتعلُّقها بالسواد.. لما كان المتعلق بياض- رؤيةً، ولو كانت لتعلقها باللون .. لما كان المتعلق بالحركة رؤيةً، ولو كان لتعلقها بالعرض .. لما كان المتعلق بالجسم رؤية .. فدل أن
And its elaboration: That vision (ru'ya) indicates a meaning that has a locus (maḥall), which is the eye, and a relation (muta‘allaq), which is color, quantity, body, and the rest of visible things. So let us consider: its true meaning, its locus, and its relation, and reflect: what is the essential pillar for applying this term? We say: As for the locus, it is not an essential pillar for the correctness of this naming; for the state that we perceive by the eye from the visible object, if we perceived it by the heart or the forehead, for example, we would still say: 'We have seen the thing and perceived it,' and our speech would be true. For the eye is a locus and an instrument, not desired for itself, but for this state to arise in it. Wherever the state arises, its reality is complete and the name is valid. And we may say: we know by our heart or our brain if we perceive the thing by the heart or the brain, similarly if we see it by the heart, or the forehead, or the eye. As for the specific relation (muta‘allaq), it is not an essential pillar for applying this name and establishing its reality; for if vision were vision due to its relation to blackness, then what is related to whiteness would not be vision; and if it were due to its relation to color, then what is related to motion would not be vision; and if it were due to its relation to an accident, then what is related to a body would not be vision. So it indicates that
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed analysis of the concept of ru'ya (vision). Ghazali breaks down the concept into three elements: the true meaning (haqiqa), the locus (mahall, i.e., the eye), and the relation or object (muta'allaq, i.e., what the vision is of, such as color, quantity, body). He then argues that neither the locus nor the specific relation is essential (rukn) for the term to apply correctly. The locus (eye) is merely an instrument; if by chance the same state of perception were achieved by another organ (like the heart or forehead), we would still call it 'seeing'. Similarly, the specific object (e.g., blackness) is not essential; the state of vision itself is independent of what it is related to. Therefore, the core meaning of ru'ya is the state of direct apprehension, which can be abstracted from physical instruments and particular objects. This supports the possibility that God can be seen without an eye or directedness.

Key Terms
محل maḥall
متعلق muta‘allaq
ركن rukn
آلة āla

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the term ru'ya necessarily implies a physical eye and specific sensible qualities in the object seen.
Correction: Ghazali shows that neither the eye nor any particular visible quality is essential; the essence is the state of perception.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali's analysis, which element is NOT essential to the definition of ru'ya?
A) The state of perception itself.
B) The eye (mahall).
C) The relation to a particular quality like color.
D) Both B and C are not essential.

Key Takeaway
The core meaning of ru'ya is a state of direct apprehension, independent of any particular physical organ or object.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you contemplate seeing Allah in the afterlife, are you unconsciously limiting that vision to physical eyes? How does Ghazali's argument free you from that limitation, and what does that imply about the nature of your soul?
?
In your daily experience, do you equate 'seeing' with the biological process of eyes receiving light? If the essence of vision is direct apprehension, what might you be 'seeing' with your heart that your eyes miss?
?
If the eye and the object are not the essence of vision, then what must you purify within yourself to truly 'see' the reality of faith? Are you willing to let go of the sensory crutches and trust the heart's vision?

Method for Applying Human Terms to God
Ghazali introduces his method for applying 'vision' to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's method for determining whether a human term like 'vision' can be applied to God.

Core Idea
We extract the core meaning of terms, remove impossibility, and apply what remains.

Before debating whether God can be seen, we must agree on what 'vision' means. Ghazali proposes a careful method: take the agreed meaning, strip away what is impossible for God, and see if a core meaning remains. This lesson introduces that method.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening argument
Read the first few lines where Ghazali states his method. Notice that he admits the impossibility of literal vision for God but seeks a refined meaning.
He is not denying that God can be seen; he is setting up the rational investigation.
2
Identify the three-step process
Ghazali outlines three steps: obtain the meaning in the agreed context, compose it, then remove what is impossible for God. Find these steps in the text.
3
Understand the two outcomes
After deletion, if a meaning remains that is not impossible, we affirm it as real and call God 'seen'. If only metaphorical, we use the term with legal permission but believe the rational meaning.
This shows Ghazali's balance between reason and revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهيهات؛ فنحن نعترف باستحالة ذلك في حق الله تعالى، ولكن ينبغي أن نحصل معنى هذا اللفظ في الموضع المتفق عليه ونسبكه، ثم نحذف منه ما يستحيل في حق الله تعالى، فإن بقي من معانيه معنى لم يستحل في حق الله تعالى، وأمكن أن نسمي ذلك المعنى رؤية حقيقة أثبتناه في حق الله تعالى، وقضينا بأنه مرئي حقيقة، وإن لم يمكن إطلاق اسم الرؤية عليه إلا بالمجاز أطلقنا اللفظ بإذن الشرع، واعتقدنا المعنى كما دل عليه العقل.

وتحصيله: أن الرؤية تدل على معنى له محل، وهو العين، وله تفصيل القول في متعلق، وهو اللون والقدر والجسم وسائر المرئيات. فلننظر: إلى حقيقة معناه، وإلى محله، وإلى متعلقه، ولنتأمل: أن الرؤية الشرعية؟ الركن من جملتها في إطلاق هذا الاسم ما هو فنقول: أما المحل فليس بركن في صحة هذه التسمية؛ فإن الحالة [نفي إرادة المحل؟] نتي ندركها بالعين من المرئي لو أدركناها بالقلب أو الجبهة مثلاً لكنا نقول: قد رأينا الشيء وأبصرناه، وصدق كلامنا؛ فإن العين محل وآلة لا تراد لعينها، بل لتحل فيها هذه الحالة، فحيث حلت الحالة تمت حقيقته، وصح الاسم.

ولنا أن نقول: علمنا بقلبنا أو بدماغنا إن أدركنا الشيء بالقلب أو الدماغ.. فكذلك إن أبصرناه بالقلب أو بالجبهة أو بالعين.

وأما المتعلق بعينه فليس ركناً في إطلاق هذا الاسم وثبوت هذه [نفي إرادة المتعلق؟] حقيقته؟ خصوص صفات المتعلق ليس ركناً لوجود هذه الحقيقة وإطلاق هذا الاسم، بل الركن فيه من حيث إنه صفة متعلقة أن يكون لها متعلق موجود، أي موجود كان، وأي ذات كانت؛ فإذن: الركن الذي الاسم مطلق عليه هو الأمر الثالث، وهو حقيقة المعنى، من غير التفات إلى محله، ومتعلقه.

فما هو المعنى المراد؟ فلنبحث عن الحقيقة: ما هي؟ ولا حقيقة لها إلا أنها نوع إدراك، هو مزيد كشف بالإضافة إلى المتخيل؛ فإنا نرى الصديق مثلاً ثم نغمض العين، فتكون صورة الصديق حاضرة في دماغنا على سبيل التخيل والتصور، ولكنا لو فتحنا البصر أدركنا تفرقة، ولا ترجع تلك التفرقة إلى إدراك صورة أخرى مخالفة لما كانت في الخيال، بل الصورة المبصرة مطابقة للمتخيلة من غير فرق، فليس بينهما افتراق، إلا أن هذه الحالة الثانية كالاستكمال لحالة التخيل وكالكشف لها، فتحدث فينا صورة الصديق عند فتح البصر حدوثاً أوضح وأتم وأكمل، والصورة الحادثة في البصر بعينها تطابق الصورة الحادثة في الخيال؛ فإذن: التخيل نوع إدراك على رتبة، ووراءه رتبة أخرى هي أتم منه في الوضوح والكشف، بل هي كالتكميل له، فنسمي هذا الاستكمال بالإضافة إلى الخيال: رؤية وإبصاراً.

فكذا من الأشياء ما نعلمه ولا نتخيله، وهو ذات الله تعالى وصفاته وكل ما لا صورة له؛ أي: لا لون له ولا قدر؛ مثل القدرة، والعلم، والعشق، والإبصار، والخيال؛ فإن هذه أمور نعلمها ولا نتخيلها، والعلم بها نوع إدراك، فلننظر: هل يحيل العقل أن يكون لهذا الإدراك مزيد استكمال نسبته إليه نسبة الإبصار إلى التخيل؟
How far from it! We admit the impossibility of that with respect to God Almighty, but we must obtain the meaning of this term in the agreed-upon context and compose it, then delete from it what is impossible with respect to God Almighty. If there remains from its meanings a meaning that is not impossible with respect to God Almighty, and it is possible to call that meaning vision in reality, we affirm it with respect to God Almighty and judge that He is seen in reality. And if it is not possible to apply the name of vision to it except metaphorically, we apply the term with the permission of the Law and believe the meaning as reason indicates.

And the elaboration of it: Vision indicates a meaning that has a locus (maḥall), which is the eye, and it has a detailed discussion about its object (mutaʿallaq), which is color, quantity, body, and the rest of visible things. So let us look: at the reality of its meaning, at its locus, and at its object, and let us reflect: What is the essential element (rukn) in applying this name? We say: As for the locus, it is not an essential element in the validity of this denomination; for the state [أنَّ في إرادة المحل؟] we perceive by the eye from the visible object—if we perceived it by the heart or forehead, for example, we would say: 'We have seen the thing and perceived it,' and our speech would be true. For the eye is a locus and an instrument not intended for its own sake, but so that this state may occur in it. Wherever the state occurs, its reality is complete and the name is valid.

And we can say: We know by our heart or our brain if we perceive the thing by the heart or brain... Likewise if we see it by the heart or forehead or eye.

As for the specific object, it is not an essential element in applying this name and establishing its reality? The particular attributes of the object are not essential for the existence of this reality and applying this name. Rather, the essential element in it, insofar as it is a relational attribute, is that it have an existing object—any existent whatever and any essence whatever. So then the essential element to which the name is applied is the third thing, which is the reality of the meaning, without regard to its locus and its object.

So what is the intended meaning? Let us investigate the reality: what is it? It has no reality except that it is a type of perception (idrāk), which is an increase of disclosure (kashf) beyond imagination (mutakhayyal); for we see a friend for example, then we close the eye, and the image of the friend is present in our brain by way of imagination and conceptualization. But if we open the sight, we perceive a difference, and that difference does not revert to perceiving another image different from what was in the imagination; rather, the visual image corresponds to the imagined one without difference. There is no distinction between them except that this second state is like a completion of the state of imagination and like a disclosure of it. So the image of the friend occurs in us upon opening the sight as a clearer, more complete, and more perfect occurrence, and the image occurring in the sight exactly matches the image occurring in the imagination. So then imagination is a type of perception at one level, and behind it is another level that is more complete in clarity and disclosure, indeed it is like a completion of it. We call this completion, in relation to imagination, 'vision' and 'sight.'

Similarly, there are things that we know but do not imagine, such as the essence of God Almighty and His attributes and everything that has no form—i.e., no color and no quantity—like power, knowledge, love, sight, and imagination. For these are matters we know but do not imagine, and knowledge of them is a type of perception. So let us consider: does reason deem impossible that this perception has a further completion whose relation to it is like the relation of sight to imagination?
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage is a careful analysis of the concept of vision (ru'ya) aimed at establishing that seeing God (ru'yat Allah) is possible and rational, contrary to those who deny it because God is not a body. Ghazali begins by stating the method: we must take the agreed-upon meaning of vision from our experience, remove what is impossible for God, and see if a core meaning remains that can be applied to God. He then analyzes vision into three aspects: its locus (mahall) – the eye; its object (muta'allaq) – color, quantity, etc.; and its essential meaning (haqiqa). He argues that neither the eye nor the specific properties of the object are essential to the definition of vision. The eye is merely an instrument; if the same perceptual state occurred via the heart or forehead, we would still call it vision. Likewise, the object need not be colored or shaped; any existent object suffices. Thus the essential element is the third thing: the reality of vision as a type of perception (idrak) characterized by a special clarity beyond mere imagination. To illustrate, Ghazali contrasts imagining a friend with actually seeing him; both produce the same image, but seeing adds a degree of disclosure and completeness. He calls this extra clarity 'vision'. Finally, he applies this to God: we have knowledge of God and His attributes, but this knowledge is a type of perception. Since vision is simply a more complete form of perception, it is rational to suppose that this knowledge could be intensified to the level of vision, just as imagination can be intensified to sight. Ghazali leaves the question open, implying that reason does not forbid it, and the shari'a confirms it. The argument is a defense of the Ash'ari position that God will be seen in the afterlife without physical eyes or spatial location.

Key Terms

المجاز
الحقيقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if a term cannot be applied literally to God, it must be rejected entirely.
Correction: Ghazali shows we can use the term metaphorically or after refining its meaning, as long as reason and revelation permit.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the first step in applying the term 'vision' to God?
A) Prove that God has eyes
B) Obtain the meaning of vision in the agreed-upon context
C) Consult the Law first
D) Deny any possibility of seeing God

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's method allows human terms to be applied to God after removing impossible elements, preserving both reason and revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the attribute 'God is seeing' in the Quran, do you instinctively imagine human-like eyes and light? How willing are you to delete that image and still affirm the attribute as real?
?
What is one human concept about God that you have clung to even though it contains impossibility? Will you apply Ghazali's method to it today, even if it makes you uncomfortable?
?
If Ghazali asked you 'Are you truly ready to let go of your own imagination of God in order to know Him as He is?', what would your honest answer be—and what one step will you take this week to do so?

Vision Does Not Require an Eye
Ghazali argues the eye is not essential to vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that the physical eye is not essential to the definition of vision.

Core Idea
The eye is just a tool; the real essence is the perceptual state.

Many assume vision necessarily involves the eye. Ghazali challenges this by imagining perceiving the same state through other organs. He argues that if the same state occurs, we would still call it vision. This lesson examines his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the analysis of components
Ghazali breaks vision into three aspects: locus (mahall), object (muta'allaq), and meaning (haqiqa). Focus first on the locus.
The locus is the eye in ordinary experience.
2
Follow the thought experiment
Imagine perceiving the same visual state through the heart or forehead. Ghazali says we would still say 'I have seen it.' Understand why he says this.
The state is what matters, not the organ.
3
Grasp the conclusion
Ghazali concludes that the eye is not an essential element (rukn) for the name 'vision' to apply. The reality is complete wherever the state occurs.

We admit the impossibility of that with respect to God Almighty, but we must obtain the meaning of this term in the agreed-upon context and compose it, then delete from it what is impossible with respect to God Almighty. If there remains from its meanings a meaning that is not impossible with respect to God Almighty, and it is possible to call that meaning vision in reality, we affirm it with respect to God Almighty and judge that He is seen in reality. And if it is not possible to apply the name of vision to it except metaphorically, we apply the term with the permission of the Law and believe the meaning as reason indicates.

And the elaboration of it: Vision indicates a meaning that has a locus (maḥall), which is the eye, and it has a detailed discussion about its object (mutaʿallaq), which is color, quantity, body, and the rest of visible things. So let us look: at the reality of its meaning, at its locus, and at its object, and let us reflect: What is the essential element (rukn) in applying this name? We say: As for the locus, it is not an essential element in the validity of this denomination; for the state [أنَّ في إرادة المحل؟] we perceive by the eye from the visible object—if we perceived it by the heart or forehead, for example, we would say: 'We have seen the thing and perceived it,' and our speech would be true. And we can say: We know by our heart or our brain if we perceive the thing by the heart or brain... Likewise if we see it by the heart or forehead or eye.

As for the specific object, it is not an essential element in applying this name and establishing its reality? The particular attributes of the object are not essential for the existence of this reality and applying this name. Rather, the essential element in it, insofar as it is a relational attribute, is that it have an existing object—any existent whatever and any essence whatever. So then the essential element to which the name is applied is the third thing, which is the reality of the meaning, without regard to its locus and its object.

So what is the intended meaning? Let us investigate the reality: what is it? It has no reality except that it is a type of perception (idrāk), which is an increase of disclosure (kashf) beyond imagination (mutakhayyal); for we see a friend for example, then we close the eye, and the image of the friend is present in our brain by way of imagination and conceptualization. So let us consider: does reason deem impossible that this perception has a further completion whose relation to it is like the relation of sight to imagination?

Commentary
This passage is a careful analysis of the concept of vision (ru'ya) aimed at establishing that seeing God (ru'yat Allah) is possible and rational, contrary to those who deny it because God is not a body. Ghazali begins by stating the method: we must take the agreed-upon meaning of vision from our experience, remove what is impossible for God, and see if a core meaning remains that can be applied to God. He then analyzes vision into three aspects: its locus (mahall) – the eye; its object (muta'allaq) – color, quantity, etc.; and its essential meaning (haqiqa). He argues that neither the eye nor the specific properties of the object are essential to the definition of vision. The eye is merely an instrument; if the same perceptual state occurred via the heart or forehead, we would still call it vision. Likewise, the object need not be colored or shaped; any existent object suffices. Thus the essential element is the third thing: the reality of vision as a type of perception (idrak) characterized by a special clarity beyond mere imagination. To illustrate, Ghazali contrasts imagining a friend with actually seeing him; both produce the same image, but seeing adds a degree of disclosure and completeness. He calls this extra clarity 'vision'. Finally, he applies this to God: we have knowledge of God and His attributes, but this knowledge is a type of perception. Since vision is simply a more complete form of perception, it is rational to suppose that this knowledge could be intensified to the level of vision, just as imagination can be intensified to sight. Ghazali leaves the question open, implying that reason does not forbid it, and the shari'a confirms it. The argument is a defense of the Ash'ari position that God will be seen in the afterlife without physical eyes or spatial location.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the eye is not essential for vision, what does that say about your reliance on physical senses to perceive spiritual realities? Are you limiting yourself to only what you see with your eyes, ignoring the insight God gives to the heart?
?
The same perceptual state can occur through different means. When you imagine seeing Allah on the Day of Resurrection, do you focus on the physical eye or on the state of your heart? Are you preparing your soul for that encounter, or are you distracted by worldly sight?
?
Ghazali argues that the reality of vision is the state, not the tool. What 'tools' in your life have you mistaken for the essence? Is there a spiritual action you've neglected because you thought the 'right instrument' (e.g., a specific place or method) was necessary, while the true reality is the inner state of sincerity? Will you repent and seek the essence?

Divine Attributes

6 min
Vision Does Not Require Color or Shape
Ghazali argues the object's attributes are not essential to vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that the specific attributes of the visible object (color, shape) are not essential to vision.

Core Idea
Vision can be of any existent, not just colored bodies.

After discarding the eye as essential, Ghazali turns to the object of vision. Some argue that we can only see things with color and shape. Ghazali counters that the object's specific qualities are irrelevant; any existing thing can be an object of vision. This lesson explores that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the object
Find where Ghazali discusses the muta'allaq (object). He says its specific attributes are not essential.
The object is typically color, quantity, body, etc.
2
Understand the essential condition
Ghazali says the only condition is that the object exists. Any existent, regardless of its attributes, can be the object of vision.
This is a logical move: vision is a relation to an object, but the relation doesn't depend on the object's properties.
3
Connect to the conclusion
Thus, the essential element of vision is neither the eye nor the object's qualities, but the third thing: the meaning itself.
This sets up the next lesson on the true nature of vision.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهيهات؛ فنحن نعترف باستحالة ذلك في حق الله تعالى، ولكن ينبغي أن نحصل معنى هذا اللفظ في الموضع المتفق عليه ونسبكه، ثم نحذف منه ما يستحيل في حق الله تعالى، فإن بقي من معانيه معنى لم يستحل في حق الله تعالى، وأمكن أن نسمي ذلك المعنى رؤية حقيقة أثبتناه في حق الله تعالى، وقضينا بأنه مرئي حقيقة، وإن لم يمكن إطلاق اسم الرؤية عليه إلا بالمجاز أطلقنا اللفظ بإذن الشرع، واعتقدنا المعنى كما دل عليه العقل.

وتحصيله: أن الرؤية تدل على معنى له محل، وهو العين، وله تفصيل القول في متعلق، وهو اللون والقدر والجسم وسائر المرئيات. فلننظر: إلى حقيقة معناه، وإلى محله، وإلى متعلقه، ولنتأمل: أن الرؤية الشرعية؟ الركن من جملتها في إطلاق هذا الاسم ما هو فنقول: أما المحل فليس بركن في صحة هذه التسمية؛ فإن الحالة [نفي إرادة المحل؟] نتي ندركها بالعين من المرئي لو أدركناها بالقلب أو الجبهة مثلاً لكنا نقول: قد رأينا الشيء وأبصرناه، وصدق كلامنا؛ فإن العين محل وآلة لا تراد لعينها، بل لتحل فيها هذه الحالة، فحيث حلت الحالة تمت حقيقته، وصح الاسم.

ولنا أن نقول: علمنا بقلبنا أو بدماغنا إن أدركنا الشيء بالقلب أو الدماغ.. فكذلك إن أبصرناه بالقلب أو بالجبهة أو بالعين.

وأما المتعلق بعينه فليس ركناً في إطلاق هذا الاسم وثبوت هذه [نفي إرادة المتعلق؟] حقيقته؟ خصوص صفات المتعلق ليس ركناً لوجود هذه الحقيقة وإطلاق هذا الاسم، بل الركن فيه من حيث إنه صفة متعلقة أن يكون لها متعلق موجود، أي موجود كان، وأي ذات كانت؛ فإذن: الركن الذي الاسم مطلق عليه هو الأمر الثالث، وهو حقيقة المعنى، من غير التفات إلى محله، ومتعلقه.

فما هو المعنى المراد؟ فلنبحث عن الحقيقة: ما هي؟ ولا حقيقة لها إلا أنها نوع إدراك، هو مزيد كشف بالإضافة إلى المتخيل؛ فإنا نرى الصديق مثلاً ثم نغمض العين، فتكون صورة الصديق حاضرة في دماغنا على سبيل التخيل والتصور، ولكنا لو فتحنا البصر أدركنا تفرقة، ولا ترجع تلك التفرقة إلى إدراك صورة أخرى مخالفة لما كانت في الخيال، بل الصورة المبصرة مطابقة للمتخيلة من غير فرق، فليس بينهما افتراق، إلا أن هذه الحالة الثانية كالاستكمال لحالة التخيل وكالكشف لها، فتحدث فينا صورة الصديق عند فتح البصر حدوثاً أوضح وأتم وأكمل، والصورة الحادثة في البصر بعينها تطابق الصورة الحادثة في الخيال؛ فإذن: التخيل نوع إدراك على رتبة، ووراءه رتبة أخرى هي أتم منه في الوضوح والكشف، بل هي كالتكميل له، فنسمي هذا الاستكمال بالإضافة إلى الخيال: رؤية وإبصاراً.

فكذا من الأشياء ما نعلمه ولا نتخيله، وهو ذات الله تعالى وصفاته وكل ما لا صورة له؛ أي: لا لون له ولا قدر؛ مثل القدرة، والعلم، والعشق، والإبصار، والخيال؛ فإن هذه أمور نعلمها ولا نتخيلها، والعلم بها نوع إدراك، فلننظر: هل يحيل العقل أن يكون لهذا الإدراك مزيد استكمال نسبته إليه نسبة الإبصار إلى التخيل؟
How far from it! We admit the impossibility of that with respect to God Almighty, but we must obtain the meaning of this term in the agreed-upon context and compose it, then delete from it what is impossible with respect to God Almighty. If there remains from its meanings a meaning that is not impossible with respect to God Almighty, and it is possible to call that meaning vision in reality, we affirm it with respect to God Almighty and judge that He is seen in reality. And if it is not possible to apply the name of vision to it except metaphorically, we apply the term with the permission of the Law and believe the meaning as reason indicates.

And the elaboration of it: Vision indicates a meaning that has a locus (maḥall), which is the eye, and it has a detailed discussion about its object (mutaʿallaq), which is color, quantity, body, and the rest of visible things. So let us look: at the reality of its meaning, at its locus, and at its object, and let us reflect: What is the essential element (rukn) in applying this name? We say: As for the locus, it is not an essential element in the validity of this denomination; for the state [أنَّ في إرادة المحل؟] we perceive by the eye from the visible object—if we perceived it by the heart or forehead, for example, we would say: 'We have seen the thing and perceived it,' and our speech would be true. For the eye is a locus and an instrument not intended for its own sake, but so that this state may occur in it. Wherever the state occurs, its reality is complete and the name is valid.

And we can say: We know by our heart or our brain if we perceive the thing by the heart or brain... Likewise if we see it by the heart or forehead or eye.

As for the specific object, it is not an essential element in applying this name and establishing its reality? The particular attributes of the object are not essential for the existence of this reality and applying this name. Rather, the essential element in it, insofar as it is a relational attribute, is that it have an existing object—any existent whatever and any essence whatever. So then the essential element to which the name is applied is the third thing, which is the reality of the meaning, without regard to its locus and its object.

So what is the intended meaning? Let us investigate the reality: what is it? It has no reality except that it is a type of perception (idrāk), which is an increase of disclosure (kashf) beyond imagination (mutakhayyal); for we see a friend for example, then we close the eye, and the image of the friend is present in our brain by way of imagination and conceptualization. But if we open the sight, we perceive a difference, and that difference does not revert to perceiving another image different from what was in the imagination; rather, the visual image corresponds to the imagined one without difference. There is no distinction between them except that this second state is like a completion of the state of imagination and like a disclosure of it. So the image of the friend occurs in us upon opening the sight as a clearer, more complete, and more perfect occurrence, and the image occurring in the sight exactly matches the image occurring in the imagination. So then imagination is a type of perception at one level, and behind it is another level that is more complete in clarity and disclosure, indeed it is like a completion of it. We call this completion, in relation to imagination, 'vision' and 'sight.'

Similarly, there are things that we know but do not imagine, such as the essence of God Almighty and His attributes and everything that has no form—i.e., no color and no quantity—like power, knowledge, love, sight, and imagination. For these are matters we know but do not imagine, and knowledge of them is a type of perception. So let us consider: does reason deem impossible that this perception has a further completion whose relation to it is like the relation of sight to imagination?
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage is a careful analysis of the concept of vision (ru'ya) aimed at establishing that seeing God (ru'yat Allah) is possible and rational, contrary to those who deny it because God is not a body. Ghazali begins by stating the method: we must take the agreed-upon meaning of vision from our experience, remove what is impossible for God, and see if a core meaning remains that can be applied to God. He then analyzes vision into three aspects: its locus (mahall) – the eye; its object (muta'allaq) – color, quantity, etc.; and its essential meaning (haqiqa). He argues that neither the eye nor the specific properties of the object are essential to the definition of vision. The eye is merely an instrument; if the same perceptual state occurred via the heart or forehead, we would still call it vision. Likewise, the object need not be colored or shaped; any existent object suffices. Thus the essential element is the third thing: the reality of vision as a type of perception (idrak) characterized by a special clarity beyond mere imagination. To illustrate, Ghazali contrasts imagining a friend with actually seeing him; both produce the same image, but seeing adds a degree of disclosure and completeness. He calls this extra clarity 'vision'. Finally, he applies this to God: we have knowledge of God and His attributes, but this knowledge is a type of perception. Since vision is simply a more complete form of perception, it is rational to suppose that this knowledge could be intensified to the level of vision, just as imagination can be intensified to sight. Ghazali leaves the question open, implying that reason does not forbid it, and the shari'a confirms it. The argument is a defense of the Ash'ari position that God will be seen in the afterlife without physical eyes or spatial location.

Key Terms
المتعلق
اللون
القدر
الجسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that to see something, it must have color and shape (i.e., be a physical body).
Correction: Ghazali shows that the only requirement is existence; any object, even a non-physical one, can be seen in principle.

Checkpoint
What does Ghazali say is the only requirement for the object of vision?
A) It must have color
B) It must have shape
C) It must exist
D) It must be a body

Key Takeaway
Vision does not require the object to have color or shape; any existent can be its object.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you have always imagined God as having some form or color, how does Ghazali's argument challenge that assumption? Are you willing to let go of a mental image of God to worship Him as He truly is?
?
This lesson says that vision does not require physical attributes. How does this change your understanding of 'seeing' God in the afterlife? Do you yearn for that vision, or are you attached to a sensory experience?
?
Ghazali abstracts the essence of vision. In your own life, are there spiritual realities you have reduced to physical or worldly terms? What would it take to see them in their pure essence?

The Essence of Vision: Perception as Disclosure
Ghazali defines vision as perception with increased disclosure beyond imagination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's definition of vision as a type of perception that is a more complete disclosure than imagination.

Core Idea
Vision is perception plus extra clarity, not a different kind of perception.

Having stripped away the eye and object details, Ghazali now defines what vision actually is. He presents a memorable example: imagining a friend versus seeing him. Both produce the same image, but seeing adds a degree of completeness and clarity. This lesson explores that definition and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of vision
Ghazali says vision is a type of perception (idrak) that is an increase of disclosure (kashf) beyond imagination (mutakhayyal). Identify this definition.
2
Work through the friend example
Imagine a friend, then close your eyes; you have an image. Open your eyes, and you see the same image but with greater clarity and completeness. This extra clarity is what vision adds.
Notice that the content is the same; the difference is in the mode of perception.
3
Understand the term 'completion' (istikmal)
Ghazali calls this extra clarity a 'completion' of imagination. Vision completes what imagination offers.
This is key: vision is not a different kind of perception but a more perfect stage of the same perceptual process.

According to Ghazali, what is the relationship between vision and imagination?
A) They are completely different and unrelated

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you content with merely imagining God's attributes from secondhand descriptions, or are you actively seeking a clearer, more direct awareness of His presence? What one concrete action can you take today to move from vague imagination to deeper disclosure?
?

?

Vision of God: A Rational Possibility
Ghazali applies the definition to argue for the possibility of seeing God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali applies his definition of vision to argue that seeing God is rationally possible.

Core Idea
Our knowledge of God is a perception; if it can be intensified to disclosure, that is vision.

Having defined vision as a higher stage of perceptual clarity, Ghazali now applies this to God. We already have knowledge of God and His attributes, but this knowledge is a type of perception. Could it be intensified to the level of vision? Ghazali poses this as a question for reason, leaving the door open for revelation to confirm it. This lesson examines the final step.

Guided Path
1
Read the final passage
Ghazali mentions things we know but do not imagine (like God's essence, power, knowledge). He asks whether reason deems impossible that this knowledge could have a further completion analogous to sight completing imagination.
2
Analyze the analogy
Just as imagination is completed by vision, knowledge of God could be completed by a higher perception—which would be vision of God.
This is an argument from analogy, not a proof.
3
Consider the conclusion
Ghazali does not assert that reason proves it, but says 'let us consider'—implying reason does not forbid it. The actual affirmation comes from revelation.
This is typical Ash'ari method: reason shows possibility, revelation confirms actuality.

Source Text

وهيهات؛ فنحن نعترف باستحالة ذلك في حق الله تعالى، ولكن ينبغي أن نحصل معنى هذا اللفظ في الموضع المتفق عليه ونسبكه، ثم نحذف منه ما يستحيل في حق الله تعالى، فإن بقي من معانيه معنى لم يستحل في حق الله تعالى، وأمكن أن نسمي ذلك المعنى رؤية حقيقة أثبتناه في حق الله تعالى، وقضينا بأنه مرئي حقيقة، وإن لم يمكن إطلاق اسم الرؤية عليه إلا بالمجاز أطلقنا اللفظ بإذن الشرع، واعتقدنا المعنى كما دل عليه العقل.

وتحصيله: أن الرؤية تدل على معنى له محل، وهو العين، وله تفصيل القول في متعلق، وهو اللون والقدر والجسم وسائر المرئيات. فلننظر: إلى حقيقة معناه، وإلى محله، وإلى متعلقه، ولنتأمل: أن الرؤية الشرعية؟ الركن من جملتها في إطلاق هذا الاسم ما هو فنقول: أما المحل فليس بركن في صحة هذه التسمية؛ فإن الحالة [نفي إرادة المحل؟] نتي ندركها بالعين من المرئي لو أدركناها بالقلب أو الجبهة مثلاً لكنا نقول: قد رأينا الشيء وأبصرناه، وصدق كلامنا؛ فإن العين محل وآلة لا تراد لعينها، بل لتحل فيها هذه الحالة، فحيث حلت الحالة تمت حقيقته، وصح الاسم.

ولنا أن نقول: علمنا بقلبنا أو بدماغنا إن أدركنا الشيء بالقلب أو الدماغ.. فكذلك إن أبصرناه بالقلب أو بالجبهة أو بالعين.

وأما المتعلق بعينه فليس ركناً في إطلاق هذا الاسم وثبوت هذه [نفي إرادة المتعلق؟] حقيقته؟ خصوص صفات المتعلق ليس ركناً لوجود هذه الحقيقة وإطلاق هذا الاسم، بل الركن فيه من حيث إنه صفة متعلقة أن يكون لها متعلق موجود، أي موجود كان، وأي ذات كانت؛ فإذن: الركن الذي الاسم مطلق عليه هو الأمر الثالث، وهو حقيقة المعنى، من غير التفات إلى محله، ومتعلقه.

فما هو المعنى المراد؟ فلنبحث عن الحقيقة: ما هي؟ ولا حقيقة لها إلا أنها نوع إدراك، هو مزيد كشف بالإضافة إلى المتخيل؛ فإنا نرى الصديق مثلاً ثم نغمض العين، فتكون صورة الصديق حاضرة في دماغنا على سبيل التخيل والتصور، ولكنا لو فتحنا البصر أدركنا تفرقة، ولا ترجع تلك التفرقة إلى إدراك صورة أخرى مخالفة لما كانت في الخيال، بل الصورة المبصرة مطابقة للمتخيلة من غير فرق، فليس بينهما افتراق، إلا أن هذه الحالة الثانية كالاستكمال لحالة التخيل وكالكشف لها، فتحدث فينا صورة الصديق عند فتح البصر حدوثاً أوضح وأتم وأكمل، والصورة الحادثة في البصر بعينها تطابق الصورة الحادثة في الخيال؛ فإذن: التخيل نوع إدراك على رتبة، ووراءه رتبة أخرى هي أتم منه في الوضوح والكشف، بل هي كالتكميل له، فنسمي هذا الاستكمال بالإضافة إلى الخيال: رؤية وإبصاراً.

فكذا من الأشياء ما نعلمه ولا نتخيله، وهو ذات الله تعالى وصفاته وكل ما لا صورة له؛ أي: لا لون له ولا قدر؛ مثل القدرة، والعلم، والعشق، والإبصار، والخيال؛ فإن هذه أمور نعلمها ولا نتخيلها، والعلم بها نوع إدراك، فلننظر: هل يحيل العقل أن يكون لهذا الإدراك مزيد استكمال نسبته إليه نسبة الإبصار إلى التخيل؟
How far from it! We admit the impossibility of that with respect to God Almighty, but we must obtain the meaning of this term in the agreed-upon context and compose it, then delete from it what is impossible with respect to God Almighty. If there remains from its meanings a meaning that is not impossible with respect to God Almighty, and it is possible to call that meaning vision in reality, we affirm it with respect to God Almighty and judge that He is seen in reality. And if it is not possible to apply the name of vision to it except metaphorically, we apply the term with the permission of the Law and believe the meaning as reason indicates.

And the elaboration of it: Vision indicates a meaning that has a locus (maḥall), which is the eye, and it has a detailed discussion about its object (mutaʿallaq), which is color, quantity, body, and the rest of visible things. So let us look: at the reality of its meaning, at its locus, and at its object, and let us reflect: What is the essential element (rukn) in applying this name? We say: As for the locus, it is not an essential element in the validity of this denomination; for the state [أنَّ في إرادة المحل؟] we perceive by the eye from the visible object—if we perceived it by the heart or forehead, for example, we would say: 'We have seen the thing and perceived it,' and our speech would be true. For the eye is a locus and an instrument not intended for its own sake, but so that this state may occur in it. Wherever the state occurs, its reality is complete and the name is valid.

And we can say: We know by our heart or our brain if we perceive the thing by the heart or brain... Likewise if we see it by the heart or forehead or eye.

As for the specific object, it is not an essential element in applying this name and establishing its reality? The particular attributes of the object are not essential for the existence of this reality and applying this name. Rather, the essential element in it, insofar as it is a relational attribute, is that it have an existing object—any existent whatever and any essence whatever. So then the essential element to which the name is applied is the third thing, which is the reality of the meaning, without regard to its locus and its object.

So what is the intended meaning? Let us investigate the reality: what is it? It has no reality except that it is a type of perception (idrāk), which is an increase of disclosure (kashf) beyond imagination (mutakhayyal); for we see a friend for example, then we close the eye, and the image of the friend is present in our brain by way of imagination and conceptualization. But if we open the sight, we perceive a difference, and that difference does not revert to perceiving another image different from what was in the imagination; rather, the visual image corresponds to the imagined one without difference. There is no distinction between them except that this second state is like a completion of the state of imagination and like a disclosure of it. So the image of the friend occurs in us upon opening the sight as a clearer, more complete, and more perfect occurrence, and the image occurring in the sight exactly matches the image occurring in the imagination. So then imagination is a type of perception at one level, and behind it is another level that is more complete in clarity and disclosure, indeed it is like a completion of it. We call this completion, in relation to imagination, 'vision' and 'sight.'

Similarly, there are things that we know but do not imagine, such as the essence of God Almighty and His attributes and everything that has no form—i.e., no color and no quantity—like power, knowledge, love, sight, and imagination. For these are matters we know but do not imagine, and knowledge of them is a type of perception. So let us consider: does reason deem impossible that this perception has a further completion whose relation to it is like the relation of sight to imagination?
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage is a careful analysis of the concept of vision (ru'ya) aimed at establishing that seeing God (ru'yat Allah) is possible and rational, contrary to those who deny it because God is not a body. Ghazali begins by stating the method: we must take the agreed-upon meaning of vision from our experience, remove what is impossible for God, and see if a core meaning remains that can be applied to God. He then analyzes vision into three aspects: its locus (mahall) – the eye; its object (muta'allaq) – color, quantity, etc.; and its essential meaning (haqiqa). He argues that neither the eye nor the specific properties of the object are essential to the definition of vision. The eye is merely an instrument; if the same perceptual state occurred via the heart or forehead, we would still call it vision. Likewise, the object need not be colored or shaped; any existent object suffices. Thus the essential element is the third thing: the reality of vision as a type of perception (idrak) characterized by a special clarity beyond mere imagination. To illustrate, Ghazali contrasts imagining a friend with actually seeing him; both produce the same image, but seeing adds a degree of disclosure and completeness. He calls this extra clarity 'vision'. Finally, he applies this to God: we have knowledge of God and His attributes, but this knowledge is a type of perception. Since vision is simply a more complete form of perception, it is rational to suppose that this knowledge could be intensified to the level of vision, just as imagination can be intensified to sight. Ghazali leaves the question open, implying that reason does not forbid it, and the shari'a confirms it. The argument is a defense of the Ash'ari position that God will be seen in the afterlife without physical eyes or spatial location.

Key Terms
ذات الله
العلم
الاستكمال

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that since God is not a physical object, He cannot be seen at all, even in principle.
Correction: Ghazali shows that vision is not limited to physical objects; it is a completion of perception, and since we have knowledge of God, a more complete perception (vision) is rationally possible.

B) Whether it can be completed to a higher perception like sight
C) Whether it is the same as imagination
D) Whether it comes from revelation

Key Takeaway
Ghazali opens the rational possibility that our knowledge of God could be intensified to a vision-like perception, making the doctrine of seeing God rationally defensible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to know God, but is your knowledge merely intellectual—like knowing a fact—or does it penetrate your heart? If that knowledge could be intensified to seeing Him, how would that change how you live today? What are you doing to prepare for that vision?
?
Ghazali says we know God without imagining Him. When you think of God, do you unconsciously picture a human-like form or location? How can you train your mind to truly think of Him as beyond imagination while still feeling His presence?
?
If reason does not forbid the possibility of seeing God, what does that say about your relationship with Him now? Are you seeking that deeper perception, or are you content with surface-level knowledge? What practical step can you take this week to move from knowing about God to knowing Him intimately?

Ghazali's Definition of Vision and Its Possibility for God
Ghazali defines vision as enhanced perception and argues for its possibility regarding God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's definition of vision as a completion of imagination and how he uses this to argue for the rational possibility of seeing God in the afterlife.

Core Idea
Vision is a clearer, more complete perception of what we already imagine or know.

Ghazali begins by asking what vision truly is. He argues it is not a different kind of perception, but a more perfect form of the same perception we have through imagination. He then extends this idea to our knowledge of God, suggesting that in the afterlife we may attain a vision that is a completion of our current knowledge. This lesson walks through his reasoning and shows why he considers the beatific vision rationally possible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the English translation. Let Ghazali's own argument unfold naturally.
Notice the careful step-by-step reasoning from common experience to theology.
2
Analyze the analogy of seeing a friend
Ghazali uses the example of seeing a friend with eyes closed then opened. Identify how he distinguishes imagination from vision: vision is a completion (istikmāl) and disclosure (kashf) of the same image, not a different image.
This establishes the foundation: vision is a superior degree of perception.
3
Trace the extension to knowledge of God
Ghazali applies the same logic to knowledge. Things like God's essence and abstract attributes are known but not imagined. He asks whether a completion of that knowledge is possible, just as vision completes imagination.
Pay attention to his conclusion: reason does not deem it impossible; rather, nature demands it.
4
Consider the obstacles and purification
Ghazali explains why this vision is not possible in this world: the soul's turbidity and occupations veil it. He compares it to physical obstacles blocking sight. Purification after death removes the veil.
This prepares for the afterlife interpretation of vision.
5
Link to the overall argument
Summarize the logical chain: definition by analogy → extension to knowledge → rational possibility → need for purification → beatific vision in the afterlife.
This shows how Ghazali defends the theological doctrine of ru'yat Allah.

Its reality? The specific attributes of the object are not a pillar for the existence of this reality and the application of this name, but rather the pillar in it is that, insofar as it is a relational attribute, that it has an existing object, whatever existing thing it may be, and whatever essence it may be. So then: the pillar to which the name is applied is the third matter, which is the reality of the meaning, without regard to its locus and its object. The perfection of the intended meaning is what is meant. So let us investigate the reality: what is it? It has no reality except that it is a type of perception, plus an increase of disclosure in addition to the imagined. For we see a friend, for example, then we close the eye, so the image of the friend is present in our brain by way of imagination and conceptualization, but if we open the sight, we perceive a distinction, and that distinction is not due to perceiving a different image contrary to what was in the imagination, rather the perceived image matches the imagined without any difference. There is no separation between them except that this second state is like a completion of the state of imagination and like a disclosure for it. So the image of the friend occurs in us upon opening the sight with a clearer, more complete, and more perfect occurrence, and the image occurring in the sight itself matches the image occurring in the imagination. So then: imagination is a type of perception at a rank, and behind it is another rank which is more perfect in clarity and disclosure, rather it is like a completion for it. So we name this completion in relation to imagination: vision (ru'ya) and sight (ibṣār). Similarly, there are things we know but do not imagine, which are the essence of God Almighty and His attributes, and everything that has no form; that is, no color and no magnitude; like power (qudra), knowledge ('ilm), love ('ishq), sight (ibṣār), and imagination (takhayyul). For these are matters we know but do not imagine, and knowledge of them is a type of perception. So let us consider: does reason deem it impossible that this perception should have a further completion whose relation to it is like the relation of sight to imagination? If that were possible, we would name that disclosure and completion in relation to knowledge: vision (ru'ya), just as we named it in relation to imagination: vision. And it is known that positing this completion in clarification and uncovering is not impossible in known existents that are not imagined, like knowledge, power, and others, and likewise in the essence of God Almighty and His attributes. Rather, we almost perceive by necessity of nature that it demands seeking further clarification in the essence of God Almighty and His attributes, and in the essences of all these known meanings. So we say: that is not impossible, for there is no impossibility-maker for it. Rather, reason is an evidence for its possibility, indeed for nature's summoning it. Except that this perfection in disclosure is not granted in this world, and the soul is occupied with the body and the turbidity of its attributes, so it is veiled from it thereby. And just as it is not unlikely that the eyelid, or the veil, or a blackness in the eye should be a cause, by the course of habit, for preventing sight of imagined things, so it is not unlikely that the turbidity of the soul and the accumulation of veils of occupations, by the course of habit, should be an obstacle to the vision (iṣār) of known things. So when what is in graves is scattered, and what is in breasts is obtained, and hearts are purified by the pure drink, and are refined by various kinds of refinement and cleansing, it is not impossible that they should become prepared thereby for increased completion and clarification in the essence of God Almighty. For in all other known things, the elevation of its degree above ordinary knowledge is like the elevation of the degree of sight above imagination. So that is expressed as meeting God Almighty, or beholding Him, or seeing Him (ru'ya), or
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage is a rational proof for the theological possibility of seeing God (ru'yat Allah) in the afterlife. Ghazali begins by defining the nature of vision (ru'ya) as a type of perception that is a completion or increased disclosure relative to imagination. He illustrates this with the example of seeing a friend: when we imagine a friend with closed eyes, we have an image, but when we open our eyes, we perceive the same image with greater clarity and completeness. Thus vision is not a different image but a superior degree of the same perception. He then extends this concept to knowledge. There are things we know but cannot imagine, such as God's essence and attributes, and abstract concepts like power and knowledge. Knowledge of these is also a type of perception. Ghazali asks: does reason deem impossible that there could be a further completion of this knowledge, analogous to the completion of imagination by sight? He argues that it is not impossible—there is no contradiction. In fact, our innate nature (tab') demands a greater clarity regarding God's essence and attributes. However, this perfection is not attainable in this world due to the soul's involvement with the body and its turbidity. Just as physical obstacles like eyelids or darkness can block sight by the ordinary course of nature, so the turbidity of the soul and occupations prevent this higher perception. But after death and resurrection, when the soul is purified, it becomes prepared for that increased clarity. This elevated perception is what is meant by 'meeting God', 'beholding Him', or 'seeing Him' in the afterlife. The argument uses an analogy from ordinary experience and reason to affirm the possibility of beatific vision.

استكمال (istikmāl)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'seeing God' must mean physical vision with the eyes, and since God is not a physical object, it is impossible.
Correction: Ghazali redefines vision as an intellectual perception—a completion of knowledge, not sensory sight. Thus, the object need not be physical; the vision is a higher clarity of understanding.

A) They are two completely different types of perception.

D) They are identical in clarity.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali argues that vision of God is rationally possible as a completion of our knowledge, analogous to how vision completes imagination, and is attainable in the afterlife after purification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that you know God, but do you truly long for a clearer, more complete perception of Him the way you long to see a loved one's face? What does your longing—or lack of it—reveal about the state of your heart?
?
Ghazali says that the soul's turbidity and occupations veil us from this vision. What specific occupations or impurities in your own life are you relying on to keep you comfortable, while they actually block you from seeing God more clearly?
?
If you truly believed that after death, if purified, you could behold God with a perception far beyond your current knowledge, would it change how you spend your time today? What would you start or stop doing to prepare for that vision?

The Rational Possibility of Seeing Allah (Ru'ya)
Ghazali argues that ru'ya is rationally possible as a state created in the eye.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that the vision of Allah (ru'ya) is rationally possible because it is a state created in the eye, analogous to knowledge created in the heart, and affirmed by revelation.

Core Idea
Seeing Allah is rationally possible as a created state in the eye, not as physical sight.

In this passage, Imam al-Ghazali addresses a key point of contention between the Ash'aris and their opponents: is it rationally possible to see Allah in the afterlife? He clarifies that 'vision' can refer to a state created in the eye, which is no more impossible than knowledge created in the heart. This subtle distinction removes the rational objection and leaves only linguistic quibbling or obstinacy as reasons for denial.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage slowly. Then read the literal translation. Notice how Ghazali begins by defining terms and then builds his argument.
Pay attention to the phrase 'خلقت هذه الحالة في العين' (this state is created in the eye).
2
Identify Ghazali's key move
Ghazali defines ru'ya as a state created in the eye, not as a physical sight of a body. This shifts the debate from whether Allah is a body to whether such a state is possible.
He argues that creation of a state in the eye is not impossible, just as creation of knowledge in the heart is not impossible.
3
Understand the role of revelation
Ghazali states that reason does not deem it impossible; rather, since revelation affirms it, reason necessitates it. This shows the harmony of reason and revelation.
Any remaining dispute is due to obstinacy, linguistic quibbling, or inability to grasp the subtle point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرؤية إبصاره، أو ما شئت من العبارات ؛ فلا مُشاحَّةَ فيها بعد إيضاح المعاني وإذا كان ذلك ممكناً : فإن خُلقت هذه الحالة في العين .. كان اسم الرؤية بحكم وضع اللغة عليه أصدق ، وخلقه في العين غير مستحيل ، كما أن خلقه في القلب غيرُ مستحيل ، فإذا فهم المراد بما أطلقه أهل الحقِّ من الرؤية .. عُلم أنّ العقل لا يحيله ، بل يوجبه ، فإنَّ الشرع قد شهد له ؛ فلا يبقى للمنازعة وجة إلا على سبيل العناد ، أو المشاحَّةِ في إطلاق عبارة الرؤية ، أو القصور عن دركِ هذه المعاني الدقيقة التي ذكرناها ، ولنقتصر في هذا الموجز على هذا القدر
Vision: seeing Him, or whatever expression you wish; there is no quibbling about it after clarifying the meanings. And if that is possible: then if this state is created in the eye, the name 'vision' by virtue of linguistic convention is more truthful, and its creation in the eye is not impossible, just as its creation in the heart is not impossible. So when the intended meaning of what the people of truth have said about vision is understood, it is known that reason does not deem it impossible, rather it necessitates it, for the Law has testified to it; so there remains no ground for dispute except by way of obstinacy, or quibbling over the application of the term 'vision', or inability to grasp these subtle meanings that we have mentioned. And we will confine ourselves in this summary to this extent.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage defines and defends the rational possibility of seeing Allah (ru'ya). Ghazali begins by stating that the term 'vision' can be used broadly, as long as the meaning is clear. He then argues that if Allah creates a specific state in the eye, that state is what is called 'vision' in language, and such creation is not impossible. He draws an analogy: just as creating knowledge in the heart is not impossible, creating vision in the eye is not impossible. Therefore, reason does not deem it impossible; rather, since revelation affirms it, reason must acknowledge it as possible. Any remaining dispute stems from obstinacy, linguistic quibbling, or inability to grasp the subtle point.

Key Terms
الرؤية (ru'ya)
الحالة (state)
خلق (creation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that vision of Allah implies Allah is a body or located in a direction.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies that ru'ya is a state created in the eye, not a physical sight of an object in space, so it does not imply corporeality.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the basis for the rational possibility of seeing Allah?
A) Because Allah is a body and can be seen
B) Because creating a state in the eye is not impossible, and revelation affirms it
C) Because reason alone proves it is necessary
D) Because the Mu'tazila have already accepted it

Key Takeaway
Ghazali shows that seeing Allah is rationally possible because it is a created state, just as knowledge is created in the heart, and revelation confirms it.

?
If seeing Allah is a created state in the eye, just as knowledge is a created state in the heart, then what does that say about your relationship with the unseen? Are you living as if the unseen is more real than the seen? How would your daily choices change if you truly anticipated that vision?

Scriptural Evidence for the Occurrence of Vision
Ghazali presents the second aspect: revelation confirms the occurrence of vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scriptural evidence for the occurrence of seeing Allah, particularly the consensus of the early Muslims and the abundance of proofs from the Law.

Core Idea
Scriptural evidence for vision is not a few texts – it is affirmed by consensus and countless indications.

Ghazali now turns from rational possibility to scriptural confirmation. He argues that the occurrence of seeing Allah (ru'ya) is not merely possible but actually promised and expected, as shown by the practice of the early Muslims and the explicit words of the Prophet. This passage presents the second aspect of the discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage with attention to the claim of consensus
Notice that Ghazali does not list all proofs; he highlights the consensus (ijma') of the early generations as a key indicator.
2
Identify the two elements Ghazali uses
The supplications of the early Muslims and the indications from the Prophet's words and actions serve as evidence.
3
Reflect on why consensus as a proof matters
Consensus from the early community carries weight in Islamic theology, showing that the belief in vision was widespread and accepted.
This strengthens the case for the occurrence of vision.
4
Consider the phrase 'the proofs are beyond enumeration'
What does it imply about the certainty of vision? Ghazali suggests that the evidence is so abundant that it leaves no doubt.

The second aspect: concerning its occurrence according to the Law. The Law confirms its occurrence, and the Law has indicated its occurrence, and its proofs are many. Their anticipation and request prove they believed it was permissible. Furthermore, the Prophet's words and actions provide countless indications. The overall consensus of the community shows that the proofs are not limited to a few but are abundant.
