The Universality of Divine Power: All Possibles Are Its Objects
Ghazali proves that God's power encompasses all possible things (mumkinat) without limit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's proof that God's single power encompasses all possible things without limit.

Core Idea
One power, infinite possibles: possibility itself is the link.

This passage is the logical heart of Ghazali's argument for the universality of divine power. He begins by stating that God's power relates to all possible things, which are infinite. Then he presents a clever disjunctive proof: either each possible requires its own power (leading to an impossible infinite regress), or one power covers all possibles through their common feature—possibility itself. The conclusion is that every possible is an object of divine power.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic text once, then the translation. Notice how Ghazali moves from stating the ruling to proving it.
The proof uses a disjunctive syllogism (either...or...).
2
Understand the infinite nature of possibles
Ghazali defines 'no end' as the possibility of endless successive events. Grasp this concept before moving to the proof.
This is crucial to avoid thinking possibles are finite.
3
Follow the disjunctive proof
Identify the two options: (1) multiple infinite powers (absurd) or (2) one power relating through a common feature. See why option 1 is impossible.
The impossibility of infinite regress was already established earlier.
4
Identify the common feature
Ghazali concludes that the common feature among all possibles is possibility (imkan) itself. That is how one power covers all.
This is the key move: the attribute of possibility unifies all objects of power.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The final sentence states that every possible is necessarily an object of power. Ask yourself: does this argument succeed?
Consider the assumption that God is one—this was proven earlier.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذ أثبتنا القدرة . . فلنذكر أحكامها :
احكام القدرة ومن حكمها : أنها متعلقة بجميع المقدورات ، وأعني بالمقدورات الممكنات كلها
ولا يخفى أنَّ الممكناتِ لا نهاية لها. فلا نهاية إذن للمقدورات ، ونعني بقولنا : لا نهاية للممكنات : أن خلق الحوادث بعد الحوادث لا ينتهي إلى حد يستحيلُ في العقل حدوث حادث بعده ، فالإمكان مستمر أبداً ، والقدرة واسعة لجميع ذلك وبرهان هذه الدعوى وهو عموم تعلق القدرة : أنه قد ظهرَ أنَّ صانع العالم واحدٌ ؛ فإما أن يكون له بإزاء كل مقدور قدرةٌ ، والمقدورات لا نهاية لها .. فتثبتُ قُدَرٌ متعددة لا نهاية لها ، وهو محال ؛ لما سبق في إبطال دورات لا نهاية لها (٣) وإما أن تكون القدرة واحدة ، فيكون تعلقها مع اتحادها بما يتعلق بها من الجواهر والأعراض مع اختلافها .. لأمر تشترك فيه ، ولا تشترك في أمر سوى الإمكان ، فيلزم منه : أن كل ممكن فهو مقدور ـ لا محالة ـ وواقع بالقدرة
And since we have established power, let us mention its rulings: among its rulings is that it is related to all objects of power (maqdurat), and by objects of power I mean all possible things (mumkinat). And it is not hidden that possibles have no end. Thus objects of power have no end. And by our saying 'possibles have no end' we mean that the creation of events after events does not terminate at a limit beyond which it is rationally impossible for an event to occur. So possibility continues forever, and power is vast for all of that. And the proof of this claim, which is the universality of power's relation, is: it has become clear that the Maker of the world is one. So either there is a power corresponding to each object of power, and objects of power have no end – then multiple powers without end would be established, which is impossible, due to what preceded in refuting infinite regresses. Or power is one, so its relation, despite its unity, to the things it relates to from substances and accidents despite their differences, is due to a common feature; and they share no common feature except possibility. Hence it follows necessarily that every possible is an object of power, and is effected by power.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage proves the universality of divine power. Ghazali first states the ruling: power relates to all possible things (mumkinat), which are infinite in number—meaning that events can continue indefinitely without rational contradiction. He then provides a proof: since God is one, and if each possible required its own power, there would have to be an infinite number of powers, which is impossible (as previously refuted in the context of infinite regress). Therefore, a single power must relate to all possibles. But how can one power relate to diverse objects? It must be due to a common feature shared by all possibles. That common feature is none other than possibility (imkan) itself. Thus, every possible is necessarily an object of power and is actualized by that power.

Key Terms
مقدور
ممكن
إمكان
قدرة واحدة
تعلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The infinity of possibles implies that God must have an infinite number of powers, one for each possible.
Correction: Ghazali argues that an infinite number of powers is impossible (due to infinite regress). Instead, one power suffices because it relates to all possibles through their shared attribute of possibility.

Checkpoint
Why must God's single power be related to all possible things?
A) Because God is all-knowing.
B) Because if each possible required a distinct power, there would be an infinite number of powers, which is impossible.
C) Because the Quran says so.
D) Because possibles are finite.

Key Takeaway
God's power is one, yet it relates to all possible things without limit, because they all share the attribute of possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's power truly covers every possible thing, what is one area of your life where you have silently assumed His power does not reach—a dream, a relationship, a healing? What would it mean for you to actually pray about it tonight as if it were possible?

The lesson says possibility continues forever and power is vast for all of that. Is there a sin you have committed that you secretly believe is beyond God's ability to forgive—not because of His mercy, but because of your own unworthiness? Is that sin a 'possible' thing? Then does God's power cover it? What will you do to test the reality of this truth this week?
?
In your daily experience, do you attribute events more to luck, coincidence, or your own efforts than to God's power? Identify one routine event you usually dismiss as 'natural' and intentionally train yourself to see it as an object of God's power. How will you remind yourself of this tomorrow?

Definition of Qudra and an Objection
Ghazali defines qudra and defers an objection about its eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's precise definition of qudra (divine power) and the objection raised about its eternity, along with the reason for deferring the answer.

Core Idea
Qudra is the attribute that prepares and causes the act, not mere ability.

Ghazali begins by giving a crisp definition of qudra (power) as the attribute by which an act is both prepared for the agent and actually occurs. He then faces an objection: if qudra is eternal, why isn't the world eternal? Instead of answering immediately, he defers the response to the later discussion on divine will. This lesson examines the definition and the objection, showing how Ghazali structures his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of qudra
Read the first part of the passage where Ghazali defines qudra as the attribute by which an act is prepared and occurs. Notice that he ties this definition back to a previous 'decisive division'.
This definition is technical, not just ordinary language.
2
Understand the objection
Read the objection: if qudra is eternal, its effect should also be eternal, but the world is temporal, so qudra must be temporal. See why this is a challenge.
The objection targets the consistency of an eternal attribute with temporal acts.
3
Note the deferred answer
Ghazali replies that the answer will come later when discussing the rulings of will (irada). Reflect on why will might be key to solving the problem.
This shows Ghazali's systematic approach: some issues depend on other concepts.
4
Connect definition and objection
Consider how the definition of qudra as the attribute that prepares and causes the act leads directly to the objection: if it is eternal, why aren't all acts eternal?
The objection hinges on the relationship between an eternal attribute and temporal effects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وضع اللسان : عبارة عن الصفة التي بها يتهيا الفعل للفاعل ، وبها يقعُ الفعل ، وهذا الوصفُ مما دلّ عليه التقسيم القاطع الذي ذكرناه ، ولسنا نعني بالقدرة إلا هذه الصفة ، وقد أثبتناها . تحريجة : إن لم فإن قيل : فهذا ينقلب عليكم في القدرة ؛ فإنها قديمة ، والفعل ليس يصدر العالم عن الذات لكيلا يكون بقديم ؟(۱) قديماً .. وجب أن تكون القدرة حادثة قلنا : سيأتي جوابه في أحكام الإرادة فيما يقع الفعل به لحدوث الفعل
The linguistic definition: it is an expression for the attribute by which the act becomes prepared for the agent, and by which the act occurs. This attribute is what the decisive division we mentioned indicated, and we mean by qudra (power) nothing other than this attribute, and we have established it. Objection: If it is said: 'This turns against you regarding qudra; for it is eternal, and the act does not proceed from the essence so that it would not be [produced] by an eternal [power]? ... eternal, so qudra must be temporal.' We said: Its answer will come in the rulings of will (al-irada) regarding how the act occurs due to the occurrence of the act.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage contains two parts: first, a definition of qudra, and second, an objection with a deferred answer. Ghazali begins by giving a linguistic definition of qudra (power) as the attribute by which an act becomes prepared for the agent and by which the act actually occurs. He clarifies that this attribute is exactly what was indicated by the 'decisive division' mentioned earlier, and he emphasizes that they mean nothing else by qudra. Then, an objection is raised: if qudra is eternal, then its act should also be eternal, but the world is temporal. The objection suggests that if the act is temporal, qudra must be temporal as well. Ghazali answers by deferring the full response to the later discussion on the rulings of will (irada), where the relationship between eternal will and temporal acts will be explained. This shows that the objection is acknowledged but its resolution depends on the nature of divine will.

Key Terms

صفة (sifa/attribute)
إرادة (irada/will)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think qudra is simply the ability to do anything, without a precise definition.
Correction: Ghazali defines qudra specifically as the attribute that both prepares the act and causes it to occur. It is not mere potentiality but an active attribute.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

Divine Power Extends to All Possibles
Ghazali proves that divine power relates to all possible things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine power must relate to every possible thing, based on the unity of God and the shared nature of possibility.

Core Idea
One divine power suffices for all possibles because they share nothing but possibility.

Ghazali has already established that God possesses power. Now he asks: what does this power cover? The answer is everything possible, but that claim is not obvious. In this passage, he provides a systematic proof that one single power can relate to an infinite range of possible things, using rational arguments about unity and shared attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Ghazali uses a disjunctive argument structure.
2
Identify the claim and the proof structure
Find the statement that divine power relates to all possible things. Then identify the two alternatives Ghazali presents: either many powers or one power, and why the first is impossible.
The proof is a disjunctive syllogism.
3
Understand the common attribute argument
Why does one power relate to different types of things? Because they all share possibility. Reflect on why possibility is a sufficient basis for a single power to cover all.
This is the key insight: imkan is the common denominator.
4
Apply the principle of likeness
Ghazali adds that power over a thing implies power over its like. See how this reinforces that one power can create endless similar events.
This prevents arbitrary exclusions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذ أثبتنا القدرة فلنذكر أحكامها : احكام القدرة ومن حكمها : أنها متعلقة بجميع المقدورات ، وأعني بالمقدورات الممكنات كلها . ولا يخفى أنَّ الممكناتِ لا نهاية لها. فلا نهاية إذن للمقدورات ، ونعني بقولنا : لا نهاية للممكنات : أن خلق الحوادث بعد الحوادث لا ينتهي إلى حد يستحيلُ في العقل حدوث حادث بعده ، فالإمكان مستمر أبداً ، والقدرة واسعة لجميع ذلك وبرهان هذه الدعوى وهو عموم تعلق القدرة : أنه قد ظهرَ أنَّ صانع العالم واحدٌ؛ فإما أن يكون له بإزاء كل مقدور قدرةٌ ، والمقدورات لا نهاية لها .. فتثبتُ قُدَرٌ متعددة لا نهاية لها ، وهو محال ؛ لما سبق في إبطال دورات لا نهاية لها (٣) وإما أن تكون القدرة واحدة ، فيكون تعلقها مع اتحادها بما يتعلق بها من الجواهر والأعراض مع اختلافها .. لأمر تشترك فيه ، ولا تشترك في أمر سوى الإمكان ، فيلزم منه : أن كل ممكن فهو مقدور ـ لا محالة ـ وواقع بالقدرة . وبالجملة : إذا صدرت منه الجواهر والأعراض .. استحال ألا يصدر منه قدرة الله تعالى واحدة أمثالها؛ فإنَّ القدرة على الشيء قدرة على مثله ، وإذا لم يمتنع التعدد في المقدور .. فنسبتها إلى الحركات كلها والألوان كلها على وتيرة واحدة ؛ فتصلح لخلق حركة بعد حركة على الدوام ، وكذا لون بعد لون ، وجوهر بعد جوهر ، وهكذا ، وهو الذي عنينا بقولنا : إِنَّ قدرتهُ تعالى متعلقة بكل ممكن؛ فإن الإمكان لا ينحصر في عدد ، ومناسبة ذات القدرة لا تختص بعدد دون عدد؛ فلا يمكن أن يشار إلى حركة فيقال : إنها خارجة عن إمكان تعلق القدرة بها ، مع أنها تعلقت بمثلها ، إذ بالضرورة نعلم : أنَّ ما وجبَ للشيء .. وجب لمثله
And since we have established qudra, let us mention its rulings: Among its rulings is that it relates to all that is within power (maqdurat), and I mean by maqdurat all possible things (mumkinat). It is not hidden that possible things have no end. Therefore, the maqdurat have no end. We mean by our saying 'no end to possible things' that the creation of events after events does not reach a limit beyond which the occurrence of an event becomes impossible in reason; possibility continues forever, and qudra is vast for all that. The proof of this claim—which is the universality of the relation of qudra—is that it has become clear that the Maker of the world is one. So either He has a qudra for every maqdur, and the maqdurat have no end—thus many unlimited qudras would be established, which is impossible due to what preceded in refuting infinite regressions—or qudra is one, so its relation, given its unity, to the substances and accidents that relate to it, despite their differences, is due to a matter in which they all share, and they share in nothing other than possibility. From this it follows necessarily that every possible is maqdur and occurs by qudra. In summary: if substances and accidents proceed from Him, it is impossible that a single qudra of God Most High does not proceed from Him like them; for qudra over a thing is qudra over its like. And since multiplicity in the maqdur is not impossible, its relation to all movements and all colors is on one plane; thus it is suitable for creating movement after movement perpetually, and likewise color after color, and substance after substance, and so on. That is what we meant by our saying: 'His qudra is related to every possible'; for possibility is not limited to a number, and the suitability of the essence of qudra is not specific to one number over another. So it is impossible to point to a movement and say: it is outside the possibility of qudra's relation, while it has related to its like, for we know by necessity that what is necessary for a thing is necessary for its like.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the universal scope of divine power. Ghazali first states the ruling: qudra (divine power) relates to all maqdurat, meaning all possible things (mumkinat). He clarifies that possible things are infinite, so maqdurat are also infinite. The proof (burhan) is presented as a disjunctive syllogism: The Maker is one. Either He has a separate power for each possible, leading to an infinite number of powers (impossible), or His power is one and relates to all possibles through a common attribute. The only common attribute among all possible things is possibility (imkan). Therefore, every possible is a maqdur and is actualized by power. Ghazali then reinforces this with a summary: since substances and accidents come from God, His power must be similar to them; power over a thing is power over its like. Thus, the relation of one power to all movements, colors, etc., is uniform, allowing endless creation. The key logical principle is that what holds for one thing holds for its like, and since possibility is not restricted to a number, power is not restricted either.

Key Terms
قدرة (qudra/power)
مقدور (maqdur/object of power)
ممكن (mumkin/possible)
إمكان (imkan/possibility)
جوهر (jawhar/substance)
عرض (arad/accident)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Infinite possible things would require infinite separate powers in God.
Correction: Ghazali shows that infinite powers lead to an impossible infinite regress; instead, one power suffices because all possibles share the attribute of possibility.

Checkpoint
What is the common attribute that allows one divine power to relate to all possible things?
A) Existence (wujud).
B) Contingency (huduth).
C) Possibility (imkan).
D) Necessity (wujub).

Key Takeaway
Divine power is one and relates to all possible things because they share the attribute of possibility, and power over a thing implies power over its like.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that nothing in your life—your trials, your successes, your daily circumstances—is outside the reach of Allah's power? Or do you secretly assume some things are too big, too random, or too 'small' for Him to have complete control over? What would change in your daily trust if you internalized that His one power relates to every possible event in your life?
?
If Allah's power extends to all possibles equally, why do you sometimes feel that your prayers for specific outcomes are chances? Is your faith in His power limited by your own imagination of what is possible? What does this passage say about the 'suitability' of His power to create any movement or any color—and how does that reshape your reliance on Him in your most personal requests?
?
The proof says that what is necessary for a thing is necessary for its like. If Allah has power over certain things you can name, do you truly believe His power is exactly the same over other things you have not seen or cannot imagine? What area of your life are you withholding from His power because you think it is somehow 'unlike' anything He has done before?

The Opposite of the Known: Is It Possible?
Ghazali's analysis of whether the opposite of God's knowledge is possible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the opposite of what God knows is not within His power, and how Ghazali resolves the apparent contradiction.

Core Idea
The opposite of the known is impossible not in itself, but due to God's prior knowledge.

This passage tackles a subtle question: if God knows everything, can He bring about something that contradicts His knowledge? For Ghazali, the issue is not about God's power but about logical consistency. He shows that when we properly define 'possible' and 'impossible', the opposite of the known is possible in itself but impossible because of its relation to God's knowledge. Let's trace his careful reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation.
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the question and Ghazali's response.
2
Identify the confusion.
Ghazali says people mistakenly think the opposite of the known is both possible and impossible. Why? Because they fail to distinguish different senses of 'possible' and 'impossible'.
This is a key logical move.
3
Understand the distinction.
Ghazali clarifies that something can be possible in itself (bi-dhatihi) but impossible due to an external factor (li-ghayrihi), like God's eternal will or knowledge.
He uses the world as an example: it is necessary given God's will, but possible in itself.
4
Apply to the opposite of the known.
Realize that the opposite of the known is possible in itself, but its occurrence is impossible because it conflicts with God's infallible knowledge.
Thus it is not within divine power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويتشعب عن هذا فروع ثلاثة : الأول : إن قال قائل : هل تقولون : إن خلاف المعلوم مقدور ؟ تحريجة: فما القول في الممكنات التي قلنا : هذا مما اختلف فيه ، ولا يتصور الخلافُ فيه إذا حقق وأزيل تعقد الألفاظ ؛ وبيانه : أنه قد ثبت أن كل ممكن مقدور ، وأن المحال ليس بمقدور ؛ فلننظر : أن خلاف المعلوم محال أو ممكن ، ولا تعرف ذلك إلا إذا عرفت معنى المحال والممكن وحصلت حقيقتهما ، وإلا .. فإن تساهلت في النظر .. فربما صدق على خلاف المعلوم : أنَّه محال ، وأنه ممكن ليس بمحال ، فإذن قد صدق أنه محال وأنه ليس بمحال ؛ والنقيضان لا يصدقان معاً . فاعلم : أنَّ تحت اللفظ إجمالاً ، وإنما ينكشف لك ذلك بما أقوله ؛ وهو : أن العالم مثلاً يصدق عليه : أنه واجب ، وأنه محال ، وأنه ممكن أما كونه واجباً.. فمن حيث إنه إذا فرضت إرادة القديم موجودة وجوداً واجباً.. كان المراد أيضاً واجباً بالضرورة، لا جائزاً؛ إذ يستحيل عدم المراد مع تحقق الإرادة القديمة وجودها؟
And from this branch three sub-issues: First: If someone says: 'Do you say that the opposite of the known (khilaf al-ma'lum) is maqdur (within power)?' Critique: Then what is the statement regarding the possible things? We said: This is something about which there is disagreement, and the disagreement is not conceivable if the matter is verified and the complication of words is removed. Its clarification: It has been established that every possible is maqdur, and the impossible is not maqdur. So let us consider: is the opposite of the known impossible or possible? You do not know that unless you know the meaning of impossible and possible and apprehend their realities. Otherwise, if you are lax in consideration, you might deem that the opposite of the known is impossible and also that it is possible, not impossible. Then it would be both impossible and not impossible, and two contradictories cannot be true together. Know that there is ambiguity in the wording, and that will be disclosed to you by what I say: For example, the world can be described as necessary, impossible, and possible. As for its being necessary, it is insofar as if the eternal will (al-irada al-qadima) is assumed to exist necessarily, then the willed object is also necessarily, not contingent; for it is impossible for the willed object not to exist while the eternal will is in existence? [unclear]
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is the first of three branches (furu') stemming from the discussion on the scope of divine power. The objection asks whether the opposite of what God knows (khilaf al-ma'lum) is within His power. Ghazali states that disagreement arises only from verbal confusion. After clarifying the meanings of 'possible' and 'impossible', he notes that if one is careless, the opposite of the known might be deemed both impossible and possible, leading to a contradiction. The resolution is that the opposite of the known is possible in itself (bi-dhatihi), but impossible due to an external factor (li-ghayrihi), namely God's prior knowledge. Ghazali uses the example of the world: it can be called necessary (from one perspective, given eternal will), impossible (from another), and possible (in itself). The key is that once God's eternal will is assumed to exist necessarily, the willed object becomes necessary in terms of occurrence, not in its essence. Thus, the opposite of the known is not maqdur because it is impossible given God's knowledge, but it remains possible in itself. The passage emphasizes careful conceptual analysis to avoid contradictions.

Key Terms
خلاف المعلوم (khilaf al-ma'lum/opposite of the known)
مقدور (maqdur/within power)
محال (muhal/impossible)
ممكن (mumkin/possible)
إرادة قديمة (irada qadima/eternal will)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if God knows something, its opposite becomes absolutely impossible, even in itself.
Correction: Ghazali distinguishes between essential impossibility and impossibility due to an external factor. The opposite of the known is possible in itself but impossible relative to God's knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why does the opposite of the known appear to be both possible and impossible?
A) Because the wording is ambiguous and people fail to distinguish different senses of 'possible' and 'impossible'.
B) Because God's power is limited.
C) Because the opposite of the known is neither possible nor impossible.
D) Because human knowledge is fallible.

Key Takeaway
The opposite of the known is possible in itself, but its occurrence is impossible because it contradicts God's infallible knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's knowledge makes the opposite of what He knows impossible, do you truly believe you could have done otherwise in your past mistakes? How does this affect your view of your own accountability before Him?
?
In what area of your life right now are you tempted to think that a good outcome is 'impossible' because of circumstances? Does Ghazali's distinction between something being impossible in itself versus impossible due to an external factor give you hope that with God's help, that 'impossible' might actually be possible?
?
When you pray for something, do you ever doubt that God can change your situation because He already knows what will happen? How does this lesson challenge that doubt, and what will you do today to act on that challenge?

God's Power Encompasses All Possible Things
Ghazali argues that God's power extends to every possible thing equally.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's power is equally related to all possible things and why no object of power can be excluded.

Core Idea
God's power is uniform and unlimited—it extends to every possible thing equally.

This passage from Ghazali's Iqtisad presents a rigorous argument for the universality of divine power. He begins with a conditional premise and uses the principle of like things having like rulings to demonstrate that God's power relates to all possibilities without exception. As you read, notice how Ghazali moves from a general principle to a specific conclusion, and then mentions three branches that follow from this foundation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once carefully, then read the literal translation. Let the argument flow naturally before analyzing it.
Pay attention to the logical connector words like 'if', 'for', 'thus'.
2
Identify the key principle
Find the statement 'power over a thing is power over its like'. This is the foundation of the entire argument.
3
Understand the argument that possibility is unlimited
Ghazali argues that possibility is not confined to a number, so the essence of power cannot be specific to one instance over another.
This refutes any notion that God's power is limited to a fixed set of things.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The conclusion is that God's power relates to every possible thing equally, and no movement or object can be excluded if its like is included.
The phrase 'by necessity we know' indicates this is a self-evident truth.
5
Note the three branches
The passage ends by stating that three branches diverge from this principle. The following lessons will explore them.
This sets up the next stage of the discussion.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تتعلق بكل ممكن
وبالجملة : إذا صدرت منه الجواهر والأعراض .. استحال ألا يصدر منه قدرة الله تعالى واحدة أمثالها ؛ فإنَّ القدرة على الشيء قدرة على مثله ، وإذا لم يمتنع التعدد في المقدور .. فنسبتها إلى الحركات كلها والألوان كلها على وتيرة واحدة ؛ فتصلح لخلق حركة بعد حركة على الدوام ، وكذا لون بعد لون ، وجوهر بعد جوهر ، وهكذا ، وهو الذي عنينا بقولنا : إِنَّ قدرتهُ تعالى متعلقة بكل ممكن ؛ فإن الإمكان لا ينحصر في عدد ، ومناسبة ذات القدرة لا تختص بعدد دون عدد ؛ فلا يمكن أن يشار إلى حركة فيقال : إنها خارجة عن إمكان تعلق القدرة بها ، مع أنها تعلقت بمثلها ، إذ بالضرورة نعلم : أنَّ ما وجبَ للشيء .. وجب لمثله
·
ويتشعب عن هذا فروع ثلاثة :
It is related to every possible thing. In summary: if substances and accidents proceed from Him, it is impossible that a single one of His power's [objects] should not proceed from Him; for power over a thing is power over its like. And if multiplicity in the object of power is not impossible, then its relation to all movements and all colors is uniform; so it is fit to create movement after movement perpetually, likewise color after color, substance after substance, and so on. This is what we meant by our saying: His power, exalted, is related to every possible thing; for possibility is not confined to a number, and the suitability of the essence of power is not specific to one number rather than another. Thus it is impossible to point to a movement and say it is outside the possibility of the relation of power to it, when it has been related to its like, since we know by necessity that what is necessary for a thing is necessary for its like. And from this three branches diverge.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is a proof that God's power encompasses every possible thing. Ghazali begins with a conditional: if substances and accidents (the two categories of contingent beings) emanate from God, then it is impossible that a single instance of what is within His power should not emanate from Him. The reason is that power over a thing entails power over its like; there is no intrinsic limitation in the divine power that would make it relate to one specific instance but not to another similar instance. Since the object of power can be multiple without contradiction, the power's relation to all possibilities is uniform—it can create any movement, color, or substance indefinitely. This is the intended meaning of saying that His power is related to every possible thing. Possibility is unlimited, and the essence of power is not tied to any particular number. Therefore, no movement can be excluded from the scope of power if a similar movement is already within it, because it is a necessary truth that what is necessary for a thing is also necessary for its like. Finally, Ghazali notes that three branches diverge from this principle, though only the first is discussed in the subsequent lesson.

Key Terms
قدرة (power)
مقدور (object of power)
ممكن (possible)
استحالة (impossibility)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think God's power is selective or limited to certain types of things.
Correction: Ghazali emphasizes that God's power is uniform and universal, relating to all possible things equally without exception.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why does God's power extend to every possible thing?
A) Because possibility is limited in number.
B) Because power over a thing is power over its like, and possibility is unlimited.
C) Because God's power is arbitrary and not bound by reason.
D) Because the world is eternal and therefore must be created.

Key Takeaway
God's power is equally related to all possible things because power over a thing entails power over its like, and possibility is unlimited.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation that seems impossible, do you truly believe God’s power is sufficient for it, or do you limit Him by your own reasoning? What would change if you internalized that His power relates equally to every possible thing?
?
The passage says power over a thing is power over its like. If God has given you one blessing, He could give you countless more like it. Are you ungrateful by expecting little from Him? Repent from limiting His generosity.
?
Ghazali says possibility is not confined to a number. Is your trust in God’s provision confined by your calculations? Will you expand your hope to encompass all that He can do, even beyond what you can imagine?

The Contrary of the Known: Possible in Essence, Impossible by Another
Ghazali resolves whether the contrary of God's knowledge is possible, using three considerations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali distinguishes between something being possible in itself and impossible due to another, using the example of the world and the contrary of God's knowledge.

Core Idea
A thing can be possible in itself yet impossible because of God's knowledge.

Ghazali tackles a deep theological puzzle: if God knows something will happen, can its opposite still be within divine power? This might seem contradictory—either something is possible or it isn't. But Ghazali shows that by carefully distinguishing aspects, we can see that the contrary of the known is possible in its own essence but impossible due to the necessary relation to God's knowledge. This lesson unpacks his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the question
The passage begins with a direct question: Is the contrary of what God knows within divine power? Recognize that this is a real theological issue that requires careful conceptual analysis.
Ghazali notes that disagreement arises from unclear terminology.
2
Learn the threefold consideration
Ghazali uses the example of the world to show that one thing can be considered necessary, impossible, or possible depending on the aspect. Pay attention to the three considerations: with will, without will, and essence alone.
This is the key to resolving the apparent contradiction.
3
Apply to the contrary of the known
Ghazali applies the same logic to the example of Zayd's life on Saturday. See how he concludes that creating life is possible in itself but impossible because it would contradict God's prior knowledge.
The impossibility is due to another, not the thing itself.
4
Distinguish types of impossibility
Notice the distinction between 'impossible in itself' (like combining black and white) and 'impossible due to another' (like Zayd's life). This is crucial for understanding divine power.
5
Reflect on the resolution
Ghazali resolves the original question: the contrary of the known is possible in itself but impossible due to God's knowledge. This preserves both divine power and the necessity of God's knowledge.
This shows how kalam uses rational distinctions to defend orthodoxy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول :
إن قال قائل : هل تقولون : إن خلاف المعلوم مقدور ؟
تحريجة: فما القول في الممكنات التي
قلنا : هذا مما اختلف فيه ، ولا يتصور الخلافُ فيه إذا حقق وأزيل تعقيد الألفاظ ؛ وبيانه : أنه قد ثبت أن كل ممكن مقدور ، وأن المحال ليس بمقدور ؛ فلننظر : أن خلاف المعلوم محال أو ممكن ، ولا تعرف ذلك إلا إذا عرفت معنى المحال والممكن وحصلت حقيقتهما ، وإلا .. فإن تساهلت في النظر .. فربما صدق على خلاف المعلوم : أنَّه محال ، وأنه ممكن ليس بمحال ، فإذن قد صدق أنه محال وأنه ليس بمحال ؛ والنقيضان لا يصدقان معاً
فاعلم : أنَّ تحت اللفظ إجمالاً ، وإنما ينكشف لك ذلك بما أقوله ؛ وهو : أن العالم مثلاً يصدق عليه : أنه واجب ، وأنه محال ، وأنه ممكن أما كونه واجباً .. فمن حيث إنه إذا فرضت إرادة القديم موجودة وجوداً واجباً .. كان المراد أيضاً واجباً بالضرورة، لا جائزاً ؛ إذ يستحيل عدم المراد مع تحقق الإرادة القديمة
وأما كونه محالاً .. فهو أنه لو قُدِّر عدم تعلق الإرادة بإيجاده . . فيكون - لا محالة - حدوثه محالاً ؛ إذ يؤدي إلى حدوث حادث بلا سبب ، وقد عرف أنه محال .
 وأما كونه ممكناً . . فهو بأن ينظر إلى ذاته فقط ، ولا يعتبر معه لا وجود الإرادة ولا عدمها ؛ فيكون له وصف الإمكان ، فإذن الاعتبارات ثلاثة : - الأول : أن نشرط فيه وجودَ الإرادة وتعلقها ، فهو بهذا الاعتبار واجب .
- الثاني : أن نعتبر فقدَ الإرادة ، فهو بهذا الاعتبار محال
- الثالث : أن نقطعَ الالتفات إلى الإرادة والسبب ، فلا نعتبر وجوده ولا عدمَهُ ، ونجردَ النظر إلى ذات العالم ، فيبقى له بهذا الاعتبار الأمر الثالث ، وهو الإمكان ، ونعني به : أنه ممكن لذاته ؛ أي : إذا لم نشترط قد يكون الشيء غير ذاته .. كان ممكناً ؛ فظهر منه : أنه يجوز أن يكون الشيء الواحد ممكناً و محالاً من وجه آخر ممكنا من وجه محالاً ، ولكن ممكناً باعتبار ذاته ، محالاً باعتبار غيره ، ولا يجوز أن يكون ممكناً لذاته محالاً لذاته ؛ فهما متناقضان ، فترجع إلى خلاف المعلوم :
السبت مثلاً يوم
فنقول : إذا سبق في علم الله تعالى إماتة زيد صبيحة فنقول : خَلْقُ الحياة لزيد صبيحة يوم السبت ممكن أو ليس بممكن ؟
فالحق فيه أنه ممكن ومحال ؛ أي : هو ممكن باعتبار ذاته إن قطع الالتفات إلى غيره ، ومحال لغيره لا لذاته ؛ وذلك إذا اعتبر معه الالتفاتُ إلى تعلق العلم بالإماتة ، والمحال لذاته : هو الذي يمتنع لذاته ؛ كالجمع بين السواد والبياض ، لا للزوم استحالة في غير ذاته
وحياة زيد لو قدَّرَتْ .. لم تمتنع لذات الحياة ، ولكن يلزم منه استحالة في غير ذاتها ، وهو ذات العلم ؛ إذ ينقلب جهلاً ، ومحال أن ينقلب جهلاً .. فبانَ أنَّه ممكن لذاته ، محال للزوم استحالة في غيره
The first: If someone says: Do you say that the contrary of the known is within power? A marginal note: What is the statement concerning the possibles that... We said: This is something about which there is disagreement, and disagreement about it is not conceivable when it is investigated and the complication of expressions is removed. Its clarification is that it has been established that every possible is within power, and the impossible is not within power. So let us consider: is the contrary of the known impossible or possible? And you do not know that unless you know the meaning of the impossible and the possible and grasp their true realities. Otherwise, if you are lax in investigation, then perhaps the contrary of the known may be true as impossible and true as possible, not impossible. Then it would be true that it is impossible and that it is not impossible; but two contradictories cannot both be true. Know that there is ambiguity in the expression, and that will be revealed to you by what I say: namely, that the world, for example, is true of it that it is necessary, that it is impossible, and that it is possible. As for its being necessary: from the aspect that if the eternal will is assumed to exist with necessary existence, then the object of will is also necessary by necessity, not contingent; for it is impossible for the object of will not to exist when the eternal will is realized. As for its being impossible: if the absence of the will's relation to its bringing into existence is supposed, then its occurrence must be impossible; for it leads to the occurrence of an event without a cause, and it has been known that this is impossible. As for its being possible: it is by looking at its essence alone, without considering with it either the existence of will or its absence; then the description of possibility applies to it. Thus there are three considerations: The first: that we posit in it the existence of will and its relation, then in this respect it is necessary. The second: that we consider the absence of will, then in this respect it is impossible. The third: that we cut off attention to the will and the cause, so we consider neither its existence nor its non-existence, and we isolate the consideration to the essence of the world, then there remains for it in this respect the third attribute, which is possibility. By this we mean that it is possible in itself; i.e., if we do not posit something other than its essence, it was possible. Thus it is clear from this that it is permissible for one thing to be possible and impossible from another aspect, possible in one respect and impossible in another. But it is possible in respect of its essence, impossible in respect of something else, and it is not permissible for it to be possible in itself and impossible in itself; for they are contradictory. Then we return to the contrary of the known: Saturday, for example, a day. So we say: If the death of Zayd on Saturday morning has preceded in God's knowledge, then we say: creating life for Zayd on Saturday morning is possible or not possible? The truth about it is that it is possible and impossible; i.e., it is possible in respect of its essence if one cuts off attention to something else, and impossible due to something else, not in itself. That is when attention to the relation of knowledge to the death is considered. The impossible in itself is that which is impossible in itself, like combining black and white, not because of an impossibility in something else. And Zayd's life, if it were supposed, would not be impossible in itself due to life, but an impossibility follows from it in something other than its essence, which is the essence of knowledge; for it would turn into ignorance, and it is impossible for it to turn into ignorance. So it became clear that it is possible in itself, impossible due to an impossibility that follows in something else.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer concerning whether the contrary of what God knows (khilaf al-ma'lum) is within divine power. Ghazali begins by presenting the objection: someone asks, 'Do you say that the contrary of the known is within power?' The answer begins by noting that this is a subject of disagreement, but once the terminology is clarified, the dispute disappears. The key is to understand the true meanings of 'possible' and 'impossible'. If one is careless, one might think that the contrary of the known is both impossible and possible, but that would be a contradiction. Ghazali resolves this by explaining that the same thing can be considered from different aspects. He uses the example of the world: it can be called necessary (if we consider the will attached to it), impossible (if we consider the absence of will), and possible (if we consider its essence alone). Thus, the world is possible in itself but necessary or impossible due to an external factor. Similarly, the contrary of the known (e.g., creating life for Zayd on Saturday, when God knows he will die then) is possible in itself, because in itself life is not impossible. However, it is impossible due to the fact that it would contradict God's prior knowledge, turning it into ignorance, which is impossible. Ghazali emphasizes that the inherent impossibility is not in the thing itself but in the necessary relation to God's knowledge. Therefore, the correct answer is that the contrary of the known is possible in itself (mumkin li-dhatihi) but impossible due to something else (muhal li-ghayrihi).

Key Terms
خلاف المعلوم (contrary of the known)
ممكن لذاته (possible in itself)
محال لغيره (impossible due to another)
محال لذاته (impossible in itself)
إرادة (will)
علم (knowledge)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God knows something, its opposite is absolutely impossible and not even possible in itself.
Correction: Ghazali shows that the opposite is possible in itself (essence) but impossible due to the necessary relation to God's knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why is creating life for Zayd on Saturday impossible even though it is possible in itself?
A) Because life is inherently impossible.
B) Because it contradicts God's prior knowledge, turning it into ignorance.
C) Because God's power is limited.
D) Because the world cannot have two different events.

Key Takeaway
The contrary of what God knows is possible in its own essence but impossible because it would entail a contradiction in God's knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a situation in your life where you believed that because God already knows the outcome, it was fixed and unchangeable. How does Ghazali's distinction between 'possible in itself' and 'impossible due to another' change your understanding of your own choices and God's knowledge? What will you do differently now?
?
Ghazali says the contrary of the known is possible in its essence but impossible because it would turn God's knowledge into ignorance. Are there areas in your life where you act as if your limited knowledge is the ultimate determiner of what is possible? Confess to yourself one specific instance where you assumed impossibility out of ignorance, and commit to seeking God's guidance to expand your understanding.
?
If everything that exists or occurs is either necessary, possible, or impossible only in relation to God's will and knowledge, then your own acts are not meaningless despite being known. How does this realization press you to take full responsibility for your actions today, knowing that they are genuinely possible in themselves and that God's knowledge does not negate your power to choose? What specific, hard choice will you face today with this renewed conviction?

Defining Maqdur: Possibility in Essence and Divine Power
Ghazali defines maqdur and defends its usage linguistically.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two conditions for something to be called maqdur (within divine power) and how Ghazali argues for its correct linguistic usage.

Core Idea
Maqdur means possibility in essence and completeness of divine power, not actual occurrence.

In this passage, Ghazali clarifies the precise meaning of the term maqdur. He distinguishes between what is possible in itself and the capacity of God's power, addressing a common misunderstanding. He also uses linguistic evidence to show that the term is correctly applied even when actual occurrence is prevented by divine foreknowledge. Let's examine his argument closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then its literal translation. Notice Ghazali's precise language and the structure of his argument.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two conditions for maqdur
Ghazali says that when we call something maqdur, we mean two things: life itself is not impossible, and God's power is fully capable. Find these in the text.
These are the two matters he says cannot be denied.
3
Understand the opponent's objection and Ghazali's response
The opponent claims it is not maqdur because its existence leads to impossibility (due to divine foreknowledge). Ghazali agrees with the meaning but argues about the word's correct usage.
Notice that Ghazali does not reject the opponent's point; he shifts to linguistic correctness.
4
Examine the linguistic evidence
Ghazali uses the example of a person being able to move or be still, even though divine knowledge determines one. This shows that 'qadir' is used based on intrinsic possibility and capability.
The linguistic usage supports calling it maqdur.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا قلنا : حياة زيد في هذا الوقت مقدورة .. لم ترد به إلا أن الحياة من حيث إنها حياة ليس بمحالٍ كالجمع بين السواد والبياض ، وقدرة الله تعالى من حيث إنها قدرةٌ لا تنبو عن التعلَّق بخلق الحياة ، ولا تتقاصر عنه ولا ضعف ولا سبب في ذات القدرة ، وهذان أمران يستحيل إنكارهما ؛ أعني : نفي القصور عن ذات القدرة ، وثبوت الإمكان لذات الحياة من حيث إنها حياةٌ فقط من غير التفات إلى غيرها ، والخصم إذا قال : غير مقدور ؛ على معنى : أن وجودَهُ يؤدي إلى استحالة .. فهو صادق في هذا المعنى . فإنَّا لسنا ننكره . ويبقى النظر في اللفظ : وهو أنَّ الصواب من حيث اللغةُ إطلاق هذا الاسم عليه أو سلبه ؟ ولا يخفى أن الصواب إطلاق اللفظ ؛ فإن الناس يقولون : فلان قادر على الحركة والسكون ، إن شاء تحرك وإن شاء سكن ، ويقولون : إن له في كل وقت قدرة على الضدين ، ويعلمون أن الجاري في علم الله تعالى وقوع أحدهما ، فالإطلاقات شاهدةً لما ذكرناه، وحظ المعنى منه ضروري لا سبيل إلى جحده
For if we say: Zayd's life at this time is within power (maqdur), we only mean that life, insofar as it is life, is not impossible like combining black and white, and the power of God Almighty, insofar as it is power, does not fall short of being attached to creating life, nor is it deficient, nor is there any weakness or cause in the essence of power. These two matters are impossible to deny: I mean, the negation of deficiency from the essence of power, and the establishment of possibility for the essence of life insofar as it is life only without attention to anything else. And if the opponent says: it is not within power (ghayr maqdur), meaning that its existence leads to impossibility, then he is truthful in this meaning, for we do not deny it. Then the consideration remains regarding the word (lafz): is it correct linguistically to apply this name (maqdur) to it or to negate it? It is not hidden that the correct is to apply the word; for people say: So-and-so is able (qadir) to move and to be still, if he wills he moves and if he wills he is still, and they say that he has at every time power over the two opposites, and they know that what proceeds in the knowledge of God Almighty is the occurrence of one of them. So the usages bear witness to what we mentioned, and the share of meaning from it is necessary, there is no way to deny it.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage argues for the correct usage of the term 'maqdur' (within power). Ghazali first clarifies that when we say Zayd's life is maqdur, we mean two things: life is not impossible in itself (like combining black and white), and God's power is fully capable of creating it. Even if the opponent points out that actualizing it would lead to an impossibility (due to divine foreknowledge), we agree but argue that linguistic usage still supports calling it maqdur. Ghazali provides a linguistic analogy: people say a person can move or be still, even though divine foreknowledge determines which will occur. This shows that the term 'qadir' is used based on intrinsic possibility and capability, not on actual outcome.

Key Terms
المقدور
القدرة
الإمكان الذاتي
اللفظ
التعلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think 'maqdur' requires that the thing actually be possible to occur, i.e., not prevented by God's foreknowledge.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies that 'maqdur' only requires possibility in essence and completeness of divine power; actual occurrence may be prevented by other factors like God's knowledge.

Checkpoint
Why does Ghazali say it is correct to call Zayd's life maqdur?

B) Because it actually occurs
C) Because the opponent agrees
D) Because it is impossible in any respect

Key Takeaway
Something is maqdur if it is intrinsically possible and God's power is fully capable of creating it, regardless of actual occurrence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah can do anything,' do you instinctively exclude things you know will never happen, or do you truly accept that His power covers all possibilities except the logically impossible? Examine your heart: is there any limitation you place on Allah based on your own expectations of what will occur?
?
Ghazali argues that the term 'maqdur' is applied based on intrinsic possibility and divine capacity, not on actual outcome. How often do you judge the power of Allah by what you see happening in your life, rather than by what He is capable of? What would change if you started seeing every possible good as truly within His power, even if it never materializes?
?
The opponent in this passage says that if something's actual existence leads to impossibility (like contradicting Allah's knowledge), it cannot be maqdur. In your own spiritual struggles, do you ever think 'Allah cannot change this situation because He already knows what will happen'? How can you distinguish between Allah's knowledge of what will be and His infinite ability to create any possible state? What would it mean for you to trust both His knowledge and His power?

Proof of a Thing Capable Between Two Powers
Refutation of preference and proof of maqdur bayn qadirayn.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that a single action can be subject to both divine and human power without implying that one power overrides the other.

Core Idea
Creation is equal; no power can be stronger than another in the same act.

Ghazali begins by refuting a mistaken idea that God's power would necessarily prevail over human power, which would lead to denying human agency. He then builds a decisive proof that leads to the conclusion of a single maqdur between two qadirs, showing that the purpose of both powers is creation, and creation is equal.

Guided Path
1
Understand the false notion
Read the passage that starts with 'وإن ظنّ' and see the assumption that God's power being stronger would make His maqdur prevail. Identify why Ghazali calls this impossible.
2
See the equality of creation
Focus on the statement that the benefit (fāʾida) of both powers is creation (ikhtirāʿ), and creation is equal, with no stronger or weaker. This is key to the refutation.
3
Grasp the decisive proof
Realize that the proof of two powers necessarily leads to a single maqdur between two powers. Trace Ghazali's reasoning from the refutation to this conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن ظنّ أن مقدور الله تعالى يترجَحُ ؛ لأن قدرته أقوى .. فهو محالٌ ؛ لأن تعلُّق القدرة بحركة واحدة لا تفضل تعلق قدرةٍ أخرى بها ؛ إذ كانت فائدة القدرتين الاختراع ، وإنما قوَّتُهُ باقتداره على غيره ، واقتداره على غيره غير مرجّح في الحركة التي فيها الكلام ؛ إذ حظ الحركة من كل واحدة من القدرتين أن تصير مخترعة بها ، والاختراع تساو ، فليس فيه أشدُّ وأضعف حتى يكون فيه ترجيح ، فإذن الدليل القاطع على إثبات القدرتين ساقنا إلى إثباتِ مقدور بين قادرين. فإن قيل : الدليلُ لا يسوقُ إلى محالٍ لا يفهم ، وما ذكرتموه غير مقدور بن قادرين غير مفهوم ؟ قلنا : علينا تفهيمُهُ ؛ وهو أنا نقول : اختراع الله تعالى للحركة في يد العبدِ معقول دون أن تكون الحركة مقدورة للعبد ، فمهما خلق الحركة وخلق معها قدرة عليها . . كان هو المستبد بالاختراع للقدرة والمقدور جميعاً وخرج منه : أنه منفرد بالاختراع ، وأنَّ الحركة موجودة ، وأنَّ المتحرك عليها قادر ، وبسبب كونه قادراً فارقَ حاله حالة المرتعد ؛ فاندفعت الإشكالات كلها. وحاصله : أنَّ القادر واسعُ القدرة ، فهو قادر على الاختراع للقدرة والمقدور معاً ، ولما كان اسم الخالق والمخترع مطلقاً على من أوجد الشيء بقدرته ، وكانت القدرة والمقدور جميعاً بقدرة الله تعالى.. سمي خالقاً. لا نسمي العبد خالقاً ومخترعاً ، ولم يكن المقدورُ بقدرة العبد وإن كان معها .. فلم يسم خالقاً ومخترعاً وإنما الكسب فقط. ووجب أن يُطلب لهذا النمط من النسبة اسم آخر مخالف ، فطلب له اسم الكسب ؛ تيمناً بكتاب الله تعالى (٢) ، فإنه وجد إطلاق ذلك على أعمال العباد في القرآن ، وأما اسم الفعل .. فترددوا في إطلاقه ولا مشاحة في الأسامي بعد فهم المعاني
And if one supposes that the thing capable of God Almighty is preferred because His power is stronger... then it is impossible; because the attachment of power to a single movement is not superior to the attachment of another power to it, since the benefit of the two powers is creation (ikhtirāʿ), and their strength lies in their ability over something else, and their ability over something else does not give preference in the movement under discussion; since the share of the movement from each of the two powers is that it becomes created by it, and creation is equal, there is no stronger or weaker in it such that there would be preference. So the decisive proof for establishing the two powers leads us to establish a thing capable (maqdūr) between two powers. If it is said: The proof does not lead to something impossible that is not understood, and what you have mentioned—a thing capable between two powers—is not understood? We say: We must make it understood; and it is that we say: God Almighty's creation (ikhtirāʿ) of the movement in the hand of the servant is conceivable without the movement being capable (maqdūr) for the servant. So whenever He creates the movement and creates with it a power over it... He is the sole possessor of creation of the power and the capable thing together, and it follows from that: that He is unique in creation, and that the movement exists, and that the mover is capable over it, and because he is capable he differs from the state of the trembler; so all the problems are repelled. The summary: that the capable one is vast in power, so He is capable of creating the power and the capable thing together. And since the name 'Creator' (al-Khāliq) and 'Inventor' (al-Mukhtariʿ) is applied to one who brings a thing into existence by his power, and the power and the capable thing are both by the power of God Almighty... He is called Creator. We do not call the servant a creator and inventor, and the capable thing is not by the power of the servant even if it is with it... so he is not called a creator and inventor, but only acquisition (al-kasb). And it became necessary to seek for this type of attribution another name, so the name 'acquisition' (kasb) was sought for it, in imitation of the Book of God Almighty, for its application to the deeds of servants was found in the Qur'an. As for the name 'act' (fiʿl), they hesitated in applying it. And there is no harm in names after the meanings are understood.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage presents a complete theological argument: a proof, an objection, an answer, and a terminological conclusion. It begins by refuting a false notion that God's power being stronger would make His maqdūr (thing capable) prevail over the servant's. Ghazālī argues that since both powers are for ikhtirāʿ (creation), and creation is equal, no preference arises. The decisive proof for two powers leads to the necessity of a single maqdūr between two qādīrs (powerful beings). An objection follows: this concept is incomprehensible. The answer clarifies: God can create both the movement and the servant's power simultaneously. The servant then has a power (qudra) over the movement, but God alone is its creator (mukhtariʿ). The servant is qādir (capable) and differs from an involuntary agent like a trembler. The summary states that God creates both the power and its object, so He alone is called Khāliq and Mukhtariʿ. The servant is not called khāliq because his power does not bring about the act independently; the act exists with his power but not by it. Hence the term kasb (acquisition) is used, taken from the Qur'an, and there is no dispute over names once meanings are understood.

Key Terms
مقدور بين قادرين
اختراع
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that God's stronger power would override human power in a shared action, making human power irrelevant.
Correction: Ghazali argues that creation is equal; there is no stronger or weaker creation. Both powers are for creating, and they do not conflict in intensity.

Checkpoint
Why does Ghazali say it is impossible for God's power to be preferred over the servant's power in the same action?
A) Because God's power is not actually stronger.
B) Because creation (ikhtirāʿ) is equal and admits no stronger or weaker.
C) Because the movement is not capable of receiving preference.
D) Because preference only applies to voluntary acts, not created ones.

Key Takeaway
The proof of two powers necessarily entails a single maqdur between them because creation is equal and admits no degrees.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you act, do you truly believe that your power is real yet entirely dependent on God’s power? How does this shape your moment-by-moment reliance on Him?
?
If creation is equal and admits no degrees, what excuse do you have for thinking your small acts of obedience are insignificant compared to others'?
?
The proof says strength of power is about ability over other things, not intensity in this act. How does this challenge your pride in your own abilities or your despair at your weaknesses?

Defining Kasb: The Servant's Role
Clarification of intelligibility and definition of kasb.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali makes the concept of a maqdur between two powers intelligible and why the servant's act is called kasb, not creation.

Core Idea
The servant is capable but not creator; his act is an acquisition (kasb).

After proving the possibility, Ghazali addresses an objection that the concept of a thing between two powers is unintelligible. He clarifies by explaining that God creates both the act and the servant's power, making the servant genuinely capable without being a creator. This leads to the terminological distinction between Khaliq and kasb, based on Qur'anic usage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the objection in the passage: 'If it is said: The proof does not lead to something impossible that is not understood, and what you have mentioned is not understood?' Recognize the challenge against the intelligibility of maqdur bayn qadirayn.
2
Understand the answer
Ghazali says: 'We must make it understood' and then explains that God creates the movement and creates power over it in the servant. The servant becomes capable (qādir) and differs from a trembler. Trace how this makes the concept clear.
3
Grasp the summary
Read the summary: 'The capable one is vast in power, so He is capable of creating the power and the capable thing together.' This reinforces that God creates both the act and the power, so He alone is Creator.
4
Learn the terminological conclusion
Notice why the servant is not called Khāliq or Mukhtariʿ: because his power does not bring about the act independently. The term kasb is adopted from the Qur'an. Understand that the name is secondary after the meaning is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن ظنّ أن مقدور الله تعالى يترجَحُ ؛ لأن قدرته أقوى .. فهو محالٌ ؛ لأن تعلُّق القدرة بحركة واحدة لا تفضل تعلق قدرةٍ أخرى بها ؛ إذ كانت فائدة القدرتين الاختراع ، وإنما قوَّتُهُ باقتداره على غيره ، واقتداره على غيره غير مرجّح في الحركة التي فيها الكلام ؛ إذ حظ الحركة من كل واحدة من القدرتين أن تصير مخترعة بها ، والاختراع تساو ، فليس فيه أشدُّ وأضعف حتى يكون فيه ترجيح ، فإذن الدليل القاطع على إثبات القدرتين ساقنا إلى إثباتِ مقدور بين قادرين. فإن قيل : الدليلُ لا يسوقُ إلى محالٍ لا يفهم ، وما ذكرتموه غير مقدور بن قادرين غير مفهوم ؟ قلنا : علينا تفهيمُهُ ؛ وهو أنا نقول : اختراع الله تعالى للحركة في يد العبدِ معقول دون أن تكون الحركة مقدورة للعبد ، فمهما خلق الحركة وخلق معها قدرة عليها . . كان هو المستبد بالاختراع للقدرة والمقدور جميعاً وخرج منه : أنه منفرد بالاختراع ، وأنَّ الحركة موجودة ، وأنَّ المتحرك عليها قادر ، وبسبب كونه قادراً فارقَ حاله حالة المرتعد ؛ فاندفعت الإشكالات كلها. وحاصله : أنَّ القادر واسعُ القدرة ، فهو قادر على الاختراع للقدرة والمقدور معاً ، ولما كان اسم الخالق والمخترع مطلقاً على من أوجد الشيء بقدرته ، وكانت القدرة والمقدور جميعاً بقدرة الله تعالى.. سمي خالقاً. لا نسمي العبد خالقاً ومخترعاً ، ولم يكن المقدورُ بقدرة العبد وإن كان معها .. فلم يسم خالقاً ومخترعاً وإنما الكسب فقط. ووجب أن يُطلب لهذا النمط من النسبة اسم آخر مخالف ، فطلب له اسم الكسب ؛ تيمناً بكتاب الله تعالى (٢) ، فإنه وجد إطلاق ذلك على أعمال العباد في القرآن ، وأما اسم الفعل .. فترددوا في إطلاقه ولا مشاحة في الأسامي بعد فهم المعاني
And if one supposes that the thing capable of God Almighty is preferred because His power is stronger... then it is impossible; because the attachment of power to a single movement is not superior to the attachment of another power to it, since the benefit of the two powers is creation (ikhtirāʿ), and their strength lies in their ability over something else, and their ability over something else does not give preference in the movement under discussion; since the share of the movement from each of the two powers is that it becomes created by it, and creation is equal, there is no stronger or weaker in it such that there would be preference. So the decisive proof for establishing the two powers leads us to establish a thing capable (maqdūr) between two powers. If it is said: The proof does not lead to something impossible that is not understood, and what you have mentioned—a thing capable between two powers—is not understood? We say: We must make it understood; and it is that we say: God Almighty's creation (ikhtirāʿ) of the movement in the hand of the servant is conceivable without the movement being capable (maqdūr) for the servant. So whenever He creates the movement and creates with it a power over it... He is the sole possessor of creation of the power and the capable thing together, and it follows from that: that He is unique in creation, and that the movement exists, and that the mover is capable over it, and because he is capable he differs from the state of the trembler; so all the problems are repelled. The summary: that the capable one is vast in power, so He is capable of creating the power and the capable thing together. And since the name 'Creator' (al-Khāliq) and 'Inventor' (al-Mukhtariʿ) is applied to one who brings a thing into existence by his power, and the power and the capable thing are both by the power of God Almighty... He is called Creator. We do not call the servant a creator and inventor, and the capable thing is not by the power of the servant even if it is with it... so he is not called a creator and inventor, but only acquisition (al-kasb). And it became necessary to seek for this type of attribution another name, so the name 'acquisition' (kasb) was sought for it, in imitation of the Book of God Almighty, for its application to the deeds of servants was found in the Qur'an. As for the name 'act' (fiʿl), they hesitated in applying it. And there is no harm in names after the meanings are understood.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage presents a complete theological argument: a proof, an objection, an answer, and a terminological conclusion. It begins by refuting a false notion that God's power being stronger would make His maqdūr (thing capable) prevail over the servant's. Ghazālī argues that since both powers are for ikhtirāʿ (creation), and creation is equal, no preference arises. The decisive proof for two powers leads to the necessity of a single maqdūr between two qādīrs (powerful beings). An objection follows: this concept is incomprehensible. The answer clarifies: God can create both the movement and the servant's power simultaneously. The servant then has a power (qudra) over the movement, but God alone is its creator (mukhtariʿ). The servant is qādir (capable) and differs from an involuntary agent like a trembler. The summary states that God creates both the power and its object, so He alone is called Khāliq and Mukhtariʿ. The servant is not called khāliq because his power does not bring about the act independently; the act exists with his power but not by it. Hence the term kasb (acquisition) is used, taken from the Qur'an, and there is no dispute over names once meanings are understood.

خالق
مخترع

فعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the servant's power (qudra) makes him a partial creator or partner in creating the act.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies that kasb does not involve creating; the servant's power coexists with the act but does not originate it. Only God creates.

Checkpoint
Why does Ghazali say the servant is not called Khāliq or Mukhtariʿ?
A) Because the servant's power is not real.
B) Because the servant does not bring the act into existence by his power alone.
C) Because the servant's act is not associated with his power.
D) Because the servant's act is involuntary.

Key Takeaway
The servant's act is called kasb because it occurs with his power but is created by God alone; names are secondary to understanding.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you truly feel that God alone created that act, or do you secretly believe you brought it about by your own power? How would owning the kasb doctrine change your repentance?
?
You have power (qudra) from God to act, but does your daily life reflect that you are only an acquirer, not a creator? In what areas do you most resist this humility?
?
If names are secondary to meanings, what are you more attached to: the label of being righteous (good name) or the reality that your every act is only an acquisition from God? Pray on this: are you willing to let go of your self-image as a 'doer' to live as a humble 'acquire'?

Ghazālī's Defense of Power's Non-Causal Relation
Ghazālī defends power's non-causal relation using will and knowledge as analogies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazālī's argument that the relation of divine power to its object is not limited to causing it, but can exist as a prior capacity, supported by analogies with will and knowledge.

Core Idea
Power's relation does not equal causation; otherwise all relational attributes would collapse.

In this passage, Ghazālī responds to an objection that seems to challenge the very coherence of the concept of 'power' (qudra) if it does not cause its object. The objector insists that relation must be causal. Ghazālī cleverly turns this against the objector, showing that the same logic would undermine other attributes like will and knowledge. The discussion uses a reductio ad absurdum to defend the Ashʿarī position on human power.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Objection
Read the objection carefully: the opponent argues that a power without a causal relation to its capable thing is impossible, as it would be like knowledge without a known object. Notice the insistence that relation must be through effect and bringing into existence.
2
Identify the Response's Structure
Ghazālī's answer has two prongs: first, if relation were confined to causing occurrence, then will and knowledge would be invalid; second, consider power before the act or continuing after it—the objector would have to deny relation, which is impossible.
This shows the reductio strategy.
3
Analyze the Analogies
Examine how Ghazālī compares power to will and knowledge. Both are relational attributes (ṣifāt mutaʿalliqa) that have objects without necessarily causing them. This undermines the objector's narrow definition of relation.
Will is related to something it may not effect; knowledge is related to something that may not exist externally.
4
Reflect on the Theological Stakes
Consider why this matters: if power could only relate causally, then human power (qudra ḥāditha) could not be truly 'power' if it doesn't create its effect. Ghazālī's defense preserves the coherence of power as a real attribute without committing to human creation of acts.
This is central to the Ashʿarī doctrine of acquisition (kasb).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : الشأن في فهم المعنى ، وما ذكرتموه غير مفهوم ؛ فإن القدرة : فأي قدرة هي التي تثبتونها المخلوقة الحادثة إن لم يكن لها تعلُّق بالمقدور لم تفهم ؛ إذ قدرةٌ لا مقدور للعبد ولا حقيقة لها محال ، كعلم لا معلوم له ، وإن تعلقت به فلا يعقل تعلق القدرة بالمقدور إلا من حيث التأثير والإيجاد وحصول المقدور بها فالنسبة بين المقدور والقدرة نسبة المسبَّبِ إلى السبب ، وهو كونه به ، فإذا لم يكن به .. لم يكن بينهما علاقة ، فلم تكن قدرةٌ ؛ إذ كلُّ ما لا تعلق له .. فليس بقدرة ؛ إذ القدرة من الصفات المتعلقة ؟ قلنا : هي متعلقة ، وقولكم : التعلق مقصورٌ على الوقوع به يُبْطِلُ تعلق الإرادة والعلم ، وإن قلتم : إن تعلق القدرة مقصور على الوقوع بها فهو أيضاً باطل ؛ فإن القدرة عندكم إذا فُرضت تبقى ، وإذا فُرضت قبل الفعل .. فهل هي متعلقة أم لا ؟ فإن قلتم : لا .. فهو محال
If it is said: The matter is in understanding the meaning, and what you have mentioned is not understood. For the power: what is the power that you affirm—the created, originated one—if it has no relation (taʿalluq) to the capable thing, it is not understood; since a power with no capable thing for the servant and no reality is impossible, like knowledge with no known thing. And if it is related to it, the relation of power to the capable thing is not conceivable except in terms of effect (taʾthīr), bringing into existence (ījād), and the occurrence of the capable thing by it. So the relationship between the capable thing and power is the relationship of the caused (musabbab) to the cause (sabab), which is its being by it. So if it is not by it, there is no relation between them, and then it is not a power; since whatever has no relation is not a power—for power is among the relational attributes (ṣifāt mutaʿalliqa)? We say: It is related. And your statement: 'Relation is confined to occurrence by it' invalidates the relation of will (irāda) and knowledge (ʿilm). And if you say: 'The relation of power is confined to occurrence by it,' that is also false. For power, according to you, if it is supposed to remain, and if it is supposed before the act, is it related or not? If you say: No, then it is impossible.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer in the kalam style. The objector challenges the coherence of a power that is related to a maqdūr (thing capable) but does not cause it. He argues that a power without a maqdūr is impossible, and that relation must be of causation (taʾthīr and ījād). The reply uses a reductio: if relation were confined to causing occurrence, then other relational attributes like will (irāda) and knowledge (ʿilm) would be invalid, since they have objects that may not occur. Ghazālī questions: if power is supposed to continue after the act or exist before the act, is it still related? The opponent would have to say no, which is impossible because a power without any relation is inconceivable. Thus, the relation of power is not limited to causing the act; it can be a prior capacity. This defends the Ashʿarī position that the servant's power does not create but exists concurrently with the act, which God creates.

Key Terms
قدرة
تعلق
مقدور
إرادة
علم
تأثير
إيجاد
صفات متعلقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The relation (taʿalluq) of power to its object must be a causal relation where the power produces the effect.
Correction: Ghazālī shows that this would invalidate other clear relational attributes like will and knowledge, and that power can have a non-causal relation, such as a prior capacity.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazālī's answer, why is it false to say that the relation of power is confined to causing the occurrence of the act?
A) Because power can exist without any relation.
B) Because this would invalidate the relation of will (irāda) and knowledge (ʿilm) as well.
C) Because power is not a relational attribute.
D) Because the act does not occur at all.

Key Takeaway
Power's relation is not limited to causing the act; it is a real but non-causal attribute, as shown by analogy with will and knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray for something and it doesn't happen, do you doubt that God's power was 'related' to your request? How does Ghazālī's argument about will (you can will something that doesn't occur) help you trust in God's power even when outcomes don't match your desire?
?
In your daily life, do you tend to think of power only as the ability to produce results? Where in your relationship with Allah do you need to recognize that His attributes—like knowledge and will—are real even when their objects are not present or realized?
?
This lesson argues that a relation does not have to be causal. Can you identify an area where you have been expecting Allah to act in a certain way (like a cause-effect), but you now see that His power is related to you in a deeper, non-causal way? How will this change your reliance on Him?
