God's Single Knowledge of the Infinite
Ghazali clarifies that divine knowledge is one despite infinite objects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali argues that God's knowledge is single and unitary despite encompassing infinite possible objects, and why the example of numbers does not imply a limit.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is one, even when it knows infinite things.

In this passage, Ghazali addresses a central puzzle in Islamic theology: if God knows everything, including infinite possibilities, does that make His knowledge multiple? He uses the example of numbers to clarify that the oneness of divine knowledge is not compromised by the infinity of its objects.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the English translation to get the overall meaning.
Notice the strong opening claim about numbers.
2
Identify the example and its purpose
Ghazali uses the example of 'double-doubles of two' – an exponential increase – to show that the knowledge of such numbers is beyond enumeration. Ask yourself: why does he start with numbers?
3
Grasp the conclusion about God's knowledge
After the example, Ghazali asserts that God's knowledge is 'one' despite being connected to infinite known things. This is the main point: oneness of attribute.
The phrase 'كما سيأتي' indicates this will be elaborated later.
4
Understand the clarification on numbers
Ghazali clarifies that the example is about possibility, not the end of numbers, because numbers are imaginary and have no real existence. This prevents a misunderstanding that God's knowledge has a limit.
5
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider why this matters: if God's knowledge were multiple, it would imply composition in the divine essence. Ghazali is defending tawhid in the attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذن ; معرفةُ أضعاف أضعاف الاثنين - وهو عدد واحد ـ يخرج عن الحصر ، وكذلك كلُّ عَدٌ ، فكيف غير ذلك من النسب والتقديرات ؟!(۱) وهذا العلم - مع تعلقه بمعلومات لا نهاية لها - واحد كما سيأتي بيانه من بعد مع سائر الصفات (۱) والمراد من المثال بيانُ تعلق علمه سبحانه بالممكن مهما احتمل تعدده ، لا علم نهاية العدد ؛ إذ العدد معنى موهوم لا وجود له في ذاته أصلاً ، فلا يقال : هذا آخر الأعداد مثلاً .
Therefore, the knowledge of double-doubles of two – which is a single number – is beyond enumeration, and likewise every number, so how about other relations and estimations?! And this knowledge – despite its connection to infinite known things – is one, as will be clarified later along with the other attributes. (1) The intended meaning of the example is to clarify His – exalted – knowledge's connection to the possible, however many its possibilities, not the knowledge of the end of number; for number is an imaginary meaning with no existence in itself at all, so it is not said: this is the last of numbers, for example.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage is a proof and clarification regarding God's knowledge. Ghazali begins by stating that the knowledge of double-doubles of two (i.e., an exponential increase from two) is beyond enumeration, and that applies to every number, so how much more so for other relations and estimations. He then asserts that this knowledge (God's knowledge) is one, despite being connected to infinite known things, and promises further clarification later along with other attributes. The footnote clarifies that the example is meant to show God's knowledge's connection to the possible (al-mumkin) regardless of its multiplicity, not the knowledge of the end of number, because number is an imaginary notion without genuine existence, so one cannot speak of a last number.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's knowledge must be multiple because it knows many things.
Correction: Ghazali argues that God's knowledge is one despite the infinity of its objects; multiplicity of objects does not imply multiplicity in the attribute.

Checkpoint
What does Ghazali say about God's knowledge in relation to infinite objects?
A) It is multiple like its objects
B) It is one despite infinite objects
C) It is limited to finite knowledge
D) It is not connected to numbers

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge is one and indivisible, even as it encompasses infinite possible objects; the infinity of known things does not fragment the divine attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If God's knowledge is not fragmented by the multitude of things He knows, why do you allow the multitude of your cares to fragment your trust in Him? What is one care you will consciously surrender to Him today?
?
In what area of your life do you act as if God's knowledge is incomplete, needing your worry or planning to fill the gaps? What specific step will you take this week to live as if His knowledge is truly sufficient?

The Attribute of Life: God is Living
Ghazali defines and proves God's attribute of Life.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's definition of 'living' and his deductive proof that God, being Knowing and Powerful, is necessarily Living.

Core Idea
God's Life is proven from His Knowledge and Power; it means self-awareness and knowledge.

This passage introduces the attribute of life. Ghazali defines what it means for God to be Living, distinguishing it from bodily life, and provides a brief logical proof from the attributes of knowledge and power. The definition is crucial: life is not a physical property but the capacity for self-awareness and knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation to get the overall argument.
Notice the concise style; Ghazali assumes the reader knows he already proved knowledge and power.
2
Understand the definition of 'living'
Ghazali defines 'living' as 'that which is aware of itself and knows its essence and others'. This is the key conceptual move.
This definition avoids any physical or biological connotations.
3
Trace the argument: from knowing/powerful to living
Apply the definition: since God knows all things and is powerful over all things, He must be living. Ask yourself: why is this connection necessary?
The argument is deductive: if X knows and has power, then X must be self-aware, hence living.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصفة التالية الحياة ندعي : أنه تعالى حيّ ، وهذا معلوم بالضرورة ، ولم ينكره أحد فيمن اعترف بكونه عالماً قادراً ، فإنَّ كونَ العالم القادر حياً ضروري ؛ إذ لا نعني بالحي إلا ما يَشْعُرُ بنفسِهِ ، ويعلم ذاتَهُ وغيره ، والعالم بجميع المعلومات ، القادر على جميع المقدورات ، كيف لا يكون حياً ؟! وهذا واضح ، والنظر في صفة الحياة لا يطول
The next attribute: Life. We claim: He, exalted, is Living, and this is known necessarily, and no one who acknowledges that He is Knowing and Powerful has denied it; for it is necessary that the Knowing, Powerful is Living; since we mean by 'living' only what is aware of itself, and knows its essence and others. And the One who knows all known things, is Powerful over all possible things – how could He not be Living?! This is clear, and the investigation of the attribute of Life is not lengthy.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage defines and proves the attribute of life (al-hayah). Ghazali first presents the claim: God is Living, and states that this is known necessarily and universally accepted among those who affirm God's knowledge and power. He then clarifies the definition (ما نعني بالحي) – we mean by 'living' that which is aware of itself (يشعر بنفسه) and knows its own essence and others (يعلم ذاته وغيره). Then he applies this definition: the One who knows all known things and has power over all possible things, how could He not be living? This is a deductive argument: if A (knowing and powerful) then B (living). He concludes that the point is clear and the investigation of this attribute is not lengthy.

حي (living)
عالم (knowing)

Correction: Ghazali defines 'living' as self-awareness and knowledge, not as bodily functions. Thus God is Living without a body.

Checkpoint

B) That which is aware of itself and knows its essence and others

God's Life is a necessary consequence of His Knowledge and Power, and is understood as perfect self-awareness.

The Attribute of Life: Necessarily Known from Knowledge and Power
Ghazali defines God's life and proves it from knowledge and power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali proves God's attribute of life as a necessary implication of His knowledge and power, and the specific meaning of 'living' in the divine context.

Core Idea
God's life follows necessarily from His knowledge and power.

In this short but decisive passage, Ghazali establishes the divine attribute of life (al-ḥayāt) as a necessary consequence of God's knowledge and power. He argues that anyone who accepts that God is knowing and powerful must also accept that He is living, because the very meaning of 'living' is self-conscious knowing. This lesson unpacks that concise argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the overall claim. Then read the literal translation to understand the meaning.
2
Identify the main claim
Ghazali begins with the claim: 'He, exalted, is living.' Notice that he says this is known necessarily (ḍarūra) and not denied by those who acknowledge God's knowledge and power.
3
Understand the proof
The proof is that a knowing and powerful being must be living. The definition of 'living' is key: it means one who is conscious of himself and knows himself and others.
4
Apply to God
Since God is knowing of all known things and powerful over all possible things, He must be living. The rhetorical question emphasizes this.
The conclusion is that the inquiry into life is brief because it is so clear.
5
Reflect on the misconception
Note that 'living' here does not imply a body; it is defined purely as self-awareness and knowledge. This guards against anthropomorphism.

Why God Must Have Will: Refuting al-Ka'bi's Sufficiency of Knowledge
Ghazali proves God has will and refutes al-Ka'bi's sufficiency of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that God's knowledge alone cannot explain why one possible action occurs over another, and why will is necessary as the preponderator.

Core Idea
Knowledge follows reality; will determines it.

In this passage, Ghazali establishes the divine attribute of will by arguing that neither God's essence, power, nor knowledge can serve as the preponderator that specifies one possible action over another. He directly refutes al-Ka'bi's claim that knowledge alone suffices, showing that knowledge is passive and cannot break the symmetry of equal possibilities. Only will can do so.

Guided Path
1
Read the claim and proof structure
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the overall claim: God is willing, and the proof is based on the need for a preponderator.
Notice how Ghazali announces his thesis immediately.
2
Identify why essence, power, and knowledge are insufficient
Follow the argument as Ghazali eliminates each candidate for preponderator: essence (relation to opposites is equal), power (same relation), and knowledge (follows the known, cannot affect it).
Focus on the phrase 'نسبة الذات إلى الضدين واحد' — this is key.
3
Apply the argument to the existence of the world
See how the general proof is applied: all possible times for the world's existence are equal in possibility, so knowledge alone cannot specify the actual time. Only will can explain why the world occurred when it did.
This shows the practical theological importance of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ندعي : أنَّ الله تعالى مريد لأفعاله وبرهانه : أنَّ الفعل الصادر منه مختص بضروب من الجواز ، لا يتميز بعضُها عن البعض إلا بمرجُح ، ولا تكفي ذاته للترجيح ؛ لأن نسبة الذات إلى الضدين واحد ، فما الذي خصَّصَ أحد الضدين بالوقوع في حال دون حال ؟! وكذلك القدرة لا تكفي فيه ؛ إذ نسبةُ القدرة إلى الضدين واحد ، وكذلك العلم لا يكفي ؛ خلافاً للكعبي ، حيث اكتفى بالعلم عن الإرادة ؛ لأن العلم يتبع المعلوم ويتعلق به على ما هو عليه ، ولا يؤثر فيه ولا يغيره . فإن كان الشيء ممكناً في نفسه ، مساوياً للممكن الآخر الذي في مقابلته .. فالعلم يتعلَّق به كما هو عليه ، فلا نجعل أحد الممكنين مرجحاً على الآخر ، بل نعقل الممكنين ونعقلُ تساويهما والله تعالى يعلمُ أنّ وجودَ العالم في الوقت الذي وجد فيه .. كان ممكناً ، وأنَّ وجودَهُ بعد ذلك وقبل ذلك .. كان مساوياً له في الإمكان ؛ لأنَّ هذه الإمكانات متساوية ، فحقُ العلم أن يتعلق بها كما هي عليه ؛ فإن اقتضت صفة الإرادة وقوعَهُ في وقت معين.. تعلَّق العلم بتعين وجودِهِ في (۱) ذهب أبو القاسم الكعبي إلى أن الباري لا يتصف بكونه مريداً على الحقيقة وإن وُصِفَ بذلك شرعاً في أفعاله ، فالمراد بكونه مريداً لها : أنه خالقها ومنشئها ، وقوله هذا كقول الفلاسفة ، وأما أبو الحسين النجار .. فقد ذهب إلى أن الباري تعالى مريد لنفسه ، وقد رد القاضي عبد الجبار هذا القول حين قال : ( قال شيخانا أبو علي وأبو هاشم رحمهما الله وسائر من تبعهما : إنه تعالى مريد في الحقيقة وإنه يحصل مريداً بعد ما لم يكن ... ) المغني ٢ (٣/٢/٦ ، ۱۰۳ ) ، وانظر الرد عليهما في ( الإرشاد ) ( ص ٦٤ ) .
We claim: God, exalted, is willing (murīd) for His actions. Its proof: that the action proceeding from Him is specified with types of possibility, some are not distinguished from others except by a preponderator (murajjiḥ). His essence alone is not sufficient for preponderance, because the relation of the essence to the two opposites is one. So what specifies one of the two opposites to occur in one state rather than another?! Similarly, power (qudra) is not sufficient for it, because the relation of power to the two opposites is one. Likewise, knowledge (ʿilm) is not sufficient, contrary to al-Kaʿbī, who sufficed with knowledge instead of will (irāda), because knowledge follows the known and attaches to it as it is, and does not affect it or change it. For if a thing is possible in itself, equal to the other possible opposite... then knowledge attaches to it as it is, so we do not make one of the two possibles preponderant over the other. Rather we understand the two possibles and understand their equalness. And God, exalted, knows that the existence of the world at the time it occurred was possible, and that its existence after that and before that was equal to it in possibility, because these possibilities are equal. So it is the right of knowledge to attach to them as they are. For if the attribute of will necessitates its occurrence at a specific time... knowledge attaches to its determined existence at... (1) Abu al-Qasim al-Kaʿbī held that the Creator is not described as being willing in reality, although He is described as such legally in His actions, meaning by His being willing for them: that He is their creator and originator. This statement is like the statement of the philosophers. As for Abu al-Husayn al-Najjār... he held that the Creator, exalted, is willing in Himself. Al-Qāḍī ʿAbd al-Jabbār refuted this statement when he said: 'Our two teachers Abū ʿAlī and Abū Hāshim, may God have mercy on them, and all who followed them said: He, exalted, is willing in reality and He becomes willing after He was not...' al-Mughnī 2 (3/2/6, 103), and see the refutation of both in al-Irshād (p. 64).
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof of the attribute of Will (al-irāda) for God, along with a refutation of al-Kaʿbī's view. It begins with the claim that God is willing for His actions. The proof is based on the need for a preponderator (murajjiḥ) to specify one possible action over another. It argues that neither God's essence alone, nor His power, nor His knowledge is sufficient to serve as that preponderator. The refutation of al-Kaʿbī is that knowledge only follows the known as it is and cannot change it, so it cannot specify one possibility over an equal counterpart. Therefore, will is necessary. The argument then applies this to the existence of the world at a specific time: since all times are equally possible, knowledge alone cannot determine why the world occurred when it did; only will can specify. The passage ends with an editorial footnote summarizing various positions.

Key Terms
إرادة
مريد
مرجح
جواز
كعبي
علم
قدرة
ضدين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Ka'bi's view that God's knowledge alone is sufficient to determine which possible action occurs, making will redundant.
Correction: Knowledge only follows the known as it is; it cannot change or preponderate among equally possible opposites. Will is required to specify one possibility over another.

Checkpoint

A) Because knowledge is not an attribute of God
B) Because knowledge follows the known and does not affect it, so it cannot make one possible preponderant over an equal
C) Because knowledge is limited to what has already happened
D) Because knowledge changes the known, making it impossible to have equal possibilities

Key Takeaway
God's will is necessary to preponderate among equally possible actions; knowledge alone cannot do so because it passively reflects reality rather than determining it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a choice between two equally possible options, do you rely on your knowledge alone to decide, or do you actively will one over the other? What does your reliance on will (or lack thereof) reveal about your understanding of God's nature?
?
God's will determined the exact moment of the world's creation. Are you content with God's timing in your life, or do you secretly wish certain events happened sooner or later? How would accepting His will as the ultimate preponderator change your patience?
?
Al-Ka'bī thought knowledge sufficed; Ghazali shows it does not. In your relationship with God, have you settled for knowing about Him without submitting your will to Him? What concrete step will you take today to move from knowledge to willing obedience?

The Necessity of a Specifier for Divine Actions
Ghazali proves the need for a specifying factor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that Allah's actions require a specifying factor (murajjih) beyond His essence and power.

Core Idea
Without a specifier, equal possibilities remain undifferentiated.

Ghazali begins his proof that Allah is willing by establishing that every divine action admits multiple possible states. He argues that neither the divine essence nor power alone can specify which possible state occurs, because both relate equally to opposites. This lesson explores the logical need for a specifier.

Guided Path
1
Read the claim and proof structure
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Ghazali states his claim and then provides the proof.
2
Identify why essence and power are insufficient
Focus on the lines about essence and power. Why does Ghazali say they cannot specify? Note the phrase 'نسبة الذات إلى الضدين واحد' (the relation of the essence to the two opposites is one).
3
Grasp the logical necessity of a murajjih
Ask yourself: if neither essence nor power suffices, what does? This sets up the need for will (irada).
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ندعي : أنَّ الله تعالى مريد لأفعاله وبرهانه : أنَّ الفعل الصادر منه مختص بضروب من الجواز ، لا يتميز بعضُها عن البعض إلا بمرجُح ، ولا تكفي ذاته للترجيح ؛ لأن نسبة الذات إلى الضدين واحد ، فما الذي خصَّصَ أحد الضدين بالوقوع في حال دون حال ؟! وكذلك القدرة لا تكفي فيه ؛ إذ نسبةُ القدرة إلى الضدين واحد ، وكذلك العلم لا يكفي ؛ خلافاً للكعبي ، حيث اكتفى بالعلم عن الإرادة ؛ لأن العلم يتبع المعلوم ويتعلق به على ما هو عليه ، ولا يؤثر فيه ولا يغيره . فإن كان الشيء ممكناً في نفسه ، مساوياً للممكن الآخر الذي في مقابلته .. فالعلم يتعلَّق به كما هو عليه ، فلا نجعل أحد الممكنين مرجحاً على الآخر ، بل نعقل الممكنين ونعقلُ تساويهما والله تعالى يعلمُ أنّ وجودَ العالم في الوقت الذي وجد فيه .. كان ممكناً ، وأنَّ وجودَهُ بعد ذلك وقبل ذلك .. كان مساوياً له في الإمكان ؛ لأنَّ هذه الإمكانات متساوية ، فحقُ العلم أن يتعلق بها كما هي عليه ؛ فإن اقتضت صفة الإرادة وقوعَهُ في وقت معين.. تعلَّق العلم بتعين وجودِهِ في ذلك الوقت لعلَّة تعلَّقِ الإرادة به ، فتكون الإرادة للتعيين علة ، ويكون العلم متعلقاً به تابعاً له غير مؤثر فيه ، ولو جاز أن يكتفى بالعلم عن الإرادة . . لاكتفي به عن القدرة ، بل كان ذلك يكفي في وجود أفعالنا ، حتى لا نحتاج إلى الإرادة ؛ إذ يترجح أحد الجانبين بتعلق علم الله تعالى به ، وذلك محال
We claim that Allah, exalted, is willing (murid) for His actions. Its proof is that the action issuing from Him is specified with types of possibility, and some of them are not distinguished from others except by a specifying factor (murajjih), and His essence is not sufficient for specification, because the relation of the essence to the two opposites is one. So what specifies one of the two opposites to occur in one state rather than another? Similarly, power (qudra) is not sufficient for it, since the relation of power to the two opposites is one. And similarly, knowledge (ilm) is not sufficient, contrary to al-Ka'bi, who sufficed with knowledge instead of will, because knowledge follows the known and relates to it as it is, and does not affect it or change it. If something is possible in itself, equal to the other possible opposite, then knowledge relates to it as it is, so we do not make one of the two possibles a specifying factor over the other. Rather, we conceive the two possibles and conceive their equality. And Allah knows that the existence of the world at the time it existed was possible, and that its existence before and after that was equal to it in possibility, because these possibilities are equal. So it is right for knowledge to relate to them as they are. But if the attribute of will (irada) necessitated its occurrence at a particular time, knowledge relates to the determination of its existence at that time because of the cause of the will's relation to it. Thus will is the cause of determination, and knowledge is related to it as a follower, not affecting it. And if it were permissible to suffice with knowledge instead of will, then it would be permissible to suffice with it instead of power, rather that would suffice for the existence of our actions, so we would not need will, since one side would be specified by the relation of Allah's knowledge to it. And that is impossible.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage is a proof that Allah is willing (murid) for His actions. Ghazali begins by stating the claim. Then he presents the proof: actions are specified with various possibilities, and they require a specifying factor (murajjih). Neither the divine essence, power, nor knowledge alone suffices. He refutes al-Ka'bi's view that knowledge is enough, because knowledge merely follows the known and does not affect or change it. If two possibilities are equal, knowledge will relate to them as equal, so it cannot specify one over the other. Will provides the specification. Ghazali illustrates with the example of the world's existence in a specific time. He concludes that if knowledge sufficed, it would also suffice for power, which is absurd.

Key Terms
مرجح (murajjih)
قدرة (qudra)
إرادة (irada)
ذات (dhat)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's essence or power alone determines which action occurs.
Correction: Ghazali shows that both essence and power relate equally to opposites, so they cannot break the symmetry.

Checkpoint
Why does Ghazali argue that the divine essence cannot specify between opposite possibilities?
A) Because the essence is eternal and unchanging.
B) Because the essence relates equally to all opposites.
C) Because the essence is not powerful enough.
D) Because the essence is unknowable.

Key Takeaway
A specifying factor (murajjih) beyond essence and power is necessary for any divine action to occur.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify one area of your life where you feel equally torn between two choices (e.g., a career decision, a relationship, a habit). If you believed that neither your own essence nor your own power can break that tie—only God's will—how would you actively seek His specification? Will you set aside 10 minutes today to sincerely ask Allah to show you the right path?
?
When you look back at a past decision that turned out well, did you attribute it to your own intelligence or strength? According to this lesson, those attributes alone cannot specify good outcomes. How can you cultivate a daily habit of thanking Allah for His specifying will, even in small choices?
?
If God's will is the ultimate specifier, then every moment of obedience or disobedience in your life has been determined by His will—not just your own effort. Does this truth humble you or unsettle you? What would it mean for you to surrender your 'power' and 'essence' to His specification starting tomorrow? Commit to one concrete act of reliance on His will today.

Knowledge Alone Cannot Replace Will
Ghazali refutes al-Ka'bi's sufficiency of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that divine knowledge, being passive and following the known, cannot serve as the specifying factor that will provides.

Core Idea
Knowledge follows the known; it does not affect or change it.

Having established the need for a specifier, Ghazali now addresses the objection of al-Ka'bi, who claimed that God's knowledge alone is sufficient to specify actions. Ghazali argues that knowledge is merely a reflection of reality and cannot break the symmetry of equal possibilities. This lesson examines the refutation and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation of al-Ka'bi
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Notice that Ghazali begins by stating that knowledge is not sufficient, contrary to al-Ka'bi.
2
Understand why knowledge is passive
Focus on the phrase 'العلم يتبع المعلوم ويتعلق به على ما هو عليه' (knowledge follows the known and relates to it as it is). Why does this make knowledge unable to specify?
3
Apply the example of the world's existence
Ghazali uses the example of the world's creation: its existence at any time is equally possible. How does knowledge relate to these equal possibilities?
4
See the absurd consequence if knowledge sufficed
Notice the reductio ad absurdum: if knowledge sufficed, it would also suffice for power, making will unnecessary. Why is that impossible?
This completes the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ندعي : أنَّ الله تعالى مريد لأفعاله وبرهانه : أنَّ الفعل الصادر منه مختص بضروب من الجواز ، لا يتميز بعضُها عن البعض إلا بمرجُح ، ولا تكفي ذاته للترجيح ؛ لأن نسبة الذات إلى الضدين واحد ، فما الذي خصَّصَ أحد الضدين بالوقوع في حال دون حال ؟! وكذلك القدرة لا تكفي فيه ؛ إذ نسبةُ القدرة إلى الضدين واحد ، وكذلك العلم لا يكفي ؛ خلافاً للكعبي ، حيث اكتفى بالعلم عن الإرادة ؛ لأن العلم يتبع المعلوم ويتعلق به على ما هو عليه ، ولا يؤثر فيه ولا يغيره . فإن كان الشيء ممكناً في نفسه ، مساوياً للممكن الآخر الذي في مقابلته .. فالعلم يتعلَّق به كما هو عليه ، فلا نجعل أحد الممكنين مرجحاً على الآخر ، بل نعقل الممكنين ونعقلُ تساويهما والله تعالى يعلمُ أنّ وجودَ العالم في الوقت الذي وجد فيه .. كان ممكناً ، وأنَّ وجودَهُ بعد ذلك وقبل ذلك .. كان مساوياً له في الإمكان ؛ لأنَّ هذه الإمكانات متساوية ، فحقُ العلم أن يتعلق بها كما هي عليه ؛ فإن اقتضت صفة الإرادة وقوعَهُ في وقت معين.. تعلَّق العلم بتعين وجودِهِ في ذلك الوقت لعلَّة تعلَّقِ الإرادة به ، فتكون الإرادة للتعيين علة ، ويكون العلم متعلقاً به تابعاً له غير مؤثر فيه ، ولو جاز أن يكتفى بالعلم عن الإرادة . . لاكتفي به عن القدرة ، بل كان ذلك يكفي في وجود أفعالنا ، حتى لا نحتاج إلى الإرادة ؛ إذ يترجح أحد الجانبين بتعلق علم الله تعالى به ، وذلك محال
We claim that Allah, exalted, is willing (murid) for His actions. Its proof is that the action issuing from Him is specified with types of possibility, and some of them are not distinguished from others except by a specifying factor (murajjih), and His essence is not sufficient for specification, because the relation of the essence to the two opposites is one. So what specifies one of the two opposites to occur in one state rather than another? Similarly, power (qudra) is not sufficient for it, since the relation of power to the two opposites is one. And similarly, knowledge (ilm) is not sufficient, contrary to al-Ka'bi, who sufficed with knowledge instead of will, because knowledge follows the known and relates to it as it is, and does not affect it or change it. If something is possible in itself, equal to the other possible opposite, then knowledge relates to it as it is, so we do not make one of the two possibles a specifying factor over the other. Rather, we conceive the two possibles and conceive their equality. And Allah knows that the existence of the world at the time it existed was possible, and that its existence before and after that was equal to it in possibility, because these possibilities are equal. So it is right for knowledge to relate to them as they are. But if the attribute of will (irada) necessitated its occurrence at a particular time, knowledge relates to the determination of its existence at that time because of the cause of the will's relation to it. Thus will is the cause of determination, and knowledge is related to it as a follower, not affecting it. And if it were permissible to suffice with knowledge instead of will, then it would be permissible to suffice with it instead of power, rather that would suffice for the existence of our actions, so we would not need will, since one side would be specified by the relation of Allah's knowledge to it. And that is impossible.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage is a proof that Allah is willing (murid) for His actions. Ghazali begins by stating the claim. Then he presents the proof: actions are specified with various possibilities, and they require a specifying factor (murajjih). Neither the divine essence, power, nor knowledge alone suffices. He refutes al-Ka'bi's view that knowledge is enough, because knowledge merely follows the known and does not affect or change it. If two possibilities are equal, knowledge will relate to them as equal, so it cannot specify one over the other. Will provides the specification. Ghazali illustrates with the example of the world's existence in a specific time. He concludes that if knowledge sufficed, it would also suffice for power, which is absurd.

Key Terms
علم (ilm)
إرادة (irada)
الكعبي (al-Ka'bi)
معلوم (ma'lum)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's knowledge actively determines which events occur.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies that knowledge is passive: it only knows what will is, not causes the determination.

Checkpoint
Why does Ghazali reject al-Ka'bi's view that knowledge suffices for specifying possibilities?
A) Because knowledge is created and temporal.
B) Because knowledge follows the known and does not affect it.
C) Because knowledge is limited to what already exists.
D) Because knowledge is not an attribute of Allah.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge alone cannot specify between equal possibilities; will is the active specifier that determines which possibility occurs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that some actions are good and some are harmful, yet you often fail to choose the good. What does this lesson reveal about the gap between your knowledge and your will? In what specific area of your life are you relying solely on your knowledge to change, without actually engaging your will to decide and act?
?
Ghazali argues that knowledge alone cannot specify between equal possibilities. Think of a situation in your life where you have two equally good options. You know both thoroughly, yet you remain stuck. How does this passage expose the insufficiency of your knowledge and call you to activate your will? What is one choice you have been postponing that now requires a decisive act of will?
?
If you truly believe that God's knowledge does not force action but His will does, how should this reshape your understanding of dua (supplication)? Are you asking God only for knowledge and guidance, or are you seeking the gift of His will to move you? What is one prayer you need to change today?

The Objection: Will Also Needs a Specifier
An objection that will itself requires a specifying factor, leading to infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the eternal will, like power, stands in need of a specifying factor, and how this threatens an infinite regress.

Core Idea
If will is symmetrical like power, it too needs a specifier—leading to an endless chain.

This passage presents a powerful objection against the earlier argument that power requires will as its specifying factor. The objector turns the reasoning inward, arguing that the divine will itself stands equally in need of a specifying factor, since it relates to opposites and times symmetrically. The challenge culminates in an infinite regress, pressing the theologian for an answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the structure of the objection. Notice the symmetry between power and will.
The passage is a continuous objection ending with a question.
2
Identify the analogical argument
The objector draws an analogy between qudra (power) and irada (will). Both are general in attachment and relate equally to opposites and times. Therefore, what is said of one applies to the other.
3
Trace the regress chain
The objection builds a chain: essence needs power, power needs will, will needs another specifying factor, and so on. Identify each link and note how it forces an infinite regress.
The chain is: essence → power → will → will? ...
4
Understand the rhetorical punch
The passage ends with 'So what is your answer?' This forces the theologian to respond. Consider why this is a serious challenge and how an answer might break the regress.
The answer is not in this chunk, so this lesson only presents the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: وهذا ينقلب عليكم في نفس الإرادة؛ فإن القدرة كما لا تناسب أحد الضدين.. فالإرادة القديمة أيضاً لا تتعين لأحد الضدين، لمخصص لتعين المقدور وزمنه.. فاختصاصها بأحد الضدين ينبغي أن يكون بمخصص، ويتسلسل ذلك إلى غير نهاية؛ إذ يقال: الذاتُ لا تكفي للحدوث؛ إذ لو حدث من الذات.. لكان مع الذات غير متأخر، فلا بد من قدرة، والقدرة لا تكفي؛ إذ لو كان للقدرة.. لما اختص بهذا الوقت وما قبله وما بعده في النسبة إلى جواز تعلقِ القدرة به على وتيرة واحدة، فما الذي خصَّصَ هذا الوقت؛ فيحتاج إلى الإرادة، فيقال: و الإرادة لا تكفي؛ فإن الإرادة القديمة عامة التعلقِ كالقدرة، فنسبتها إلى الأوقات واحدةٌ، ونسبتها إلى الضدين واحدةٌ، فإن وقعت الحركة مثلاً بدلاً عن السكون؛ لأن الإرادة تعلقت بالحركة لا بالسكون.. فيقال: وهل كان يمكن أن تتعلق بالسكون؟ فإن قيل: لا فهو محال، وإن قيل: نعم.. فهما متساويان؛ أعني: الحركة والسكون في مناسبة الإرادة القديمة، فما الذي أوجب تخصيص الإرادة القديمة بالحركة دون السكون؛ فيحتاج إلى مخصص، ثم مثلها، وهذا مفضي للمتسلسل، فما جوابكم؟
For it is said: This same argument applies against you concerning the will itself. For just as power (qudra) does not suit one of the two opposites, so the eternal will (al-irada al-qadima) also is not determined for one of the two opposites without a specifying factor for the determination of the object of power and its time. Therefore, its specification to one of the two opposites should be by a specifying factor, and this would regress to infinity. For it is said: The essence is not sufficient for origination, for if it originated from the essence, it would be with the essence not delayed, so power is necessary. And power is not sufficient, for if it were for power, it would not be specified for this time and what is before and after in terms of the relation of power's attachment to it on one mode. So what specifies this time? Then will is needed. And it is said: And will is not sufficient, for the eternal will is general in attachment like power, so its relation to times is one, and its relation to the two opposites is one. If movement occurred instead of rest because the will was attached to movement not rest, it is said: Could it have been attached to rest? If it is said no, that is impossible. And if it is said yes, then they are equal — I mean movement and rest in suitability for the eternal will. So what necessitated the specification of the eternal will to movement rather than rest? Then it needs a specifying factor. And then similar. And this leads to an infinite regress. So what is your answer?
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage is an objection against the previous proof. The objector argues that the same reasoning that required a specifying factor for power also applies to will itself. Since the eternal will is general and relates equally to opposites and times, it also needs a specifying factor to determine it. This leads to an infinite regress of specifying factors. The objection elaborates through a chain: essence needs power, power needs will, will needs another will, etc. The passage ends with the question 'So what is your answer?' indicating that the author will respond.

تعيين
تسلسل
مخصص
القدرة

Checkpoint
According to the objection, why does the eternal will itself need a specifying factor?
A) Because the will is not eternal.
B) Because the will relates equally to opposites and times.
C) Because the will is created.
D) Because the will is identical to power.

Key Takeaway
The objection reveals that if will is symmetric like power, it too demands a specifier, threatening an infinite regress that must be resolved by a deeper theological point.

Objection of Infinite Regress and the Four Views on Will
Ghazali presents an objection demanding a mukhassis for divine will and answers by classifying four schools.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection of infinite regress against divine will and how Ghazali resolves it by distinguishing four sectarian positions.

Core Idea
The eternal will needs no specifier—only Ahl al-Sunna saw this clearly.

Ghazali confronts a powerful objection: if divine will specifies one event, who specifies the will? This leads to an infinite regress that perplexed all schools. He answers by categorizing four views on how the world relates to God's will, showing that only the Sunni position escapes the regress.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the flow of the objection. Then follow the English translation. Notice how the argument progresses from power to will, demanding a specifier at each step.
The ellipsis (...) indicate missing text; focus on the logical structure.
2
Identify the infinite regress
The objection claims that the eternal will itself stands in need of a specifier to choose one opposite over another, leading to an endless chain. Ask yourself: why would this be a problem for any view that posits multiple conditions?
3
Study the four schools
Ghazali lists four groups: Philosophers (eternal world), Mu'tazila (originated will not in a locus), Karrāmiyya (originated will in God's essence), and Ahl al-Sunna (eternal will without change). Note how each defines the role of will.
Relate each view to the regress objection.
4
Understand why Ahl al-Sunna's view resolves it
The Sunni position denies that a new specification is needed because the eternal will is simple and timelessly attaches to its object. This avoids the regress entirely. Reflect on how this contrasts with the other views.
This is the key takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذلك الوقت لعلة تعلق الإرادة به، فتكون الإرادة للتعيين علة، ويكون العلم متعلقاً به تابعاً له غير مؤثر فيه، ولو جاز أن يكتفى بالعلم عن الإرادة... لاكتفي به عن القدرة، بل كان ذلك يكفي في وجود أفعالنا، حتى لا نحتاج إلى الإرادة؛ إذ يترجح أحد الجانبين بتعلق علم الله تعالى به، محال وذلك

فإن قيل: وهذا ينقلب عليكم في نفس الإرادة؛ فإن القدرة كما لا تناسب أحد الضدين... فالإرادة القديمة أيضاً لا تتعين لأحد الضدين، لمخصص لتعين المقدور وزمنه. فاختصاصها بأحد الضدين ينبغي أن يكون بمخصص، ويتسلسل ذلك إلى غير نهاية؛ إذ يقال: الذات لا تكفي للحدوث؛ إذ لو حدث من الذات... لكان مع الذات غير متأخر، فلا بد من قدرة، والقدرة لا تكفي؛ إذ لو كان للقدرة... لما اختص بهذا الوقت وما قبله وما بعده في النسبة إلى جواز تعلق القدرة به على وتيرة واحدة، فما الذي خصص هذا الوقت؛ فيحتاج إلى الإرادة، فيقال: والإرادة لا تكفي؛ فإن الإرادة القديمة عامة التعلق كالقدرة، فنسبتها إلى الأوقات واحدة، ونسبتها إلى الضدين واحدة، فإن وقعت الحركة مثلاً بدلاً عن السكون؛ لأن الإرادة تعلقت بالحركة لا بالسكون... فيقال: وهل كان يمكن أن تتعلق بالسكون؟

فإن قيل: لا فهو محال، وإن قيل: نعم... فهما متساويان؛ أعني: الحركة والسكون في مناسبة الإرادة القديمة، فما الذي أوجب تخصيص الإرادة القديمة بالحركة دون السكون؛ فيحتاج إلى مخصص، ثم مثلها، وهذا مفضي للمتسلسل، فما جوابكم؟

قلنا: هذا سؤال حير عقول الفرق، ولم يوفق للحق إلا أهل السنة

الناس في الإرادة أربع فرق:
- قائل يقول: إن العالم يوجد بذات الله سبحانه وتعالى، وأنه ليس للذات صفة زائدة البتة، ولما كانت الذات قديمة... كان العالم قديماً وكانت نسبة العالم إليه كنسبة المعلول إلى العلة، ونسبة النور إلى عين الشمس والظل إلى الشخص، وهؤلاء هم الفلاسفة
- وقائل يقول: إن العالم حادث، ولكن حدث في الوقت الذي حدث فيه لا قبله ولا بعده؛ لإرادة حادثة لا في محل، فاقتضت حدوث العالم، وهؤلاء هم المعتزلة
- وقائل يقول: حدث بإرادة حادثة في ذاته، وهؤلاء هم القائلون بكونه محلاً للحوادث.
- وقائل يقول: حدث العالم في الوقت الذي تعلقت الإرادة القديمة بحدوثه في ذلك الوقت من غير حدوث إرادة ومن غير تغير صفة القديم

فانظر إلى الفرق وأنسب مقال كل واحد إلى الآخر؛ فإنه لا ينفك كل فريق عن إشكال لا يمكن حله إلا إشكال أهل السنة؛ فإنه سهل الانحلال
That time [is specified] due to the cause of the will's attachment to it, so the will is the cause for specification, and knowledge is attached to it [the specification] as a follower, not effective in it. And if it were permissible to be content with knowledge instead of will... one would be content with it instead of power; rather that would suffice for the existence of our actions, so that we would not need will; since one of the two sides is preferred by the attachment of the knowledge of Allah Almighty to it—impossible, and that [is invalid].

If it is said: And this turns against you regarding the will itself; for just as power does not fit one of the two opposites... so also the eternal will is not specified for one of the two opposites without a specifier for the determination of the determined thing and its time. So its specification to one of the two opposites must be by a specifier, and this leads to infinite regress; for it is said: The essence is not sufficient for origination; for if origination came from the essence... it would be with the essence not delayed, so power is necessary; and power is not sufficient; for if power [were sufficient]... it would not be specified for this time and what is before and after it in relation to the permissibility of power's attachment to it on one level; so what specified this time? So will is needed; then it is said: And will is not sufficient; for the eternal will is general in attachment like power, so its relation to times is one, and its relation to the two opposites is one. If movement occurs instead of stillness, because the will is attached to movement not to stillness... it is said: Could it have been attached to stillness?

If it is said: No, that is impossible; and if it is said: Yes... then they are equal—i.e., movement and stillness in suitability for the eternal will; so what necessitated the specification of the eternal will to movement rather than stillness? So a specifier is needed; and likewise with others; and this leads to the infinite regress. So what is your answer?

We said: This is a question that perplexed the minds of the sects, and none were guided to the truth except Ahl al-Sunna.

People regarding [the question of] will are four sects:
- One says: The world exists by the essence of Allah Almighty, and that the essence has no additional attribute at all; and since the essence is eternal... the world is eternal, and the relation of the world to Him is like the relation of the effect to the cause, and the relation of light to the sun and shadow to the person. These are the philosophers.
- And one says: The world is originated, but it occurred at the time it did, not before or after, due to an originated will not in a locus, which necessitated the origination of the world. These are the Mu'tazila.
- And one says: It occurred by an originated will in His essence. These are those who hold that He is a locus for originated events.
- And one says: The world occurred at the time that the eternal will attached to its occurrence at that time, without the occurrence of a will and without change in the attribute of the Eternal.

So consider the sects and relate the statement of each to the other; for no group is free from a problem that cannot be solved except the problem of Ahl al-Sunna; for it is easy to resolve.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage presents a sophisticated objection against the Ash'ari explanation of how the eternal divine will specifies one temporal event over another, leading to an infinite regress. Ghazali then answers by classifying the opinions of four groups on the relation of God's will to the world's creation.

The objection runs as follows: If will is needed to specify what power and knowledge alone cannot, then the eternal will itself stands in need of another specifier, since it is equally related to two opposites (e.g., motion vs. rest, or one time vs. another). This demand for a specifier can be repeated indefinitely—hence infinite regress. 

Ghazali answers that this question has perplexed all groups except Ahl al-Sunna. He then enumerates four schools of thought on how the world came to be:
1) Philosophers: The world exists necessarily from God's essence, like light from the sun—hence it is eternal.
2) Mu'tazila: The world is originated by an originated will not in a locus (i.e., a created will in no substrate).
3) Anthropomorphists (Karrāmiyya): The world is created by an originated will that occurs in God's essence, making Him a locus for temporal events.
4) Ahl al-Sunna: The world occurs at the moment specified by the eternal will without any new will or change in God's attributes.

Ghazali concludes that only Ahl al-Sunna's position—positing a single eternal will without temporal origination—avoids the infinite regress and is easy to resolve. Each other view has an insurmountable problem.

Key Terms
الإرادة
القدرة
المخصص
التسلسل
المعتزلة
الفلاسفة
الكرامية
أهل السنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection of infinite regress challenges the very need for a Creator or divine will.
Correction: The objection specifically targets how an eternal will can specify a temporal event without requiring a further specifier; it does not deny the need for a Creator at all.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, which group avoids infinite regress by positing an eternal will without a new will or modification?
A) Philosophers
B) Mu'tazila
C) Those who say will occurs in God's essence (Karrāmiyya)
D) Ahl al-Sunna

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's taxonomy of four views on divine will shows that only the Ash'ari (Sunni) position avoids infinite regress by positing a single eternal will without temporal origination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosophers, Mu'tazila, and Karrāmiyya all offered explanations for God's will that seemed logical to them, yet they reached dead ends. When you try to reason about God's attributes on your own, without submitting to the guidance of Ahl al-Sunna, what logical pitfalls do you fear falling into? Will you now commit to trusting the Sunni creed even when your intellect feels perplexed?
?
Ghazali says the question of how an eternal will specifies time 'perplexed the minds of the sects.' Has there been a question about God's actions or decrees that has perplexed you and shaken your certainty? Are you willing to accept the 'easy resolution' of Ahl al-Sunna, which demands you recognize the limits of your own reasoning?
?
The objection that the will needs a specifier seems powerful—yet Ahl al-Sunna see it as easily resolved. How often do you let the seeming contradictions in divine attributes (like will and power) trouble your heart, rather than resting in the simple truth that God's attributes are perfect and beyond full human comprehension? What step will you take today to surrender such doubts to the Sunni scholars?

5 min
First Refutation of the Mu'tazila: Will Not in a Locus
Ghazali refutes the Mu'tazila claim of a will not in a locus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that a will not subsisting in a subject cannot be attributed to God, and why he considers the Mu'tazilite position incoherent.

Core Idea
A will not in a locus is nonsense — attribution requires real inherence.

In this passage, Ghazali tackles one of the most contested issues in kalam: the nature of God's will. The Mu'tazila claimed that God wills by an originated will that does not subsist in Him. Ghazali counters that such a claim is not just false but linguistically incoherent, because if the will does not inhere in God, then saying 'He wills by it' is like saying He wills by someone else's will. This lesson unpacks that powerful argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the literal translation. Let Ghazali's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how Ghazali begins by branding the Mu'tazila claims as 'ugly and false'.
2
Identify the two 'ugly matters'
The passage mentions two matters. Focus on the first one: that God is willing by an originated will not in a locus. Identify how Ghazali describes it.
The second matter is not detailed here but will be covered later.
3
Analyze Ghazali's reduction to absurdity
Ghazali argues that if the will is not subsisting in God, then saying 'He is willing by it' is like saying 'He is willing by a will subsisting in another.' This is the core of his refutation. Understand why he considers this 'nonsense of speech'.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum argument.
4
Reflect on the linguistic argument
Consider why Ghazali uses the phrase 'hajr min al-kalam' (nonsense of speech). It indicates that the claim is not just false but incoherent in its very formulation.
This ties to the broader kalam principle that attributes must inhere in a subject.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرد على المعتزلة وأما المعتزلة: فقد اقتحموا أمرين شنيعين باطلين: أحدهما: كون الباري تعالى مريداً بإرادة حادثة لا في محل، وإذا لم تكن الإرادة قائمة به.. فقول القائل: إنه مريد بها.. هجر من الكلام؛ كقوله: إنه مريد بإرادة قائمة بغيره.
Refutation of the Mu'tazila: As for the Mu'tazila, they have embarked on two ugly and false matters: The first: that the Exalted Creator is willing by an originated will that is not in a locus. And if the will is not subsisting in Him, then the statement of the one who says 'He is willing by it' is nonsense of speech, like his statement 'He is willing by a will subsisting in another.'
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage begins Ghazali's refutation of two Mu'tazilite claims. The first claim is that God is willing by an originated (haditha) will that does not subsist in a locus (i.e., not in a subject). Ghazali argues that if the will does not subsist in God, then saying 'He is willing by it' is meaningless, because it would be like saying He is willing by a will that exists in someone else. This reduces the attribution to absurdity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A will can exist without a subject.
Correction: Ghazali argues that a will must subsist in a subject; otherwise, attributing it to God is meaningless.

Checkpoint
Why does Ghazali consider the Mu'tazilite claim of a will not in a locus as nonsense?
A) Because will cannot exist without a subject.
B) Because it would imply God is willing by something external to Him.
C) Both A and B.
D) Because the Mu'tazila do not believe in will.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali shows that the Mu'tazilite claim of a will not in a locus is logically incoherent and linguistically nonsensical, because an attribute must inhere in its subject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'God wills', do you truly mean that His will is real and part of His essence, or have you ever let your own imagination shape that phrase? This lesson shows that misattribution is not just wrong but nonsensical. Will you commit to learning the precise meaning of every attribute you ascribe to Him?
?
Ghazali calls the Mu'tazila claim 'ugly and false'. Are you willing to examine your own beliefs about God—no matter how comfortable—and set them aside if they don't stand up to rational and scriptural scrutiny? What one belief will you investigate this week?
?
If your description of God's will were put to the test of logical coherence, would it pass? Or have you unknowingly adopted a view that reduces God to your own limited concepts? This lesson demands that our speech about Allah be grounded in truth. What will you change today so that your worship is built on correct understanding?

Second Refutation: Timing of the Will
Ghazali presents a dilemma against the Mu'tazila regarding the timing of the will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's second refutation of the Mu'tazila's claim about the timing of the divine will and how it leads to either infinite regress or the possibility of the world occurring without a will.

Core Idea
The timing of the will cannot be explained without infinite regress or arbitrariness.

Ghazali continues his critique of the Mu'tazilite view on the divine will by focusing on a simple but devastating question: why does the will occur at this specific time? The Mu'tazila claim that the will is an accident that originates at a particular moment. But if it originates, there must be a cause for its specific timing. In this passage, Ghazali shows that the Mu'tazilite position either leads to an infinite regress of wills or to the admission that the world could originate without a will—both unacceptable.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully. It is a dense argument. Notice the question posed and the two possible answers.
The passage ends mid-sentence due to a page break; the argument is incomplete in the OCR but the structure is clear.
2
Identify the dilemma
Ghazali asks why the will occurred at this specific time. He presents two possibilities: either it was by another will (infinite regress) or not by a will (then the world could occur without a will).
This is a classic dilemma argument.
3
Understand the principle of contingency
The need for a will is due to contingency (jawaz), not the nature of the thing. All originated things are equal in this regard, so if the will itself can occur without a cause, so can the world.
This weakens the Mu'tazilite position.
4
Apply the same question to content
Ghazali also asks why the will was for motion rather than stillness. The same dilemma applies to the specific content of the will.
This shows the problem is not just about time but also about specification.
5
Reflect on the refutation
Consider why this argument is effective against the Mu'tazila. It forces them into an absurdity: either infinite regress or the elimination of the need for a will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني: أن الإرادةَ لِمَ حدثت في هذا الوقت على الخصوص؟ فإن كانت بإرادة أخرى.. فالسؤال في الإرادة الأخرى لازم، ويتسلسل إلى غير نهاية وإن كان لا بإرادة.. فليحدث العالم في هذا الوقت على الخصوص لا بإرادة؛ فإنَّ افتقار الحادث إلى الإرادة لجوازه، لا لكونه جسماً أو سماء، أو إرادة أو علماً، والحادثات في هذا متساوية. ثم لم يتخلصوا عن الإشكال؛ إذ يقال لهم: لِمَ حدثت الإرادة في هذا الوقت على الخصوص؟ ولِمَ حدثت إرادة الحركة دون إرادة السكون؟ فإنَّ
And the second: Why did the will occur at this specific time? If it was by another will, then the question applies to that other will, leading to an infinite regress. And if it was not by a will, then let the world occur at this specific time without a will; for the need of the originated thing for the will is due to its contingency (jawaz), not because it is a body or a heaven, or a will or knowledge, and originated things are equal in this. Then they do not escape the problem, for it is said to them: Why did the will occur at this specific time? And why did the will for motion occur rather than the will for stillness? For...
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage presents the second false claim of the Mu'tazila: that the accident will exists and originates at a specific time. Ghazali's objection is: why did this will occur at that particular time? If it occurred by another will, that leads to infinite regress. If it occurred without a will, then the world itself could occur at that time without a will, since the need for a will is due to contingency, which is the same for all originated things. Additionally, one can ask why the will was for motion rather than stillness. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the argument is clear: the Mu'tazila cannot account for the specification of the will's time and content.

Key Terms
إرادة
حدوث
تخصيص
تسلسل
افتقار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The timing of the will is arbitrary and not problematic.
Correction: Ghazali shows that the timing of the will leads to logical absurdities: either infinite regress or the possibility of the world occurring without a will.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence of the Mu'tazila claiming that the will occurred at a specific time without a preceding cause?
A) The world could have occurred at any time without a will.
B) The will would need another will, causing infinite regress.
C) Both A and B are correct.
D) The will would be eternal.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's dilemma forces the Mu'tazila to either accept infinite regress or deny the necessity of a will for origination, both of which contradict their principles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face an event that seems to have no clear cause or timing, do you instinctively try to rationalize it like the Mu'tazila did? What would it mean for you to accept that some things are simply determined by God's will beyond your understanding?
?
Imagine you are frustrated with why something happened at a particular moment in your life. How does Ghazali's argument expose the futility of demanding an explanation for God's timing? Can you commit to surrendering that need for an answer today?
?
The Mu'tazila believed they could logically dissect God's will. In what areas of your life do you subtly assume you can fully comprehend God's decisions? What would change if you truly believed that His will is beyond the chains of cause and effect that bind created things?

Refutation of the Mu'tazila on Will
Ghazali refutes the Mu'tazila's two false claims about God's will being created and not subsisting in Him.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila's view that God's will is created and not subsisting in Him is incoherent, and how Ghazali refutes their two claims.

Core Idea
A will that does not reside in the agent cannot be the agent's will.

In this passage, Ghazali dismantles two core Mu'tazila doctrines about divine will. He first establishes why an additional attribute of will is necessary, then shows how the Mu'tazila's attempt to affirm God's will while denying it as an eternal attribute leads to absurdities. Prepare to follow a tight logical argument that exposes the contradictions in their position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the literal translation. Get a feel for the argument's flow.
Notice that Ghazali begins by stating the necessity of will as an attribute.
2
Identify the two false claims
Ghazali says the Mu'tazila ventured into two hideously false matters. Find and list them in your own words.
The first claim is about the will not subsisting in God; the second is about the timing of the will.
3
Analyze the first refutation
Understand why a will that does not subsist in God cannot be attributed to Him. Ghazali compares it to attributing a will that subsists in another.
The absurdity is that it would be like saying 'He is willing by a will subsisting in another'.
4
Analyze the second refutation
Consider the dilemma Ghazali poses: if the created will arises by another will, infinite regress; if not, then the world could occur without will.
The key point is that contingency requires will, not that a thing is a body or sky.
5
Examine the final objection
Ghazali concludes that the Mu'tazila cannot escape even with their own theory: they must explain why a will for motion arises rather than for rest.
This shows the problem of explaining particularity remains.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلا بد من وصف زائد على الذات، من شأنه تخصيص الشيء عن مثله ، وليس ذلك إلا الإرادة ، وقد استوفينا تحقيق الإلزامين في كتاب التهافت الرد على المعتزلة وأما المعتزلة : فقد اقتحموا أمرين شنيعين باطلين : القائلين بالإرادة الحادثة في غير محل أحدهما : كونُ الباري تعالى مريداً بإرادة حادثة لا في محل ، وإذا لم تكن الإرادة قائمة به .. فقول القائل : إِنَّهُ مريد بها .. هجر من الكلام ؛ كقوله : إنه مريد بإرادة قائمة بغيره والثاني : أن الإرادةَ لِمَ حدثت في هذا الوقت على الخصوص ؟ فإن كانت بإرادة أخرى .. فالسؤال في الإرادة الأخرى لازم ، ويتسلسل إلى غير نهاية وإن كان لا بإرادة .. فليحدث العالم في هذا الوقت على الخصوص لا بإرادة ؛ فإنَّ افتقار الحادث إلى الإرادة لجوازه ، لا لكونه جسماً أو سماء ، أو إرادة أو علماً ، والحادثات في هذا متساوية ثم لم يتخلصوا عن الإشكال ؛ إذ يقال لهم : لِمَ حدثت الإرادة في هذا الوقت على الخصوص ؟ ولِمَ حدثت إرادة الحركة دون إرادة السكون ؟ فإنَّ عندهم تحدث لكل حادث إرادة حادثة متعلقة بذلك الحادث ، فلم لم تحدث إرادة تتعلق بضده ؟ !
It is necessary that there be an attribute additional to the essence, whose function is to specify a thing as distinct from its like, and that is nothing but the will (al-irada). And we have fully investigated the two entailments in the book al-Tahafut. The refutation of the Mu'tazila: As for the Mu'tazila, they have ventured into two hideously false matters: 1) that the Exalted Creator is willing by a created will not in a locus; and if the will is not subsisting in Him, then the statement of one who says 'He is willing by it' is nonsense, like his saying 'He is willing by a will subsisting in another'. 2) Why did the will occur specifically at this time? For if it was by another will, then the question about that other will is necessary, and it would regress infinitely. And if it was not by a will, then let the world occur at this specific time without a will; for the dependency of the contingent upon will is due to its being contingent, not because it is a body or a sky or a will or knowledge, and contingent things are equal in this regard. Then they did not escape the problem, for it is said to them: Why did the will occur at this specific time? And why did the will for motion occur rather than the will for rest? For according to them, for every contingent thing there arises a created will pertaining to that contingent thing, so why did not a will pertaining to its opposite arise?
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila's position on the divine attribute of will. It begins by establishing that a distinguishing attribute beyond the essence is necessary to specify a thing as distinct from its like, and that attribute is the will. The author then identifies two false claims of the Mu'tazila: (1) that God is willing by a created will not subsisting in Him, which is absurd because attributing an action to someone requires that the attribute subsist in the agent; (2) that the will itself, being created, would require an explanation for its occurrence at a specific time, leading either to infinite regress if by another will, or to the possibility of the world occurring without will if not by a will. The author then notes that the Mu'tazila cannot escape the objection even after positing a created will for each event, because one can still ask why a particular will arose rather than its opposite.

Key Terms
الإرادة (al-irada)
الحادثة (al-haditha)
المعتزلة (al-Mu'tazila)
الجواز (al-jawaz)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila's view that God creates a will in no locus for each event avoids the problem of explaining why things happen when they do.
Correction: Ghazali points out that even with a created will, one can still ask why that will arose at that specific time rather than another, leading to infinite regress or the possibility of the world occurring without will.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why is the Mu'tazila's claim that God is willing by a created will not in a locus problematic?
A) Because it implies God is temporally prior.
B) Because it makes God's will an attribute subsisting in another, which is nonsensical.
C) Because it leads to infinite regress.
D) Because it denies God's omnipotence.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila cannot consistently affirm God's will as an attribute while denying its subsistence in Him, nor can they avoid the question of why a particular will occurs when it does.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'God wills,' do you truly believe that His will is an eternal, real attribute of His very essence, or do you unconsciously imagine it as something external that happens in time? How would this affect your trust in His decrees?
?
The Mu'tazila wanted to rationalize everything about God's will. In your own life, is there an area where you are tempted to ‘explain away’ God's decree because it doesn't fit your logic? Will you submit to His will even when it makes no sense to you?
?
If God's will were not subsisting in Him, then every moment would be random. Do you live with the conviction that every detail of your life is under God's specific will? What one habit can you change today to remind yourself of this truth?

Refuting the Karramiyya on Will in God's Essence
Ghazali refutes the Karramiyya by exposing the consequences of their view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that the Karramiyya's claim of will occurring in God's essence avoids one problem but creates another—making God a locus of temporal events—and leaves the original issue unresolved.

Core Idea
Claiming will occurs in God's essence trades one problem for a worse one: making God a locus of temporal events.

The Karramiyya, a group within the Corporalists, attempted to solve a theological problem by asserting that God's will occurs in His essence. While this avoids the problem of will being outside God, Ghazali shows it introduces a far more serious problem: the implication that God is a substrate for temporal events, which would make Him temporal. Additionally, they still have not answered the original question of why will arises at a particular time. In this passage, Ghazali succinctly refutes their position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the structure of the argument.
2
Identify the two problems
The passage mentions two problems: one that the Karramiyya avoided (will not in essence) and one they introduced (essence as locus of events). Find both in the text.
3
Consider the implications
Why does being a locus for temporal events imply temporality? And what is the remaining problem they still face? Reflect on how this refutation works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المجسمة وأما الذين ذهبوا إلى حدوث الإرادة في ذاته : فقد دفعوا أحد الإشكالين ؛ وهو كونه مريداً بإرادة في غير ذاته ، ولكن زادوا إشكالاً آخرَ ؛ وهو كونه محلاً للحوادث ، وذلك يوجب حدوثه ، ثم قد بقي عليهم بقية الإشكال ، ولم يتخلصوا عن السؤال
The Corporalists (al-Mujassima): As for those who held the occurrence of the will in His essence, they have avoided one of the two problems, namely that He is willing by a will not in His essence, but they added another problem: that He becomes a locus for temporal events, and that necessitates His temporality. Then the remainder of the problem remains upon them, and they did not escape the question.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Karramiyya (or Mujassima) who claim that the will occurs in God's essence. It notes that they escape one problem (the will not in the essence) but fall into another: that God becomes a substrate for temporal events (hawadith), which would imply that God Himself is temporal. Additionally, they still face the lingering problem of why the will occurs at a specific time, which they have not resolved.

Key Terms
المجسمة
الكرامية
محل للحوادث
حدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Positing will in God's essence solves the problem of divine will.
Correction: Ghazali shows it creates a new problem of God being a locus for temporal events, implying His temporality, and does not fully resolve the issue.

Checkpoint
What new problem arises for those who claim the will occurs in God's essence?
A) It leads to infinite regress.
B) It makes God a locus of temporal events, implying His temporality.
C) It makes God's will subsist in another.
D) It denies the attribute of will altogether.

Key Takeaway
Positing will in God's essence does not solve the problem of divine will; it merely shifts the difficulty to an even more serious one: making God subject to temporality.

?

?
When you catch yourself oversimplifying a theological issue (like the Karramiyya did), what does that reveal about your own fear of complexity or desire for control? Will you commit to sitting with the tension rather than forcing a false solution?

The Eternal Will: Why It Needs No Reason for Its Choice
Affirming the eternal will and refuting the objection against its distinguishing function.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic theological argument that the eternal will of Allah distinguishes between like things by its very essence, and why questioning this distinction is as futile as questioning why knowledge knows.

Core Idea
Will is defined by distinguishing; asking why is like asking why knowledge knows.

In this passage, Imam al-Ghazali presents the Sunni position on the divine attribute of will. He argues that the will is eternal and distinguishes contingent events from their similar opposites. He then refutes a common objection by showing that the will's nature is to distinguish, just as knowledge's nature is to disclose the known. This lesson unpacks his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Ghazali first states the position of Ahl al-Haqq, then addresses an objection.
The passage is dense but logically structured.
2
Understand the analogy
Ghazali uses an analogy: questioning the will's distinction is like questioning knowledge's disclosure. Grasp why this analogy is powerful.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Ghazali praises the group that affirms an eternal will. Consider why this is the most upright path.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما أهل الحقِّ : فإنهم قالوا : إن الحادثاتِ تحدث بإرادة قديمة تعلقت بها ، فميَّزَتها عن أضدادها المماثلة لها ، وقول القائل : إِنَّهُ لِمَ تعلقت بها وضدها مثلها في الإمكان .. سؤال خطاً ؛ فإنَّ الإرادة ليست إلا عبارة عن صفة شأنها تمييز الشيء عن مثله فقول القائل : لِمَ ميَّزتِ الإرادةُ الشيء عن مثله .. كقول القائل : لِمَ أوجب العلم انكشاف المعلوم ؟ فيقال : لا معنى للعلم إلا ما أوجب انكشاف المعلوم ، فقول القائل : لِمَ أوجب الانكشاف .. كقوله : لم كانَ العلم علماً ، ولم كان الممكن ممكناً ، والواجب واجباً ، وهذا محال ؛ لأنَّ العلم علم لذاته ، وكذا الممكنُ والواجب وسائر الذوات ، فكذلك الإرادة ، وحقيقتها تمييز الشيء عن مثله . فقول القائل : لِمَ ميزت الشيء عن مثله .. كقوله : لم كانت الإرادة إرادة ، والقدرة قدرة ؟ وهو محال ، وكلُّ فريق مضطر إلى إثبات صفة شأنها تمييز الشيء عن مثله ، وليس ذلك إلا الإرادة فكان أقومُ الفرق قِيلاً ، وأهداهم سبيلاً .. مَنْ أثبت هذه الصفة ولم يجعلها حادثة ، بل قال : هي قديمة متعلقة بالأحداث في وقت مخصوص
As for the People of Truth (Ahl al-Haqq), they said: Contingent things occur by an eternal will (irada qadima) that is attached to them, so it distinguishes them from their similar opposites. And the statement of one who says 'Why did it attach to this while its opposite is equal in possibility?' is an erroneous question. For the will is nothing but an attribute whose function is to distinguish a thing from its like. So the statement of one who says 'Why did the will distinguish the thing from its like?' is like the statement of one who says 'Why did knowledge necessitate the disclosure of the known?' It is said: There is no meaning of knowledge except that it necessitates the disclosure of the known. So the statement of one who says 'Why did it necessitate disclosure?' is like his saying 'Why was knowledge knowledge, and why was the possible possible, and the necessary necessary?' And this is absurd, because knowledge is knowledge by its essence, and likewise the possible, the necessary, and all essences. So too the will, its reality is distinguishing a thing from its like. So the statement of one who says 'Why did it distinguish the thing from its like?' is like his saying 'Why was the will will, and power power?' And that is absurd. And every school is compelled to affirm an attribute whose function is to distinguish a thing from its like, and that is nothing but the will. Therefore, the most upright of the groups in statement and most guided in path is the one who affirms this attribute and does not make it temporal, but says it is eternal, attached to events at a specific time.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage presents the correct view of Ahl al-Sunnah (the People of Truth) regarding the attribute of will. They affirm an eternal will that is attached to contingent events, thereby distinguishing them from their similar opposites. The author then addresses a potential objection: why did the will attach to a particular thing when its opposite is equally possible? He explains that this question is erroneous because the very nature (haqiqa) of will is to distinguish one thing from another, just as the nature of knowledge is to necessitate the disclosure of the known. Asking why will distinguishes is like asking why knowledge knows, or why the possible is possible, or why the necessary is necessary—these are questions about essences, which are self-evident. The author concludes by praising the group that affirms this eternal will, as it is the most upright and guided path.

Key Terms
أهل الحق (Ahl al-Haqq)
الإرادة القديمة (al-irada al-qadima)
تمييز الشيء عن مثله (tamyiz al-shay' 'an mithlihi)
حقيقة (haqiqa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the will needs a reason for its choice, as if it were a rational agent deliberating between options.
Correction: Ghazali shows that will is defined by distinguishing, so asking why it distinguishes is like asking why knowledge knows – it is a question about the essence of the attribute itself.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why is the question 'Why did the will choose this over its similar opposite?' erroneous?
A) Because the will is not real.
B) Because the will is inherent in the essence of the thing chosen.
C) Because the essence of will is to distinguish, so the question is like asking why knowledge knows.
D) Because the will is free to choose arbitrarily.

Key Takeaway
The eternal will is an attribute whose essence is to distinguish, and questioning its distinction is as meaningless as questioning why knowledge knows.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you questioned why Allah allowed a certain trial in your life even though another outcome seemed equally possible. Did you feel the need to find a reason for His choice? After this lesson, how does your perspective on that question change? Will you now choose to submit to His will without demanding an explanation?
?
When you see injustice in the world, do you ever think, 'Why did Allah's will choose this over that?' Reflect on the analogy: asking why the will distinguishes is like asking why knowledge knows. Are you willing to accept that the function of His will is simply to distinguish, and that there is no 'why' beyond that? How might this reshape your trust in His decree?
?
This lesson says that every school must affirm an attribute that distinguishes. In your own life, do you try to impose your own 'distinguishing' reasons on Allah's decisions? What would it look like for you to stop demanding a reason and instead rest in the fact that His will is will by its very nature?
