Ghazali on the Eternal Divine Will
Ghazali refutes the Karramiyya and defines the eternal divine will as a distinguishing attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the divine will must be eternal and how it functions as a distinguishing attribute, according to Ghazali's refutation of the Karramiyya.

Core Idea
The divine will is an eternal attribute that, by its essence, distinguishes between equally possible things.

In this passage, Ghazali addresses a key theological dispute about the nature of God's will. He critiques the view that will is created in God's essence and defends the Sunni position that will is eternal and serves to distinguish between similar possibilities. The argument hinges on understanding will as an attribute whose essence is to distinguish, much like knowledge's essence is to disclose. Let's explore how Ghazali dismantles the objections and establishes the correct doctrine.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the Disagreement
Identify the two positions: the Karramiyya claim that will originates in God's essence, and the Sunni (Ahl al-Haqq) claim that it is eternal.
Ghazali begins by mentioning the Mujassima, who are anthropomorphists, but the main refutation is against the Karramiyya.
2
Problems with Originated Will
Note the two problems Ghazali lists for the Karramiyya: they avoid one problem (will not in essence) but create another (God becomes locus of accidents implying temporality), and they still fail to answer all objections.
3
The Sunni Solution
Understand that originated things occur by an eternal will that attaches to them at a specific time, distinguishing them from their similar opposites.
The will is not created; it is eternal and 'attaches' to temporal events.
4
Analyze the Key Analogy
See why asking 'why did the will distinguish?' is like asking 'why does knowledge know?'—it is asking about the essence, which is self-explanatory.
Ghazali compares will to knowledge, possible, necessary—all defined by their essences.
5
Conclusion and Regress-Stopping
Recognize that every school must affirm a distinguishing attribute, and the eternal will cuts off infinite regress of questioning.
The soundest view is to affirm will as eternal, not originated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المجسمة

وأما الذين ذهبوا إلى حدوث الإرادة في ذاته : فقد دفعوا أحد الإشكالين؛ وهو كونه مريداً بإرادة في غير ذاته، ولكن زادوا إشكالاً آخرَ؛ وهو كونه محلاً للحوادث، وذلك يوجب حدوثه، ثم قد بقي عليهم بقية الإشكال، ولم يتخلصوا عن السؤال.

وأما أهل الحقِّ: فإنهم قالوا: إن الحادثاتِ تحدث بإرادة قديمة تعلقت بها، فميَّزَتها عن أضدادها المماثلة لها، وقول القائل: إنَّهُ لِمَ تعلقت بها وضدها مثلها في الإمكان .. سؤال خطأ؛ فإنَّ الإرادة ليست إلا عبارة عن صفة شأنها تمييز الشيء عن مثله.

فقول القائل: لِمَ ميَّزتِ الإرادةُ الشيء عن مثله .. كقول القائل: لِمَ أوجب العلم انكشاف المعلوم؟ فيقال: لا معنى للعلم إلا ما أوجب انكشاف المعلوم، فقول القائل: لِمَ أوجب الانكشاف .. كقوله: لم كانَ العلم علماً، ولم كان الممكن ممكناً، والواجب واجباً، وهذا محال؛ لأنَّ العلم علم لذاته، وكذا الممكنُ والواجب وسائر الذوات، فكذلك الإرادة، وحقيقتها تمييز الشيء عن مثله.

فقول القائل: لِمَ مُيِّزَ الشيء عن مثله .. كقوله: لم كانت الإرادة إرادة، والقدرة قدرة؟ وهو محال، وكلُّ فريق مضطر إلى إثبات صفة شأنها تمييز الشيء عن مثله، وليس ذلك إلا الإرادة.

فكان أقومُ الفرق قِيلاً، وأهداهم سبيلاً .. مَنْ أثبت هذه الصفة ولم يجعلها حادثة، بل قال: هي قديمة متعلقة بالأحداث في وقت مخصوص، فكان الحدوث في ذلك الوقت لذلك، وهذا مما لا يستغني عنه فريق من الفرق، وبه ينقطع التسلسل في لزوم هذا السؤال.
The Mujassima (Anthropomorphists). As for those who held that will originates in His essence: they pushed away one of the two problems—namely, that He is willing by a will not in His essence—but they added another problem: that He becomes a locus for originated things, and that necessitates His originatedness. Then the remaining problem still remains for them, and they have not freed themselves from the question. As for the People of Truth, they said: originated things occur by an eternal will that attached to them, and distinguished them from their similar opposites. The saying of one who says: 'Why did it attach to them while their opposite is similar in possibility?' is an invalid question; for will is nothing but an expression for an attribute whose function is to distinguish a thing from its like. So the saying of one who says: 'Why did the will distinguish a thing from its like?' is like the saying of one who says: 'Why did knowledge necessitate the disclosure of the known?' It is said: There is no meaning for knowledge except what necessitates disclosure of the known. So the saying 'Why did it necessitate disclosure?' is like his saying 'Why was knowledge knowledge, why was the possible possible, and the necessary necessary?' This is impossible; because knowledge is knowledge by virtue of its essence, and likewise the possible, the necessary, and all essences. Thus also the will, whose reality is distinguishing a thing from its like. So the saying 'Why did it distinguish a thing from its like?' is like his saying 'Why was will will, and power power?' This is impossible. Every school is compelled to affirm an attribute whose function is distinguishing a thing from its like, and that is nothing but will. So the most upright of the schools in saying and the most guided in path is the one who affirmed this attribute and did not make it originated, but said: it is eternal, attached to originated events at a specific time; thus originatedness occurs at that time for that reason. This is something that no school can dispense with, and by it the infinite regress in the necessity of this question is cut off.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Karramiyya (and implicitly the Mujassima) and a positive definition of the divine will according to Ahl al-Haqq. The author first critiques the position that will is originated in God's essence, showing that it replaces one problem with another and fails to answer the remaining objection. Then he presents the Sunni position: will is eternal and distinguishes events from their similar counterparts. He then answers the anticipated objection 'why did it distinguish?' by arguing that will is an attribute whose very essence is to distinguish, just as knowledge is an attribute whose essence is to disclose. Therefore, asking 'why it distinguishes' is like asking 'why knowledge knows' or 'why the possible is possible'—it is asking about the essence of the thing, which is absurd because essences are necessary properties. Finally, he concludes that every school must affirm such a distinguishing attribute, and the soundest view is to affirm it as eternal, not originated. This cuts off infinite regress of questions.

القديم
الحادث
التمييز
العلم
الممكن
الواجب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is legitimate to ask 'Why did God will this particular event?' and expect a reason beyond the will itself.
Correction: Ghazali shows that this question misunderstands the nature of will. Will is the attribute that distinguishes, so asking why it distinguishes is like asking why knowledge knows—it is a question about the essence, which is self-explanatory.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what does it mean to ask 'why did the will distinguish a thing from its like?'?
A) A valid question about God's wisdom.
B) Like asking why knowledge knows, which is an absurd question about essence.
C) A question that can be answered by citing the object's merits.
D) A question that proves the will is created.

Key Takeaway
The divine will is eternal and distinguishes between equally possible events by its very essence, and questioning this distinction is as meaningless as questioning why knowledge knows.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation that seems unfair or inexplicable, do you catch yourself demanding to know 'Why, God?' as if His will needs a reason intelligible to you? How does Ghazali's teaching that will distinguishes by its very essence challenge your impulse to question God's choices?
?
If the divine will is eternal and distinguishes without external cause, what does that mean for your own attempts to 'earn' or 'sway' God's favor through your deeds? Are you secretly trying to manipulate His will by being good enough, as if He needed a reason?
?
You believe God's will is perfect and wise, yet you often complain about the 'bad timing' of events in your life. How does the truth that God's will is attached to each event at 'a specific time' (as the passage says) confront your impatience or discontent with His timing?

Divine Will Encompasses All Events Including Evil
Ghazali presents the Ash'ari thesis and the Mu'tazila objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari argument that all originated events, including evil and sin, are willed by God, and the Mu'tazila objection that this implies imperfection.

Core Idea
God wills everything that exists, even evil and sin.

Ghazali begins by establishing that divine will (irada) relates to all created events. He argues that every event is brought into existence by divine power, which requires a will to specify and direct it. Therefore, every event, including evil and sin, is willed by God. This lesson presents this positive argument and then introduces the Mu'tazila counterclaim that God hates evil and thus cannot will it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Ghazali uses logical connection: 'every originated thing is invented by power, and everything invented by power is in need of will.'
Pay attention to the sequence of reasoning.
2
Identify the positive argument
Find where Ghazali states that every originated thing is willed. What are the premises? How does he conclude that evil and sin are willed?
3
Understand the Mu'tazila objection
Read the Mu'tazila claim that sins and evils occur without God's will, and that what God hates outweighs what He wills, implying deficiency.
Notice how Ghazali presents this as a challenge to his doctrine.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: Why does this matter? How does this affect the concept of divine power and justice?
This connects to the broader debate on free will and divine decree.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والآن : فكما تمهَّدَ القولُ في أصل الإرادة . . فاعلم : أنها متعلقة بجميع الحادثات عندنا؛ من حيث إنَّه ظهر أن كل حادث فمخترع بقدرة، وكلُّ مخترع بالقدرة فمحتاج إلى إرادة تَصْرِفُ القدرة إلى المقدور وتخصصها به، فكلُّ مقدور مراد، وكلُّ حادث مقدورٌ .. فكلُّ حادث مراد، والشرُّ والكفرُ والمعصية من مرادات الله تعالى، والشرُّ والكفرُ والمعصية حوادث؛ فهي إذن - لا محالة - مرادةٌ، فما شاء الله . . كان، وما لم يشأ .. لم يكن، هذا مذهب السلف الصالحين، ومعتقد أهل السنة أجمعين، وقد قامت عليه البراهين. وأما المعتزلة .. فإنهم يقولون : إنَّ المعاصي كلها والشرور كلها جاريةٌ بغير إرادته سبحانه، فالمعاصي والشرور بغير إرادة الله، بل هو كاره لها ومعلوم أن أكثر ما يجري في العالم المعاصي، فإذن .. ما يكرَهُهُ أكثر مما يريدُهُ، فهو إلى العجز والقصور أقرب بزعمهم، تعالى ربُّ العالمين عن قول الظالمين. فإن قيل : فكيف يأمرُ بما لا يريد؟ وكيف يريد شيئاً وينهى عنه؟ وكيف يريد الفجور والمعاصي والظلم والقبيح، ومريد القبيح سفيه؟ قلنا : إذا كشفنا عن حقيقة الأمر، وبيَّنا أنَّه مباين للإرادة، وكشفنا عن القبح والحسن، وبيَّنا أنَّ ذلك يرجع إلى موافقة الأغراض ومخالفتها، وهو سبحانه منزه عن الأغراض .. اندفعت هذه الإشكالات، وسيأتي ذلك في موضعه إن شاء الله تعالى.
And now, just as the discussion on the origin of will has been prepared, know that it (will) is related to all originated things according to us, in that it has become evident that every originated thing is invented by power, and everything invented by power is in need of a will that directs power to the thing willed and specifies it. So everything willed is intended, and every originated thing is willed. Therefore, every originated thing is intended. Evil, disbelief, and disobedience are among the intentions of God Almighty, and evil, disbelief, and disobedience are originated things. So they are, without doubt, intended. So what God wills is, and what He does not will is not. This is the doctrine of the righteous predecessors and the belief of the Ahl al-Sunna collectively, and proofs have been established upon it. As for the Mu'tazila, they say that all acts of disobedience and all evils occur without His will, rather He dislikes them. And it is known that most of what occurs in the world is disobedience. So what He dislikes is more than what He wills. Thus, according to their claim, He is closer to incapacity and deficiency — exalted is the Lord of the worlds above the saying of the wrongdoers. If it is said: How can He command what He does not will? And how can He will something and forbid it? And how can He will immorality, disobedience, injustice, and evil? And one who wills evil is foolish. We say: If we uncover the reality of command, and show that it is distinct from will, and uncover ugliness and beauty, and show that they refer to agreement with purposes and opposition to them, and He is exalted above purposes, then these difficulties are dispelled, and that will come in its place, God willing.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage is a structured argument defending the Ash'ari doctrine that divine will (irada) encompasses all created events, including evil and sin. Ghazali begins by establishing that all originated things (hawadith) are brought into existence by divine power (qudra), and power requires a will to specify and direct it to a particular object. Thus, every originated thing is willed (murad) by God. Since evil, disbelief, and sin are originated, they must be willed. This he presents as the consensus of the Salaf and Ahl al-Sunna. He then presents the Mu'tazila objection: they claim that sins and evils occur without God's will, that He hates them, and that since most of the world is sin, what He hates outweighs what He wills, implying imperfection. After this objection, Ghazali provides the Ash'ari response: the apparent tension between divine command (amr) and will is resolved by distinguishing command from will — command pertains to the realm of obligation, not to the actual existence of things. Similarly, the concepts of good (husn) and evil (qubh) are relative to purposes (aghrad), and God is free from purposes. Therefore, God may will something that He commands against, and His willing evil is not a sign of foolishness because He is transcendent above the anthropomorphic considerations of good and evil. The detailed explanation is deferred to later in the book.

Key Terms
إرادة
حادث
قدرة
مراد
مقدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ash'ari belief implies God commands evil.
Correction: The passage does not address command; it only states that all events are willed. Command is distinct from will, as the next lesson explains.

Checkpoint
What is the basis for Ghazali's claim that evil and sin are willed by God?
A) They are good in some respects.
B) They are originated events and all originated events are willed.
C) God explicitly reveals that He wills them in the Quran.
D) The Mu'tazila agree that they are willed.

Key Takeaway
Divine will encompasses every created event, including evil and sin, because every event requires divine power and specification through will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial or witness evil in the world, do you truly submit to the fact that it is willed by Allah, or does your heart resist and question His wisdom? What does your reaction reveal about your iman in divine will?
?
The Mu'tazila thought that God's will does not include evil because they could not reconcile it with His justice. Have you ever felt that Allah should not have willed something in your life because it seemed too harsh or unjust? How can you train your heart to accept that His will is perfect even when it includes pain?

Resolving the Tension Between Divine Will and Command
Ghazali answers the Mu'tazila objections by distinguishing command from will and defining good/evil relative to purposes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali resolves the apparent contradiction between God willing evil and commanding against it, by distinguishing will from command and clarifying the nature of good and evil.

Core Idea
Command and will are distinct; God may will what He commands against.

In response to the Mu'tazila objection that it is contradictory for God to command what He does not will, and to will evil, Ghazali provides a twofold answer. He first distinguishes between divine command (amr) and divine will (irada). Then he explains that the concepts of good (husn) and evil (qubh) are relative to purposes (aghrad), and God is free from all purposes. This lesson unpacks this response, which forms a cornerstone of Ash'ari theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage containing the Mu'tazila question and Ghazali's answer. Focus on the key distinctions he makes.
The question is: How can God command what He does not will? And how can He will evil without being foolish?
2
Understand the distinction between command and will
Ghazali says that 'command is distinct from will.' What does that mean? Consider that a king may command his subjects to do something, but will that they fail. This is possible if command pertains to obligation, not to actual existence.
3
Grasp the meaning of good and evil as relative to purposes
Ghazali states that good and evil are not intrinsic but are defined by agreement with purposes. Since God has no purposes, actions like evil are not 'evil' in the sense of being contrary to His goals. Thus, God willing evil is not a sign of foolishness.
This is a key Ash'ari doctrine about moral values.
4
Connect to the broader Islamic creed
Reflect on how this defense upholds God's omnipotence and sovereignty while acknowledging that His commands guide human morality.
This lesson prepares for further discussion on why God creates evil.

Source Text

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's willing evil makes Him unjust or foolish.
Correction: Ghazali argues that good and evil are relative to purposes; God is above purposes, so His will for evil is not a sign of deficiency.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why is it not contradictory for God to command what He does not will?
A) God's will is always in harmony with His commands.
B) Command and will are distinct; command pertains to obligation, not existence.
C) God's will is only for good, so evil is not willed.
D) The Mu'tazila misinterpret the nature of command.

Key Takeaway
The apparent contradiction between divine will and command is resolved by distinguishing command from will and understanding that good and evil are relative to purposes, from which God is free.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you secretly believe that God's will for that sin makes Him responsible? How does Ghazali's distinction between command and will free you from blaming God while still acknowledging His sovereignty?
?
In your own life, are you guilty of assuming that what God commands must be what He wills for you personally? How does that assumption affect your trust in His decrees when things don't go as you prayed?
?
If God wills evil and commands against it, where does that leave your own responsibility? Are you willing to accept full accountability for your actions while affirming that nothing happens outside His will?

Proof That God Is Hearing and Seeing
Ghazali establishes hearing and seeing via scripture and reason.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali establishes the attributes of hearing and seeing for God using both scriptural and rational arguments.

Core Idea
God's hearing and seeing are proven by revelation and the logic of prophetic argument.

Ghazali now turns to the fifth and sixth attributes: hearing and seeing. He claims that the Creator must be described as Hearing and Seeing, and he supports this with two types of evidence. First, the Quran explicitly states these attributes. Second, the argument of Prophet Abraham against idols implies that the true object of worship must possess them. In this lesson, we will examine both proofs and see how they reinforce each other.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two attributes
Note that Ghazali treats hearing and seeing together as the fifth and sixth attributes. Read the opening sentence carefully.
2
Examine the scriptural proof
Look at the Quranic verses Ghazali cites: the general verse and the specific statement of Abraham. Reflect on why these verses are considered proof.
Abraham's words refute idolatry by pointing out that idols lack hearing and sight.
3
Follow the rational argument
Understand the logical point: if Abraham had worshipped a God who does not hear or see, his own argument would turn against him. Thus, his worship implies that God has these attributes.
This is an indirect proof via consistency.
4
Combine the proofs
Notice that Ghazali uses both revelation and reason together. See how they complement each other.
This is a standard method in Sunni kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصفة الخامسة والسادسة السمع والبصر : ندعي : أنَّ صانع العالم سميع بصير، وندلُّ عليه بالشرع والعقل. أما الشرع : فتدل عليه آياتٌ من القرآن كثيرةٌ؛ كقوله تعالى : وَهُوَ السَّمِيعُ البَصِيرُ، وكقول إبراهيم عليه السلام : لِمَ نَعْبُدُ مَا لَا يَسْمَعُ وَلَا يُبْصِرُ وَلَا يُغْنِي عَنكَ شَيْئًا . ونعلمُ : أَنَّ الدليل غير منقلب عليه في معبوده، كان يعبد سميعاً بصيراً، وإلا .. شاركهم في الإلزام.
The fifth and sixth attributes: hearing and sight. We claim that the Maker of the world is hearing and seeing, and we prove it by revelation and reason. As for revelation, many verses of the Quran indicate it, such as His saying: 'And He is the Hearing, the Seeing,' and the saying of Abraham, peace be upon him: 'Why do we worship that which does not hear and does not see and cannot benefit you at all?' And we know that the proof does not turn back upon its worshipper? Rather, he worshipped the Hearing, the Seeing; otherwise, he would share in the refutation.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage is a concise definition and proof of the divine attributes of hearing (sam') and seeing (basr). Ghazali first identifies these as the fifth and sixth attributes. He states the claim that the Creator is hearing and seeing, and then provides two types of proof: scriptural and rational. The scriptural proof cites Quranic verses, notably the verse 'And He is the Hearing, the Seeing' and the statement of Prophet Abraham rejecting idols because they cannot hear or see. The rational argument is implicit: if Abraham had worshipped a being that was not hearing and seeing, his argument against idolatry would collapse, because he would be committing the same error. Hence, the object of his worship must possess these attributes. The phrase 'otherwise he would share in the refutation' means that if God were not hearing and seeing, Abraham's proof would be invalid and he would be subject to the same criticism he leveled at the idolaters.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That hearing and seeing for God imply physical organs or sensory faculties like humans.
Correction: Ghazali means that God has perfect knowledge of all audibles and visibles without any physical organ or modality.

Checkpoint
How does Ghazali prove the attributes of hearing and seeing for God?
A) By rational proofs alone.
B) By scriptural verses and the logical argument from Ibrahim's statement.
C) By the consensus of the Salaf.
D) By the debate with the Mu'tazila.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali proves God's hearing and seeing through both explicit revelation and the logical coherence of prophetic argument.

God's Hearing and Sight: Scriptural Evidence
Ghazali states the attributes of hearing and sight and gives scriptural evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the attributes of hearing and sight are proven by revelation, specifically through Qur'anic verses and the argument of Abraham.

Core Idea
God hears and sees; this is proven by scripture and sound reasoning.

In this lesson, we begin with the claim that God is Hearing and Seeing, two of His essential attributes. The author cites clear Qur'anic verses and highlights a critical methodological point: the argument against idols cannot be reversed against God.

Guided Path
1
Read the claim and verses
Read the Arabic passage once. Identify the claim that God is Hearing and Seeing, and note the two Qur'anic verses cited as evidence.
The verses are from Surah 42 and Surah 19.
2
Understand the non-reversibility principle
Notice the author's statement that Abraham's argument against idols cannot be turned against God. Why is it not reversible? Because God truly hears and sees.
This is a key theological method: arguments against false gods do not apply to the true God.
3
Connect to broader theology
Ask yourself: why is this principle important for defending divine attributes? It shows that analogical reasoning about God must start from revelation, not from creatures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ندعي :
الصفة الخامسة والسادسة
السمع والبصر
: أنَّ صانع العالم سميع بصير ، وندلُّ عليه بالشرع والعقل
أما الشرع : فتدل عليه آياتٌ من القرآن كثيرةٌ ؛ كقوله تعالى : ﴿وَهُوَ السَّمِيعُ البَصِيرُ ) ، وكقول إبراهيم عليه السلام : لِمَ نَعْبُدُ مَا لَا يَسْمَعُ وَلَا يُبْصِرُ وَلَا يُغْنِي عَنكَ شَيْئًا ) ، ونعلمُ : أَنَّ الدليل غير منقلب عليه في معبوده ، كان يعبد سميعاً بصيراً ، وإلا . . شاركهم في الإلزام (۱)
وأَنَّهُ
وتوضيح الحق في هذه المحجة .. يحسن إيراد مناظرة الأستاذ أبي إسحاق الإسفرايني مع شيخ المعتزلة في عصره القاضي عبد الجبار المعتزلي ؛ وقد أوردها الإمام السبكي في طبقاته : ( ٢٦١/٤ ) فقال : ( قال عبد الجبار في ابتداء جلوسه للمناظرة : سبحان من تنزه عن الفحشاء ، فقال الأستاذ مجيباً : سبحان من لا يقع في ملكه إلا ما يشاء ، فقال عبد الجبار : أفيشاء ربنا أن يعصى ؟ فقال الأستاذ : أيعصى ربُّنا قهراً ؟! فقال
عبد الجبار : أفرأيت إن منعني الهدى ، وقضى علي بالردي .. أحسن إليَّ أم أسا ؟ فقال الأستاذ : إن كان منعك ما هو لك .. فقد أسا ، وإن منعك ما هو له .. فيختص برحمته من يشا ! فانقطع عبد الجبار )
وقد سبق حبرُ القرآن سيدنا ابن عباس رضي الله عنهما لهذا ؛ فقد روى ابن عبد البر بسنده في ( التمهيد : ( ٦٤/٦ ) : ( عن عطاء بن أبي رباح قال : كنت عند ابن عباس ، فأتاه رجل فقال : أرأيت من حرمني الهدى وأورثني الضلالة والردى . . أتراه أحسن إلي أو ظلمني ؟ فقال ابن عباس : إن كان الهدى شيئاً كان لك عنده فمنعكه . . فقد ظلمك ، وإن كان الهدى له يؤتيه من يشاء .. فما ظلمك شيئاً ، ولا تجالسني بعده ) .
ثم روى شيئاً في هذا المعنى رحمه الله تعالى .
We claim: the fifth and sixth attributes: hearing and sight: that the Maker of the world is Hearing, Seeing. And we prove it by revelation and reason. As for revelation: many verses of the Qur'an indicate it, like His saying: 'And He is the Hearing, the Seeing,' and like the saying of Abraham, peace be upon him: 'Why do we worship that which does not hear and does not see and does not benefit you anything?' And we know that the argument is not reversible against his object of worship, since he used to worship a Hearing, Seeing [God]; otherwise, they would share in the objection (1). And clarifying the truth in this path: it is good to present the debate of the master Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini with the chief of the Mu'tazila in his time, al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar al-Mu'tazili; and Imam al-Subki mentioned it in his Tabaqat (4/261) and said: ( 'Abd al-Jabbar said at the beginning of his sitting for debate: Glory be to Him who is free from obscenity. The master replied: Glory be to Him in whose kingdom nothing occurs except what He wills. Then 'Abd al-Jabbar said: Does our Lord will that He be disobeyed? The master said: Is our Lord disobeyed by force? Then 'Abd al-Jabbar said: Do you think if He prevents me from guidance and decrees upon me ruin... has He done good to me or evil? The master said: If He prevented you from something that is yours, then He has done evil; but if He prevented you from something that is His, then He chooses for His mercy whom He wills. So 'Abd al-Jabbar was silenced.) And the ink of the Qur'an, our master Ibn 'Abbas, may God be pleased with them both, preceded this; for Ibn 'Abd al-Barr narrated with his chain in al-Tamhid (6/64): ( from 'Ata' ibn Abi Rabah, he said: I was with Ibn 'Abbas, and a man came to him and said: Do you think that one who deprived me of guidance and bequeathed to me misguidance and ruin... do you think he has done good to me or wronged me? Ibn 'Abbas said: If guidance was something that was yours with him and he prevented you from it, then he has wronged you; but if guidance belongs to Him, He gives it to whom He wills... then he has not wronged you at all, and do not sit with me after this.) Then he narrated something in this meaning, may God have mercy on him.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage is a combined claim, scriptural proof, and illustrative debate. The author begins by stating the claim: the fifth and sixth divine attributes are hearing (sam') and sight (basar); the Maker of the world is Hearing and Seeing. He asserts that proof comes from both revelation (shar') and reason ('aql). For the scriptural evidence, he cites two Qur'anic verses: 'He is the Hearing, the Seeing' (Qur'an 42:11), and Abraham's argument against idol worship: 'Why do we worship that which does not hear and does not see?' (Qur'an 19:42). The author then makes an important methodological point: the argument Abraham used against his people (that their idols cannot hear or see) cannot be reversed against his own object of worship, because Abraham worshipped a God who is truly Hearing and Seeing. If it were reversible, then the polytheists could use the same argument against Abraham's God, which shows Abraham's argument is sound. To further clarify this methodological point, the author recounts a debate between the Ash'arite theologian Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini and the Mu'tazilite al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar. The debate does not directly concern hearing and sight but rather divine decree and justice. In it, al-Isfara'ini refutes 'Abd al-Jabbar's objection to predestination by showing that God's actions are just because He is the owner of all things; if He prevents what is His, He is not unjust. This illustrates the principle that arguments against God's attributes or actions often rely on an invalid analogy between God and creatures. The author then cites a similar narration from Ibn 'Abbas, who gives the same response to a question about divine justice: if guidance is God's to give, He does not wrong anyone by withholding it. These anecdotes reinforce the correct method of analogical reasoning with respect to God: one must not assume that what is unjust for a creature is unjust for the Creator.

Key Terms
سميع
بصير
الشرع
العقل
الدليل غير منقلب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the argument against idols can be turned against God (e.g., if Abraham's argument shows idols are unworthy because they cannot hear or see, then one could argue that God also cannot be worshipped because He does not hear or see in a human way, or because the same logic applies).
Correction: The author shows the argument is not reversible because God truly hears and sees in a manner befitting His majesty, while idols lack these attributes entirely.

Checkpoint
What is the author's primary scriptural evidence for God being Hearing and Seeing?
A) A verse from Surah al-Ikhlas
B) A verse from Surah ash-Shura and the argument of Abraham
C) The Torah and Gospel
D) A hadith from the Prophet

Key Takeaway
God's hearing and sight are established by revelation, and the rational argument against idols is uniquely non-reversible because God is truly Hearing and Seeing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly worship a God whom you believe hears and sees you at all times? How does this awareness change your actions today?

The polytheists worshipped idols that could not hear or see. Are there any 'idols' in your life—things you rely on or prioritize that cannot truly hear or see your needs? What would it take to reject them?
?

The Non-Reversibility of Arguments: A Theological Debate
Ghazali recounts a debate and an anecdote to illustrate correct analogical reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the principle that arguments about God's attributes cannot be reversed by analogical reasoning, as demonstrated by the debate between al-Isfara'ini and 'Abd al-Jabbar and the answer of Ibn Abbas.

Core Idea
What is just for God differs from what is just for us; we cannot reverse human analogies onto God.

This lesson presents two historical anecdotes that illustrate a crucial methodological principle: when reasoning about God, we must not assume that what applies to creatures applies to Him. The debate between the Ash'arite master and the Mu'tazilite judge showcases this, as does the wise answer of Ibn Abbas.

Guided Path
1
Read the debate
Read the debate between al-Isfara'ini and 'Abd al-Jabbar. Identify the point of contention: Is God just when He decrees guidance or misguidance?
The debate is about divine decree and justice, not directly about hearing/sight.
2
Understand al-Isfara'ini's response
Note that al-Isfara'ini distinguishes between what is God's own and what is due to creatures. He argues that if God prevents what is His, He does not wrong anyone.
This is the key principle: God owns all things, so His actions are just.
3
See the same principle in Ibn Abbas's answer
Read Ibn Abbas's response to a similar question. He makes the same distinction: if guidance belongs to God, He is not unjust by withholding it.
Ibn Abbas even dismisses the questioner, showing the seriousness of the error.

The First Objection: Are Hearing and Sight Just Knowledge?
Objection that hearing/sight mean knowledge, with response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the hermeneutical principle that metaphorical interpretation of divine attributes is only justified when the literal meaning is impossible, and why Ghazali insists on affirming the literal meaning of hearing and sight for Allah.

Core Idea
Metaphorical interpretation is only justified when the literal meaning is impossible.

In this passage, Ghazali addresses a common objection raised by Mu'tazilites: perhaps the terms 'hearing' and 'sight' in the Qur'an simply refer to God's knowledge. He responds by laying down a clear hermeneutic principle: the words of revelation are only diverted from their literal sense when affirming that literal sense is impossible. Since there is no impossibility in God being truly Hearing and Seeing, the literal meaning stands, and there is no justification for arbitrary denial.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first part of the passage where the objector proposes that hearing and sight might mean knowledge. Notice how the objection is framed as a question.
The objector is trying to reduce two attributes to one.
2
Identify Ghazali's response principle
Read the response and identify the key principle: words of revelation are only taken from their literal meanings if affirming them literally is impossible.
This principle is central to Ghazali's methodology.
3
Apply the principle to hearing and sight
Notice that Ghazali asserts there is no impossibility in God being Hearing and Seeing; rather, it is necessary. Therefore, the literal meaning must be retained.
He also appeals to consensus as confirmation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تحريجة : ولم لا يراد فإن قيل : إنما أريد به العلم (۱) ؟
بالسمع والبصر صفة
العلم؟
قلنا : إنما تصرفُ ألفاظ الشرع عن موضوعاتها المفهومة السابقة إلى الأفهام إذا كان يستحيل تقريرها على الموضوع ، ولا استحالة في كونه سميعاً ، بل يجب أن يكون كذلك ، فلا معنى للتحكم بإنكار ما فهمه أهلُ
الإجماع من القرآن (۲) .
A point of objection: And why not mean by hearing and sight the attribute of knowledge? If it is said: only knowledge is intended by them? We say: The words of revelation are only diverted from their previously understood meanings to [other] understandings if it is impossible to affirm them in their [literal] subject; and there is no impossibility in His being Hearing, rather it is necessary that He be so. So there is no meaning to arbitrarily denying what the people of consensus understood from the Qur'an (2).
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection (taḥrīja) and its response. The objector (likely a Mu'tazilite like al-Ka'bi) argues that the terms 'hearing' and 'sight' in the Qur'an should be interpreted metaphorically as referring to God's attribute of knowledge, not as real hearing and sight. The response is straightforward: the words of revelation (alfāẓ al-shar') are only taken away from their literal, commonly understood meanings when affirming the literal meaning is impossible (mustaḥīl). Since there is no impossibility in God being Hearing and Seeing—indeed, it is necessary (wājib) that He be such—there is no justification for arbitrarily denying the literal meaning. The author appeals to the consensus (ijmā') of the early community (the salaf) that these attributes are real. The response is concise: the objector must provide a proof of impossibility before abandoning the literal sense.

Key Terms
تحريجة
العلم
ألفاظ الشرع
موضوعاتها
الإجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That hearing and sight must be metaphorical because they imply physical organs in God.
Correction: Ghazali argues that metaphorical interpretation is only justified when the literal meaning is impossible. Since there is no impossibility in God being Hearing and Seeing, the literal meaning must be affirmed. The burden of proof is on the one who denies the literal sense.

Checkpoint
According to the author, when can words of revelation be taken away from their literal meaning?
A) When the literal meaning leads to polytheism.
B) When the literal meaning is considered weak by some scholars.
C) When affirming the literal meaning is impossible.
D) When the context suggests a metaphorical interpretation.

Key Takeaway
The literal meanings of divine attributes such as hearing and sight are affirmed unless there is a rational impossibility; otherwise, arbitrary metaphorical interpretation is rejected based on consensus and sound methodology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter an attribute of God in the Qur'an that challenges your intuition, do you pause to ask whether there is a genuine impossibility in the literal meaning, or do you immediately resort to metaphorical interpretation to make it easier for yourself? How will you change your approach after this lesson?
?
The author insists that the early community (salaf) understood these attributes literally. Have you ever dismissed the salaf's understanding as outdated or unsophisticated? What would it cost you to trust their methodology even when it feels uncomfortable?
?
If you were asked to defend God's hearing and sight as real attributes to someone who finds them anthropomorphic, would you be able to articulate the methodological principle from this passage? Commit today to memorizing the response: 'No impossibility, so no diversion.' How will you internalise this principle?

Divine Attributes
intermediate
4 min
Defending God's Eternal Hearing and Sight
Objection about eternal hearing/sight and response via analogy with knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Ghazali defends God's eternal hearing and sight against the objection that they cannot relate to nonexistent objects, by drawing an analogy with God's knowledge.

Core Idea
God's hearing and sight, like His knowledge, relate to nonexistents without change.

The objection claims that if God's hearing and sight are eternal, they cannot relate to nonexistent sounds or sights. Imam al-Ghazali identifies the source of this objection as either a Mu'tazilite or a philosopher. In this passage, he provides a clear response to the Mu'tazilite by drawing an analogy with God's attribute of knowledge, which relates to past, present, and future without change.

Guided Path
1
Read the double objection
Carefully read the objection: (1) if hearing/sight are created, God becomes locus of accidents; (2) if eternal, how can they relate to nonexistents?
Notice the dilemma: either created or eternal, both seem problematic.
2
Identify the source of the objection
Al-Ghazali says this objection comes from a Mu'tazilite or a philosopher. The response differs for each.
The Mu'tazilite already accepts God's knowledge of particulars.
3
Understand the analogy with knowledge
For the Mu'tazilite, al-Ghazali argues: God's knowledge relates to past, present, future without change. Similarly, hearing and sight can relate to nonexistents without change.
This response uses the same principle: an attribute can relate to multiple objects without changing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تحريجة : إنما امتنعتا فإن قيل : وجه استحالته أنه إن كان سمعه وبصره حادثين .. صار محلاً للحوادث ، وهو محال ، وإن كانا قديمين .. فكيف يسمع صوتاً معدوماً ؟
لكيلا يصير محلاً
للحوادث، ثم كيف
رأى العالم في الأزل وكيف يرى العالم في الأزل والعالم معدوم ، والمعدوم لا يرى ؟ وهو معدوم ؟ قلنا : هذا السؤال يصدر عن معتزلي أو فلسفي :
أما المعتزلي : فدفعُهُ هَيِّنٌ ؛ فإنه سلَّم أنه تعالى يعلمُ الحادثات ،
تعلق العلم بالمعدوم فنقول : يعلمُ الله تعالى الآن أنَّ العالم كان موجوداً قبل هذا ، فكيف علم كتعلق الإرادة به في الأزل أنه كان موجوداً وهو بعد لم يكن موجوداً ؟! فإن جاز إثبات صفةٍ تكون عند وجودِ العالم علماً بأنه كائن ، وقبله بأنه سيكون ، وبعده بأنَّه
كان ، وهو لا يتغيَّرُ ، عُبر ، عن هذه الصفة بالعلم والعالمية . . جاز ذلك في السمع والسمعية ، والبصر والبصرية
(۳).
وإن صدر عن فلسفي : فهو منكر لكونه عالماً بالحادثات المعينة الداخلة في الماضي والحال والمستقبل ، فسبيلنا أن ننقل الكلام إلى العلم ، ونثبت
A point of objection: They are only impossible, for if it is said: the aspect of their impossibility is that if His hearing and sight were originated (ḥādithān), then He would become a locus for originated things, which is impossible; and if they were eternal (qadīmān), then how can He hear a nonexistent sound? Lest He become a locus for originated things; then how did He see the world in pre-eternity when the world was nonexistent, and the nonexistent cannot be seen? And it is nonexistent? We say: This question comes from a Mu'tazilite or a philosopher. As for the Mu'tazilite, repelling it is easy; for he acknowledges that God, exalted, knows originated things. [Regarding] the relation of knowledge to the nonexistent, we say: God, exalted, knows now that the world existed before this; so how does He know—like the relation of will to it in pre-eternity—that it existed when it was not yet existing? For if it is permissible to affirm an attribute that, at the existence of the world, is knowledge that it is existent, and before it that it will be, and after it that it was, while He does not change, and this attribute is expressed as knowledge and knowing-ness, then it is permissible for hearing and hearing-ness, and sight and sight-ness (3). And if it comes from a philosopher, then he denies that He knows particular originated things included in the past, present, and future; so our way is to transfer the discussion to knowledge and prove...
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection and its detailed response. The objector raises two related arguments: (1) if God's hearing and sight were originated, He would be a locus of originated things (which is impossible for God); (2) if they are eternal, then how can they relate to nonexistent objects (sounds that do not yet exist, a world that did not yet exist)? The author identifies the source of this objection as either a Mu'tazilite or a philosopher. For the Mu'tazilite, the answer is easy because the Mu'tazilite already accepts that God knows originated things (al-ḥādithāt). The author then draws an analogy with God's attribute of knowledge: God knows the world before it exists (as future), while it exists (as present), and after it ceases (as past), yet His knowledge does not change. Similarly, if knowledge can relate to nonexistent objects without change, then hearing and sight can as well, without God changing or becoming a locus of accidents. The response to the philosopher is truncated at the page break; the author says that since the philosopher denies that God knows particular events, we must first establish God's knowledge of particulars, and then analogize. This response uses the same principle: the modality of the attribute is not limited by physical categories.

محلاً للحوادث
تعلق العلم بالمعدوم
المعتزلي
الفلسفي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's hearing and sight require a physical change or relation to physical objects.
Correction: Like God's knowledge, His hearing and sight relate to nonexistents without implying change in His essence.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the objection that eternal hearing cannot relate to a nonexistent sound?
A) He denies that God hears sounds.
B) He says that sound is always existent in God's knowledge.
C) He draws an analogy with God's knowledge, which relates to nonexistents without change.
D) He says that hearing and sight are metaphorical for knowledge.

Key Takeaway
God's hearing and sight, like His knowledge, relate to nonexistents without implying change in God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that God's attributes like hearing and sight relate to nonexistents without change. Consider a sin you have committed that no one knows about, but you know that God's hearing and sight encompass it even when it did not yet exist in time. How does this reality affect your daily choices and your repentance? Will you still treat that sin as forgotten, knowing that God's eternal hearing and sight are never forgetful?
?
The analogy shows that God's knowledge of the past, present, and future is unchanging. Think of a moment of deep regret or anxiety about the future. If you truly internalize that God's sight and hearing are eternal and relate to all times without change, how would your perspective on that regret or anxiety shift? What specific action will you take today to align your heart with this truth?
?
The passage responds to an objection that arises from thinking of God's attributes as limited like human attributes. Humbly, examine your own heart: do you sometimes imagine God's hearing and sight as similar to human faculties, expecting them to 'miss' something? This lesson corrects that. When you pray, do you pray with the certainty that God hears your words before you utter them, and sees your state even before it manifests? If not, what prevents you from living with that certainty?
